_shall tear be shed because the blossom fall,

 because the cloudy cherry slip away,

 and leave its branch in a leafy thrall

 till ruddy fruit do hang upon the spray?_



_shall tear be shed because the youthful bloom

 and all th'excess of early life must fade

 for large wealth of joy in small room

 to dwell contain in love of man and maid?_



_Nay, rather leap, o heart, to see fulfil

 in certain joy th'uncertain promise glee,

 to have so many mountain torrent spill

 for one fair river move to the sea._

EAST







I



LONDON where the king live



the boat-train have disgorge its passenger, who have huddle together

in a crowd round the luggage as it be drag out of the van, and

then have jostle their way out into the London they have be so long

approach. when the crowd scatter it leave like a deposit a little

knot of strange-look people in brilliant clothe who stare about

they pathetically and helplessly. there be three old man who seem

to be stranger to each other and a handsome Jewess with her

family--two girl and three boy. the two elder boy carry on their

back the family bed, and the young clung to his mother's

skirt and be frighten by the noise, the hurry crowd of people,

the vastness and the ugly, complicated angular line of the station.

the woman look disappointed and hurt. her eye search through the

crowd, through every fresh stream of people. she be baffle and

anxious. once or twice she be accost, but she could not understand

a word of what be say to she. at last she produce a piece of paper

and show it to a railway official, who come up think it be time

these outlandish folk move on. he could not read what be write on

it, for the paper be very dirty and the character be crab and

awkwardly write. he turn to the old man, one of whom say

excitedly the only english word he knew--"London--Jewish--Society."

the official look relieve. these people do not look like Jews, and

the eld girl and the little boy be lovely. he go away, and the

woman, whose hope have rise, once more look disconsolate. the

little boy bury his face in her apron and wept.



a suburban train came wheeze into the platform, which be at once

alive with hurry man in silk hat and tail-coat. catch sight of

the brilliantly attire group, the handsome woman and the lovely girl,

the boy with their head bow beneath the billow pile of feather

bedding, some of they stop. the little boy look up with tear in

his eye. one man put his hand in his pocket and throw down a few

copper. other follow his example, and the little boy run after the

shower penny as they bounce in the air, and roll, span, and

settle. he dance from penny to penny and a crowd gather; for, in

his bright jerkin and breech and little top-boot, dance like a

sprite, gay and wild, he be an astonishing figure to find in the

grime and ugliness of the station. silver be throw among the penny

to keep he dancing, but at last he be exhausted and run to his

mother with his fist full of money, and the man hurry on to their

office.



the official return with an interpreter, who discover that the

woman's name be Kühler, that she have expect to be meet by her

husband, that she have come from Austrian Poland, and that the address

write on the piece of paper be Gun Street. the number be

indecipherable.



the three old man be give instruction and they go away. the

interpreter take charge of the family and lead they to a refuge, where

he leave they, say that he would go and find Mr. Kühler. with a roof

over her head and food provide for her child, Mrs. Kühler sit

stoically to wait for the husband she have not see for two year. she

have no preconceived idea of London, and this bleak, bare room be

London to she, quite acceptable. the stress and the anxiety of the

detestable journey be over. this be peace and good. her husband

would find she. he have come to make a home in London. he have send for

she. he would come.



hour pass. they sleep, eat, talk, walk about the room, and

still Mr. Kühler do not come. the peace of the refuge be invade

with memory of the journey, the rattle, rattle, rattle of the

train-wheel, the brusque official who treat the poor traveller

like parcel, the soldier at the frontier, the wet, bare quay in

Holland, the first sight of the sea, immense, ominous, heaving,

heave up to the sky; the stink ship that heave like the sea and

make the brain oscillate like milk in a pan; the solidity of the

english quay, wet and bare, and of the English train, astonishingly

comfortable. . . . and still Mr. Kühler do not come.



the girl be cold and miserable. the boy wrestle and practise

feat of strength with each other to keep warm, and look to their

mother for applause. she give it they mechanically as she sit by the

little boy, whom she have lay to sleep on the bedding. he would be

hungry, she think, when he wake up, and she must get he food. there

be the money which have be throw to he, but she do not know its

value. People do not throw much money away. at home people do not

throw even small money away. there such a thing could not happen.

there money, like everything else, avoid the poor. but this be rich

England, where it rain money.



when the boy wake up she would go out and buy he something good to

eat, and if Mr. Kühler do not come to-morrow she would find some work

and a room, or a corner of a room, to live in. perhaps Jacob have go

to America again. he have be there twice, and both time suddenly.

People always go to America suddenly. he go out and buy a clean

collar, and say he be go and would send money for she as soon as

he have enough. . . . poor Jacob, he could not endure their poverty and

he would not steal, but he would always fight the soldier and the

bailiff when they come to take the bedding. . . . the sea heave, and

it rain money. the two boy begin to fight, a sudden fury in both of

they. their sister rush to part they and Mrs. Kühler rise.



at the end of the long room she see Jacob peer from group to group.

he look white and ill, as he have do when he come again and again

to implore she to marry he, and she feel half afraid of he, as she

have do when the violent fury of love in he have break down her

resistance and drag she from her comfortable home to the bare life

he have to offer she. he come to she now with the same ungraciousness

that have mark his wooing, explain to she that he have just get a

job and could not get away to meet she, and turn from she to the

child. the boy be grow big and strong, and the eld girl be

a beauty. he be satisfied, stoop and pick up little Mendel in his

strong arm. the child wake up, give a little grunt of pleasure as he

recognize the familiar smell of his father, and go to sleep again.



"he's heavy," say Mrs. Kühler. "you cannot carry he all the way."



"his face be like a flower," say Jacob.



he go first, carry the boy, and his family follow he into the

roar street. the lamp be light and the shop be dazzle. there

be barrow of fruit, fish, old iron, book, cheap jewellery, all light

up with naphtha flare. the child be half frightened, half

delighted. the smell and the noise of the street excite they. every

now and then they hear snatch of their own language and be

comfort. they come to shop bear yiddish character and London no

long seem to they forbiddingly foreign, though they begin to feel

conscious of their clothe, which make they conspicuous. the boy

curse and growl under the bed and begin to complain that they

have so far to go. Mr. Kühler find the child too heavy and have to put

he down. Mendel take his mother's hand and trot along by her side.



they turn into a darkish street which run for some length between

very tall house. it be obscure enough to allow the clear sky to be

see, patch with cloud and deep blue, starry space. at the end of

it be a building cover with light and illuminated sign. they

shine golden and splendid. never have Mendel see anything so glorious,

so rich, so dream-like, so clearly correspond to that marvellous

region where all his thought end, pass out of his reach, and take

on a brilliant and mighty life of their own, a glory great than that

of the Emperor at home. but this be England and have only a king.



"do the King live there?" he ask his mother.



"No; that be a shop."



"have father get a shop like that?"



"not yet."



"will he soon have a shop like that?"



"very soon."



Mendel would have like to have stand and gaze at the glorious,

glitter shop. he feel sure the King must buy his boot there, and

he think that if he stay long enough he would see the King drive

up in his crystal coach, with his crown on his head, and go into the

shop. but his father lead the way out of the darkish street into

another that be still darker, very narrow, and flank with little

low house. one of these they enter, and in a small, almost

unfurnished room they have supper, and Mendel go to sleep hear his

father say to his mother, "thirteen shilling." just before that his

father have hold his hand out under the candle, and they be raw and

bleed.



* * * * *



one room be luxury to they. at home in Austria they have have a corner

of a room, and the three other corner be occupy by the carpenter,

the stableman, and the potter. in the centre of the room stand the

common water-bucket and the common refuse-tub. London have shower

money on they and provide they with a whole room. they feel hopeful.



Mr. Kühler make thirteen shilling a week polish walking-stick,

and when that trade be bad he could sometimes get work as a furrier.

he have intend to take his family over to America, but find work

in London, he think it well to stay there. besides, he have a

grudge against America, for while there he have invent a device for

twisting tail of fur, but his invention have be steal from he and

he have miss his chance of make a fortune. America be evil and

living be very dear. London be the more comfortable place for the

struggle. and in London he have find Abramovich, the friend of his

boyhood, the one creature in the world upon whom he rely. he have no

reason for his faith. Abramovich have never do he any good, but he

be not of those who pass. he might disappear for year, but he always

come back again, and time make no difference. he be always the same.

if help be need he give it, and if he need help he ask for it.

Abramovich be a very strong reason for stay in London. . . . the

boy would soon be work and the eld girl be a beauty. the

match-maker would be busy with she. another two year, and the

match-maker would find she a rich man who would help they all and put

money into a business. that be Jacob's desire, to have a business of

his own, for he loathe work for another man. he could not do it

for long. always he end with a quarrel, perhaps with blow, or he

simply walk out and would not return.



he be a devout Jew and despise Christians, as he despise luxury,

pleasure, comfort, not actively nor with any hatred. he simply do not

need they. he have live without they, and he ask nothing of life. he

be alive; that be enough. passion seize he and he follow they.

without passion he never move, never stir a finger except to keep

himself alive. passion have choose his wife for he. Golda, the

beautiful, be his wife. in she he be bind more firmly to his race

and his faith, and there be no need to look beyond. he be root.

she have bear he child, but he have no more ambition for they than

for himself. Leah, the beauty, should wed a rich man, not for

ambitious reason, but because, in life, beauty and rich be proper

mate. there be a certain orderliness about life, and certain thing

can only be prevent by an irruption of passion. if that happen,

then life take its revenge and become hard and bleak, but it be

still life, and only a fool will complain. Jacob never complain, and

he take his Golda's reproach in silence, unless she become unjust,

and then he silence she brutally and callously. she bear with he,

because she prize his honesty, his steadfast simplicity, and because

she know that his passion have never waken a profound answer in

herself. she have very slowly be rouse to love, which have flower

in she with the birth of her young child, in whom she have learn a

power of acceptance almost equal to her husband's. like he, she clung

to her race and her faith and never look beyond.



in London she find that she be leave alone and her life be no long

hem in by a menacing world of soldier and police and peasant, who

swear the Jews cheat they and spread terrifying tale of jewish

practice upon christian child. Christian London be indifferent to

the Jews, and she could be indifferent to Christian London. she have no

curiosity about it and never go above a mile from her house. she

make no attempt to learn English, but could not help glean a few

word from her child as they pick it up at school. the synagogue

be the centre of her life, and from it come all the life she care to

have outside her family. she be absorb in little Mendel, by whom

her world be colour. if he be happy, that be sunshine to she. if

he be oppress and tearful, her sky be overcast. if he be ill, it

seem to she a menace of the end.



he be a strange child and very slow in grow into a boy. the other

child have seem to shoot into independence almost as soon as they

could walk. but Mendel clung to she, would not learn to feed himself,

and would not go to sleep at night unless she sing to he and rock

he in the cradle, in which he sleep even after he go to school. as

long as he could curl up in it he sleep in his cradle, and he make she

learn as much as she could of an English song which have catch his

fancy. it be the only english song she ever know, and night after

night she have to sing it over and over again as she rock the heavy

cradle:--



    Daisy, Daisy, give I your answer, do;

    I'm half crazy, all for the love of you.



she have no idea what the word mean, but the boy love the tune and

her funny accent and intonation, and even when she be ill and tired

she would sing he to sleep, and then sit brood over he with her

finger just touch his curly hair. and in her complete absorption

in his odd, unchildlike childhood she be perfectly content, and

entirely indifferent to all that happen outside he. brutal thing,

terrible thing happen, but they never touch the child, and if she

could, she hide the knowledge of they from he.



Abramovich collect a little capital and persuade Mr. Kühler to join

he in a furrier's business. they be not altogether unsuccessful,

and Mr. Kühler take a whole house in Gun Street and buy a piano,

but soon their capital be exhausted and they have give more credit

than they be accord and the business trickle through their

finger. Mr. Kühler take to his bed, for he could sleep at will and

almost indefinitely, and so could avoid see poverty once more

creep up like a muddy sea round his wife and child. it have be

bad enough when that happen at home, where at the bad there be

his relation to help, and there be the potato field to be

despoiled, and, at least, the child could be happy playing in the

road or by the river, or on the side of the mountain. but here in

London poverty be black indeed, and there be no one but Abramovich

to help, and he be in almost as bad case as himself. yet

astonishingly Abramovich come again and again to the rescue. he be a

little squat, ugly man, the stunt product of some obscure russian

ghetto, and he seem to live by and for his enthusiasm for the Kühler

family. in their presence he glow, greedily drink in every word that

Jacob or Golda say, and be always loud in his praise of the

beautiful child. . . . "the sky be dark now," he use to say, "but

they will be rich, and they will give you horse and carriage, and

Turkey carpet, and footman, and flower in the winter, and they will

bring english gentleman to the house and what you want, that you shall

have. . . ." "I want nothing," Jacob would say. "I want nothing. I

will work and be my own master. I will not steal or help other man to

steal." "you wait," Abramovich would reply. "these child have only

to go out into London and all will be give to they."



only the eld girl listen to these conversation, and she use to

hold her head high, and her face would go pale as ferociously she

follow up the idea they suggest to she.



but Abramovich could bring no consolation. Jacob would not go back to

the stick-polishing, and at last he could bear it no long, go out

and buy a clean collar, clip his beard, and without a word of

farewell, go to America.







II



poverty



then follow, for Golda, the black year of her life. she remove

once more to one room in Gun Street, and she and the two boy earn

enough to keep body and soul together. she find work in other

people's house, help at party, and when nothing else be

available she go to a little restaurant to assist as scullery-maid,

and stay after closing-time to scrub the table and sweep the floor.

for this she be give crust of bread and scrap from the plate. she

never have a word from her husband, and she know she would not hear

unless he make money. if he fail again, as of course he would, he

would live in silence, solitary, proud, avoid his fellow-man, who

would have nothing to do with he except he make the surrender of

dignity which it be impossible for he to make. she would not hear

from he, and he would return one day unannounced, without a word, as

though he have come from the next street; and as likely as not he would

have give the coat off his back to some one poor than himself.

. . . Jacob be like that. he would give away on an impulse thing

that it have cost he week of save to acquire. low as he stand in

the world, he seem always to be look downward, as though he

could believe in what come up from the depth but not in anything that

go beyond he. Golda could not understand he, but she believe in

he absolutely. she know that he suffer even more than she, and she

have learn from he not to complain. the Jews have always suffer.

that be make clear in the synagogue, where, in wail over the

captivity in Babylon, Golda find a vent for her own sorrow. she

would weep over the suffering of her race as she weep for those who

be dead, her father and her mother, and her father's father and her

little brother, on the anniversary of their death. however poor she

might be she have money to buy candle for they, and whatever the cost

she keep the observance of her religion.



so she live isolated and proud, untouched by the excitement her

child find in the house of their friend and in the street.



very wild be the life in the neighbourhood of Gun Street. there be

constant feud between Jews and Christians, battle with fist and

stick and stone. Old Jews be insult and pelt by Christian

youth, and the young Jews would take up their cause. there be

violent dispute between landlord and tenant, husband and wife,

prostitute and their bully. any evening, walk along Gun Street,

you might hear scream and growl in one of the little house.

louder and loud it would grow. suddenly the male voice would be

silent, the female would rise to a shriek, the door would open, and

out into the street would be propel a half-naked woman. she would

wail and batter on the door, and, if that be of no avail, she would

go to the house of a friend and silence would come again. . . . or

sometimes a door would open and a man would be shoot out to lie limp

and flabby in the gutter.



Harry, the second boy, take to this wild life like a duck to water. he

practise with dumb-bell and learn the art of boxing, and so

excite Mendel with his feat of strength that he too practise

exercise and learn to stand on his hand, and cheerfully allow

his brother to knock he down over and over again in his ambition to

learn the element of defence and the use of the straight left. in

vain: his brain be not quick enough, or be too quick. his hand

would never obey he in time, but he dream of be a strong man,

the strong man in the world, who by sheer muscle should compel

universal admiration and assume authority.



in the family the child's superiority be acknowledge tacitly. he have

his way in everything. he want such strange thing, and be adamant

in his whim. if he be not allow to do as he wish, he lie on the

ground and roar until he be humour; or he would refuse to eat; or

he would go out of the house with the intention of lose himself. as

he be know all through the neighbourhood for his beauty that be

impossible. he be an object of pride to the neighbour, and whenever

he be find far from home, there be always some one who know he to

take he back. but Golda could not realize this, and she suffer

torture.



the boy love the street and the shop, the market with their

fruit-stall and fish-barrow, the brilliant colour in Petticoat

Lane. he would wander drink in with his eye colour and beauty,

shake with emotion at the sight of the pretty little girl with

their little round face, their ivory skin, and their brilliant black

eye. Ugliness hurt he not at all. it be the condition of thing,

the dark chaos out of which flash beauty. but cruelty could drive

he nearly mad, and he would tremble with rage and terror at the sight

of a woman with a bloody face or a man kick a horse.



he have a friend, a christian boy, name Artie Beech, who adore he

even as Abramovich adore his father. Golda be alarm by this

friendship, think no good could come out of the Christians, and she

try to forbid it, but the boy have his way, and he love Artie Beech

as a child love a doll or a king his favourite. together the two boy

use to creep home from school gaze into the shop window. one day

they see a brightly coloured advertisement of a beef extract: a

picture of a man rend a lion. "it will make you strong than a

lion," say Mendel. "yes," say Artie, "one drop on the tip of your

tongue." "I would be strong than Harry if I eat a whole bottle,"

reply Mendel, and they decide to save up to buy the strength-give

elixir. it take they seven week to save the price of it. then with

immense excitement they buy the treasure, take it home, and,

loathe the taste of it, gulp it down and toss a button for the

right to lick the cork. feel rather sick, they gaze at each other

with frightened eye, half expect to swell so that they would burst

their clothe. but nothing happen. Mendel take off his coat and feel

his bicep and swear that they have grow. Artie take off his coat:

yes, his bicep have grow too.



they go through the street with grow confidence, and at school

they be not afraid. Mendel's new arrogance lead he into the only

fight he ever have and he be lay low. ache with humiliation, he

shun Artie Beech and go alone to gaze at the picture of the man

rend the lion. it take he a week of hard concentrated thought to

realize that the picture and its legend be not to be take

literally, and his close study lead he to another and a strangely

emotional interest in the picture. his eye would travel up the line

of the man's body along his arm to the lion's jaw, and then down its

taut back to its paw clutch the ground. the two line spring

together, the two form lock, give an impression of strength, of

tremendous impact, which, as the boy gaze, become so violent as to

make his head ache. at the same time he begin to develop an appetite

for this shock, and unconsciously use his eye so as to obtain it. it

would sometimes spring up in he suddenly, without his know the

cause of it, when he watch his mother sit with her hand fold

on her stomach, or cook with her hand--her big, strong, working

hand--on a fish or a loaf of bread.



one day in Bishopsgate, that lordly and splendid thoroughfare which

lead from the dark street to the glitter world, he come on a man

kneeling on the pavement with coloured chalk. first of all the man

dust the stone with his cap, and then he lay another cap full of

little piece of chalk by his side, and then he draw and smudge and

smudge and draw until a slice of salmon appear. by the side of the

salmon he draw a glass of beer with a curl of froth on it and a little

bunch of flower. on another stone he draw a ship at sea in a storm, a

black and green sea, and a brown and black sky. Mendel watch he

enthral. what a life! what a career! to go out into the street and

make the dull stone lovely with colour! he see the man look up and

down and then lie a penny on the salmon. a fine gentleman pass by

and throw down another penny. . . . oh, certainly, a career! to make

the street lovely, and immediately to be reward!



from school Mendel steal some chalk and decorate the stone in the

yard at Gun Street. he draw a bottle and an onion and a fish, though

this he rather despise, because it be so easy. always he have amuse

himself with drawing. as a tiny child, the first time his father go

to America, he draw a picture of a watch to ask for that to be send

he, and this picture have be keep by his mother. and after that he

often draw, but chiefly because it make his father and mother proud of

he, and they laugh happily at everything he do. the pavement

artist fill he with pride and pleasure in the doing of it: and

every minute out of school and away from the Rabbi he devote to

draw. his brother buy he a box of colour, and he paint

imaginary landscape of river and swan and cow and castle. every

picture he make be treasure by his mother. they seem to she, as

they do to himself, perfectly beautiful. he use his water-colour as

though they be oil, and lay they on thick, to get as near the

pavement artist's colour as possible. at school there be

drawing-lesson, but they seem to have no relation to this keen

private pleasure of his.



in the evening he would lie on the ground in the kitchen and paint

until his eye and his head ache. sometimes his perpetual, silent

absorption would so exasperate his brother that they would kick his

paint away and make he get up and talk to they. then he would curse

they with all the rich curse of the yiddish language, and rush away

and hide himself; for day he would live in a state of gloom and dark

oppression, feel dimly aware of a difference between he and they

which it be beyond his power to explain. he would try to tell his

mother what be the matter with he, but she could not understand. his

happiness in painting, the keen delight that use to fill he, be to

her compensation enough for her anxiety and the stress and strain of

her poverty.



his little local fame procure she some relief. at school he win a

prize accord by vote for the most popular boy. this have amazed he,

for he have very little traffic with the other, and during playtime

use to stand with his back to the wall and his arm fold, stare

with unseee eye. when his sister ask one of the boy why Mendel

have win the vote, the answer she receive be: "he _can_ draw." as a

result his brother be help and his mother be able to get work as

a sempstress. they be relieve from the poverty that paralyse. they

could go from day to day and carry their deficit from week to week.

they could afford friend, and the visit of friend on a ceremonious

basis, and Abramovich be always try to interest rich man in the

wonderful family.



it be Abramovich who buy Mendel his first box of oil-paint, not

so much to give the boy pleasure as with the idea that he might learn

to paint portrait from photograph. that, however, be not in the

boy's idea. he abandon his imaginary landscape and begin to paint

object, still in the manner of the pavement artist, thrill with the

discovery that he could more and more exactly reproduce what he see.

he paint a loaf of bread and a cucumber so like the original that

Abramovich be wildly excited and rush off to bring Mr. Jacobson, a

Polish Jew, a timber-merchant and very rich, to see the marvel.



Mendel be unprepared. he sit paint in the kitchen with his mother

and Lotte, his young sister. Abramovich and Mr. Jacobson come in.

Jacobson be ruddy, red-haired, with a strange broad face and a flat

nose, almost negroid about the nostril. he wear a frock-coat, a white

waistcoat with a cable-chain across it, and ring upon his finger.

Mendel have a horror of he, and be overcome with shyness. Mr.

Jacobson put on his spectacle, stare at the picture. "ye-es," he

say. "that bread could be eat. that cucumber could be cut and put

into the soup. the boy be all right. eh? ye-es, and a beautiful boy,

too." Mendel writhe. Golda be almost as overcome with shyness as he.

in silence she produce all the boy's drawing and picture and lay

they before the visitor. Abramovich be loud in his praise, but not

too loud, for he know that Mr. Jacobson love to talk. and indeed it

seem that Mr. Jacobson would never stop. he stand in the middle of

the room and wag his fat, stumpy hand and hold forth:--



"in my country, Mrs. Kühler, there be once a poor boy. he be always

draw. give he a piece of paper and a pencil and he would draw

anything in the world. the teacher at school have to forbid he to

draw, for he would learn nothing at all. so one day the teacher could

not find that boy. and where do you think they find he? under the

table. the teacher pull he out and find in his hand a piece of

paper--a piece of paper. the teacher look down at the piece of paper

and faint away. the boy have draw a picture of the teacher so like

that he faint away. well, when the teacher come to himself, he say:

'boy, do you do that?' 'yes,' say the boy, 'I do that.' 'then, say

the teacher, 'I will tell you what you must do. you must paint a

portrait of the king and take it to the king, and he will give you

money, and carriage, and house, and ring, and watch, for you and

your father, and your uncle and all your family. ahin and aher. the

boy do that. he paint a portrait of the King and he take it to the

palace. he go to the front door and knock, knock, knock. a lady

open the door and she say: 'what do you want, little boy?' 'I want

to see the king. I have something to show he.' 'I be the Queen,' say

the lady. 'you can show it to I.' the boy show the picture and the

Queen faint away. the servant and the King come run in to see

what have happen, and they stand like stone. 'who do that?' say the

King. 'I do,' say the boy. 'I don't believe he,' say the King.

'shut he up for a day and a night, give he paint and brush, and we

will see what he can do.' well, they shut the boy up for a day and a

night, and in the morning the door be open and the King and the

Queen come in. the King take off his hat and put it on the table and

it fall to the ground. that boy have paint a picture of a table so

like that the King think it be a real table and try to put his

hat on it. it be true, and the boy paint the King's portrait every

Saturday until he die, and he have house and money and footman and

statue in his garden, and his father and mother drive in their

carriage and wear sable even in the summer. and some day, Mrs.

Kühler, we shall see you in your carriage and this boy paint the

portrait of the king."



the story be receive in silence. the emotion it arouse in Golda

and her son be so profound, so violent that they be daze. the

tension be relieve by a giggle from Lotte, who know that king do

not wear hat. Mendel sit stare at his picture, which, try as he

would, he could not connect with the story.



Abramovich say: "I tell you so, Mrs. Kühler. I tell you something

would come of it." already he be convince that Mendel only have to go

out into London to make the family's fortune.



but Golda reply: "there's time enough for that, and don't go put

idea into the boy's head."



there be no danger of that. Mendel's be not the kind of head into

which idea be easily put. he be slow of comprehension, powerful in

his instinct, and everything he perceive have to be refer to they.

School be to he a perfectly extraneous experience. what he learn

there be of so little use to any purpose of which he be conscious,

and it could not be share with his mother. to her schooling be the

law of the land. a strange force take her boy from she every day and,

as it be, imprison he. when he be fourteen he would be free. she

must endure his captivity as she have learn to endure so much else.



when Mr. Jacobson have go she say: "there have be boy like that,

and a good boy never forget his father and mother."



Mendel look puzzled and say: "when _i_ draw a picture of teacher he

cane I."



"cane you?" cry Golda, horrify.



"he often do."



"thrash you!" cry Golda; "on the hand?"



"no," reply Mendel, "on the seat and the back."



Golda make he undress, and she give a gasp of anger when she see the

weal and bruise on his back. "but what do you do?" she cry.



"I don't know," answer Mendel. this be true. at school he would

suddenly find the teacher tower over he in a fury; he would be

tell to stay behind, and then he would be flog. he have suffer

more from the humiliation than from the pain inflict. he could never

understand why this fury should descend upon he out of his happy

dream. and now as his mother weep over the mark upon his body the

suffering in he be release. all the feeling suppress in he by

his inability to understand come tear out of he and he shake with

rage. he could find no word to express these new emotion, which be

terrible and frighten he.



Lotte come up and feel the weal on his back with her finger, and she

say: "they don't do that to girl."



"be quiet, Lotte," say Golda. "don't touch he. you will hurt he."

and she stand stare in amazement at the boy's back. "that's an awful

mess," she say to herself, and her thought fly back to man who have

be flog by the soldier in Austria. but this be England, where

everybody be leave alone. she could not understand it. she do not

know what to do. the boy could not be keep from school, for they would

come and drag he to it. there be often dreadful scene in Gun

Street when child be drag off to school. she make Lotte sit at

the table and write: "please, teacher, you must not beat my son. his

back be like a railway-line, and it be not good to beat child." she

could think of no threat which could intimidate the teacher, no power

she could invoke to her aid. her powerlessness appal she. she

sign the letter and think she would go to the Rabbi and ask he

what she must do. "yes," she say, "the Rabbi will tell I, and

perhaps the Rabbi will write to the teacher also." she could feel the

torture in the boy, and she know that it must be stop. it be all

very well to knock Harry or Issy about. they could put up with any

amount of violence. but Mendel be different. with he pain go so

deep. that be what make it horrible. he be like a very little child.

it be wicked to hurt he. his silence now be almost more than she

could bear.



there come a knock at the door. Lotte go to open it and give a

little scream. it be her father come back from America. he come into

the room, not different by a hair from when he go away; thin,

perhaps, a little more haggard and hollow under the eye, so that the

slight squint in his right eye, injure to avoid conscription, be

more pronounced. he come in as though he have return from his day's

work, nod to his wife, and look at the boy's back.



"who have do that?" he ask.



"at school," reply Golda. "the teacher."



Jacob take off his coat, roll up his sleeve, pick up a chair and

smash it on the floor. Mendel put on his shirt and coat again and

say: "it be like when you knock the soldier over with the glass."



Jacob give a roar: "ah, you remember that? ah! yes. that be when I

have the inn near the barrack. he be an officer. two of they come in.

they be drunk, the swine! the man make for your mother and the

officer for your sister. the glass be big, with a heavy base. I

take one of they . . ."



"and the man spin round three time and fall flat on the floor," say

Mendel.



"ah! you remember that? yes. and I lift he out into the street and

leave he there in the snow. I be a strong man then. I want nothing

from they, but if they touch what be mine . . . !" he seize Mendel

and lift he high over his head. he be tremendously excited and

could not be get to sit down or to talk of his doing in America or of

his voyage. that be his way. he would talk in his own time. his

doing would come out piecemeal, over year and year. now he be

entirely absorb with his fury. he be nearly ill with it and could

not eat. up and down the room he walk, lash up his rage. Mendel

be send to bed, and until he go to sleep he could hear his father

pace up and down and his mother talk, explain, entreating.



next morning Mendel have almost forget the excitement and go to

school as usual. in the middle of an arithmetic lesson in walk

Jacob, very white, with his head down. he go quickly up to the

teacher and speak to he quietly. the class be stun into silence.

Jacob raise his fist and the teacher go down. Jacob pick he up,

shake he, and throw he into a corner. then he shout: "you willn't

touch my boy again!" shake himself like a dog, and walk out, close

the door very quietly. the teacher hurry out and do not return. the

class slowly recover from its astonishment, shrill voice grow out

of the silence like a strong wind, and book and inkpot begin to fly.

soon the wall be streak and spatter with ink and when it became

know that it be Kühler's father who have do it, Mendel find

himself a hero. but he take no pride in it. he be haunt by the

teacher's white, terrified face. he have always think of the teacher

as a nice man. the thrashing inflict on he have always seem to

he impersonal and outside humanity altogether. yet because it be his

father who have thrash the teacher he accept it as right. at home

his father, even in his absence, be the law, and could do no wrong.

the violent scene seem to Mendel to have nothing to do with himself,

and he resent having become the centre of attention.



the head master hurry in, quell the class, go on with the lesson

where it have be interrupt. Mendel could not attend. he be

bewilder by a sudden realization of life outside himself. it be no

long a procession of event, figure, scene, colour, shape, light

and darkness pass before his eye, always charming, sometimes

terrifying, but something violent which meet another something in

himself with a fearful impact. it could hurt he, and he know that it

be merciless, for the thing in himself that answer to it and rush

out to meet it be wild and know no mercy either. he have hear of a

thing call the maelstrom in the sea, a kind of spout, with whirling

side, down which great ship be suck. and he feel that he be

be suck down such a spout, in which he could see all that he have

ever know, the mountain and the river in Austria, the train, the

telegraph wire, town, bus, face, the street, the school, Artie

Beech, Abramovich, his father. . . . only his mother stand firm, and

from she come a force to counteract that other force which be

drag he towards the whirlpool.



he become conscious of the discomfort in which he live and be

acutely aware of the people by whom he be surround.







III



prison



this time in America Jacob have fare well, and by dint of

half-starve himself and sleep when he have nothing to do, he have

manage to save over fifty pound. Abramovich borrow another fifty,

and once again they set up in business as furrier. they take one of

the old georgian house off Bishopsgate, start a workshop in the top

room, and in the low room the Kühler family live, with Abramovich

in lodging round the corner. they be only twenty yard from the

synagogue and Golda be happy; Jacob too, for in such a house he feel

a solid man. and, indeed, amid the extreme poverty with which they

be surround he could pass for wealthy. he have his name on a brass

plate on the door and be always proud when he write it on a cheque.

he take his eld son into his workshop to rescue he from the fate

of work for another master, and he assume a patriarchal authority

over his family. his son be never allow out after half-past nine,

and, tall youth though they be, if they cross his will he

thrash they. the girl be forbid to go out alone. they be

keep at home to await their fate.



the eld boy fling all his ardour into dancing, and be the champion

slow waltzer of the neighbourhood. with egg-shell on his heel to

show that he never bring they to the ground, he could keep it up for

hour and win many prize. Harry scorn this polite prowess. for he

the romance of the street be irresistible: easy amorous conquest,

battle of tongue and fist, visit to the prize-ring, upon which his

young ambition centre. a bout between a Jew and a Christian would

lead to a free-fight in the audience, for the Jews yell in Yiddish

to their champion, and the British would suspect insult to they or

vile instruction, and would try to enforce silence . . . and Harry

would bring gruff young man to the house, youth with puffy eye and

swollen or crooked or broken nose, and he would treat they with an

enthusiastic deference which find no echo in any member of the family

save Mendel, who find the world open up to he by Harry large and

adventurous, like the open sea stretch away and away from the

whirlpool.



there be one extraordinarily nice man whom Harry bring to the

house. his name be Kuit, and he have fail as a boxer and have become

a thief, a trade in which he be an expert. his talk fascinate

Mendel, and indeed the whole family. none could fail to listen when he

tell of his adventure and his skill. he have begin as a pickpocket,

ply his trade in Bishopsgate or the Mile End Road, and to show his

expertise he would run his hand over Jacob's pocket without his

feel it, and tell he what they contain. or he would ask Golda to

let he see her purse, and she would grope for it only to find that he

have already take it. he have advance from pick pocket to the

high form of theft: plunder hotel or dog diamond merchant,

and he be keenly interested in America. it be through he that the

family know the little that be ever reveal to they of Jacob's

doing there.



Kuit say he would go to America and not return until he have ten

thousand pound, all make by honest theft, for he would only rob the

rich, and, indeed, he be most generous with his earning, and give

Golda many handsome piece of jewellery, and he lend Jacob money when

he badly need it. that, however, be not Jacob's reason for

admit Kuit to his family circle. he like the man, be fascinate

by he, and think his moral be his own affair. he know his race

and the poor too well to be squeamish, and never dream of extend

his authority beyond his family. he warn Harry that if he take to

Kuit's practice he would no long be a son of his, and as the

account of prison give to Harry by some of his acquaintance be

not cheer, Harry prefer not to run any risk. instead, he

devote himself to training for the glory of the prize-ring.



for Mendel the moral aspect of Kuit's profession have be settle once

and for all by his see the Rabbi with his face turn to the wall,

in the middle of the most terrible of prayer, filch some penny from

an overcoat. religion therefore be one thing, life be another, and

life include theft. Kuit be the only man who could think of paint

apart from money, and it be Kuit who give he a new box of oil

colour, steal from a studio which he break into on purpose, and _en

passant_ from one rich house in Kensington to another. Kuit use to

say: "one thing be true for one man and another for another. and what

be true for a man be what he do well. for Harry it be box, for

Issy it be woman and dancing, and for Mr. Kühler it be be honest.

for I it be show the business thief that they cannot have thing

all their own way, and outwit the police. oh yes! they know I and

I know they, but they will never catch I."



so charming be Mr. Kuit that Jacob could not object to take care

from time to time of the property that pass through his hand, and

the kitchen be often splendid with marble clock and oriental china

and Sheffield plate, which never look anything but out of place

among the cheap oleograph and the sideboard with its green paper

frill round the flash gilt china that be never use. the kitchen

be the living-room of the house, for Jacob only eat when he be

hungry, and it be rarely that two sit down to a meal together.



as often as not Mendel have his paint on the table, and the object he

be paint be not to be move. he cling to his painting as the

only comfort in his distress, and he would frequently work away with

his brush though he could hardly see what he be at, and know that

he be entirely devoid of the feeling that until the discomfort break

out in his soul have never fail he. he dared not look outside his

circumstance for comfort, and within they be the most absolute

denial of that cherish feeling for loveliness and colour. beyond

certain street he never venture. he feel lose outside the immediate

neighbourhood of his home, and only Mr. Kuit reassure he with the

confidence with which he speak of such remote region as Kensington

and Bayswater and Mayfair. the rest clung to the little district where

the shop and the language and the smell be jewish. yet there, too,

Mendel feel lost, though he have an immense reverence for the old Jews,

for the Rabbis who pore all day long over their book, and the

ancient bearded man who, like his mother, could sit for hour together

do nothing at all. he love their tragic, wrinkle face and their

steadfast peace, so stark a contrast to the chatter and the wrangling

and the harshness that fill his home.



there be constant row. Harry upset the household for week after

his father forbid he to pursue his prize-fight ambition. Jacob

would not have a son of his make a public show of himself. to that

disturbance be add another when Issy begin to court, or be court

by, a girl who be think too poor and base-bear. if he be out a

minute later than half-past nine Jacob would go out and find he at

the corner of the street with the girl in his arm. issy would be

drag away. then he would sulk or shout that he be a man, and Jacob

would tell he in a cold, furious voice that he could go if he like,

but, if he go, he must never show his face there again. for a time

Issy would submit. poor though the home be, he could not think of

leave it except to make another for himself. but there be no

keep the girl away, and he would be for ever peep into the

street to see if she be there, and if she be he could not keep

away from she.



Leah, the eld girl, have her courtship too. the match-maker be

busy with she, and a number of man, young and old, be bring to

view she. she be dress up to look fine, and Jacob and Golda would

sit together to inspect the suitor, and at last they choose a huge,

ugly russian Jew, name Moscowitsch--Abraham Moscowitsch, a

timber-merchant, who have pull himself up out of the East end and have

a house at Hackney. he be a friend of Kuit's and be willing to take

the girl without a dowry. Leah hide herself away and weep. it be in

vain that Golda, prime by Jacob, tell she that she would be rich, and

would have servant and carriage, and could buy at the great shop:

she could not forget the Russian's bristling hair and thick lip and

coarse, splayed nostril. the tear be of no avail; the marriage have

be offer and accept. the wedding be fix, and nothing be

spare to make it a social triumph. the bride be deck out in

conventional english white, with a heavy veil and a bouquet: and very

lovely she look. Jacob wear his first frock-coat and a white linen

collar, Golda have a dress make of mauve cashmere, with a bodice

heavily adorn with shine bead, and Mendel have a new sailor suit

with a mortar-board cap. there be three carriage to drive the party

the twenty yard to the synagogue. the wedding group be photograph,

and a hall be take for the feast and the dance in the evening. the

wedding cost Jacob the saving of many year and more, but he grudge

not a penny of it, because he have a rich son-in-law and wish it to

be know. there be over fifty guest at the feast.



within a week Leah come home again, pale, thin, and shrink.

Moscowitsch have be arrest. he have go bankrupt and have do

"something with his book."



"bankrupt!" say Jacob; "bankrupt!"



he stand in front of his weeping daughter and beat against the air

with his clenched fist. she moan and protest that she would never

go back to he. Jacob shake she till her tooth chatter together.



"you dare talk like that! he be your husband. you be his wife. it be

a misfortune. you should be with the lawyer to find out when you can

see he. I be to lose everything because he be unfortunate! a dog will

not turn from a man in his misery, and must a woman learn from a dog?

you be a soft girl! go, I say, and find out when you can see he. be

ever a man so cross by Fate! where I go, there luck take wing."



his violence shake Leah out of the daze misery in which she have come

home, have no other idea, no other place to which to go. Jacob be

at first for make his daughter wait in her new home until she

husband be return to she. his simple imagination seize on the idea

and visualize it. it seem to he admirable, and Golda have hard work

to shake it out of his head. as a piece of unnecessary cruelty he

could not realize it, but when it be bring home to he that he

would have to pay the rent of the house in Hackney, he yield and

allow the girl to stay at home.



Moscowitsch be sentence to six month' imprisonment, and a gloom,

such as not the dark day of poverty have be able to create,

descend upon the house. Jacob be ashamed and irritable. he insist

upon the most scrupulous observance of all the rite of his religion,

and he forbid Mendel to paint. painting have nothing to do with

religion and he would have none of it. he trample on Mendel's

friendship with Artie Beech. the christian world of police and judge

and the law have destroy his happiness, and not the faint smell of

Christendom should cross his door. friction between the father and his

two son be exasperate, and it seem to Mendel that Hell be let

loose. he be nearly of an age to leave school, and he dream by the

hour of the freedom he would have when he go to work. he would go

out early in the morning and come home late in the evening. he would

stay in the street and look at the shop and watch the girl go by.

he would go one day out beyond London to see what the world be like

there. he would find a place where there be picture, and he would

feast on they: for when he go to work he would paint no more, since

painting would be shed with the miserable childhood that be so fast

slip away from he.



yet a bad calamity be to happen. once again the christian world of

police, law, and judge be to invade the home of the Kühlers, and

this time it be Jacob himself who be take. he be charge with

receive steal good. a detective-inspector and two constable

invade the house and take possession of an ormolu clock, a number of

silver knife, and a brooch which Mr. Kuit have give to Golda. five of

Mr. Kuit's friend have be arrest, but Mr. Kuit himself be not

implicate. he pay for the defence of the prisoner and take charge

of the Kühler family, transfer the business into Issy's name, and

advanced money to keep it go. he spare neither time nor trouble to

try to establish Jacob's innocence, but it look almost as though

some one else be take an equal amount of trouble to prove his

guilt, for every move of Mr. Kuit's be counter, and Jacob himself

be so bewildered and enrage that he could not give a coherent answer

to the question put to he. he babble and rave of an enemy who have

do this thing, of a rival who have plot his ruin, but as he could

not give a satisfactory account of the article find in his

possession, his passionate protestation and his fanatical belief in

his own honesty be of no avail. from the dock in which he be place

with Mr. Kuit's other friend he deliver a vehement harangue in

broken English, not more than ten word of which be intelligible to

the judge and jury. the judge be kindly, the jury somnolent. Jacob

be the only member of the party with a clean record, and he receive

the light sentence of eighteen month; the rest have double that term

and more. in the Sunday paper they be describe as a dangerous

gang, and their portrait be draw like profile on a coin by an

artist whose business it be to make villain look villainous for the

delectation of the sober million who taste the joy of wickedness

only in print. Golda be stagger by the blank indifference of the

world to her husband's honesty. his word to she be law, but the judge

and the newspaper sweep it aside, and he be regard as one with the

wicked man whose crooked dealing have involve the innocent. this be

the bad disaster that have ever break upon she: husband and

son-in-law both sweep away from she, as it seem now, in one moment.

the sympathy she receive from the neighbour touch she profoundly,

and she accept their view that the sudden abstraction of male

relative be a natural calamity, like sickness or fire. thank to Mr.

Kuit the business would be keep together, and thank to Abramovich she

never lack company. that faithful friend would come in in the

evening and go over the trial, every moment of which he have hear,

and recount every word of Jacob's speech, which to he be a piece of

magnificent oratory. "not a tear be leave in my eye," he say. "not a

throb be leave in my heart, and the judge be move, for his face sink

into his hand and I could see that he know how unjust he must be."

and he spend many day ferret out a villain to be the cause of it

all, some inveterate, implacable enemy who have plot the downfall of

the most honest man in London. he fix on a certain Mr. Rosenthal,

who year ago have try to sell they machine for the business when

they have already buy all that be necessary. he be quite sure it

be Mr. Rosenthal who have bribe the thief to hold their tongue,

when any one of they could have clear Jacob in a moment. and Golda

believe that it be Mr. Rosenthal and dream of unattainable act of

revenge.



Mendel use to listen to they talk, and their voice seem to he

to come from very far away. the upheaval have stun he, have

destroy his volition and paralyse his dream. he feel as though a

tight band be fix round his head. he have neither desire nor will.

the world could do as it like with he. if the world could suddenly

invade his home and brand its head and lawgiver as thief, then the

world be empty and foolish and it do not matter what happen. it

amaze he that his brother and sister could go about as usual: that

Harry could come home and talk of prize-fighter and sit write to

girl, and that Issy could go out to meet his Rosa at the corner of

the street. it be astonishing that his mother could still cook and

they could still eat, and that every morning Harry could go down and

open the door to let in the workpeople to clatter up the stair. . . .

and Harry dislike get out of bed in the morning. in his father's

absence he venture to apply his considerable ingenuity to the

problem, and rig up a wire from his bed to the knob of the

front-door. nor be this the only sign of the removal of the centre of

authority from the family, for Issy actually bring his girl Rosa to

the house and make his mother be pleasant to she. . . . Golda feel

that her child be grow beyond she, and she think it be time

Issy be think of get marry, though not to Rosa, whose father

be a poor cobbler and could give she no money.



at regular interval. Golda swallow down her dread of the busy

street and go to Pentonville, where through the bar of the

visitors'-room Jacob receive her report and give his instruction. he

decreed against Rosa, who accordingly be forbid to enter the house

again. he have order for every one of his child except Mendel, as

to whom Golda do not consult he. deep in her inmost heart she be in

revolt against her husband, for she have begin to see that he have

carry pride to the point of folly, and all her hope, all her

dream, all her ambition be centre upon her darling boy. her

ambition be not worldly. she know nothing at all about the world,

and do not believe three part of what she hear of it. only she

long for he to escape the bitterness and bareness that have be her

portion. the boy be so beautiful and could be so gay and could dance

so lightly, and would sometimes be so tempestuous and masterful. it

would be a sin if he be to be cramp over a board or be send to

work in a tailoring shop. she herself have a love of flower and of

moonlight and the star shine through the smoky sky, and she would

sometimes find herself be urge to the use of strange word, which

would make Mendel raise his head and cock his ear as though he be

listen to the very beat of her heart. to that no one in the world

have ever listen, and her life seem very full and worthy when

Mendel in his childish fashion be awake to it. . . . Pentonville

seem to suit Jacob. he look almost fat and say the cocoa be very

good.



the time come for Mendel to leave school and Issy say he have well

be take into the workshop. Harry want he in the timber-yard in

which he loaf away his day. Abramovich be for get Mr. Jacobson

to take he into his office, for Mr. Jacobson never fail to ask

after the boy who paint the picture. now it so happen that Mendel

have find a bookshop, outside which he have discover a life of W. P.

Frith, R.A. in daily visit over a period of three week he have read

it from cover to cover, the story of a poor boy who have become an

artist, rise to such fame that he have paint the portrait of the

Queen. there it be in print, and must be true. Mr. Jacobson's boy be

only in a story, but here it be set down in a book, with

reproduction of the artist's wonderful pictures--"The Railway

Station," "Derby Day." the book say they be wonderful. the book

speak with reverence and enthusiasm of picture and the man who

paint they.



with tremulous excitement he secretly produce his box of paint

again, and squeeze out the colour on to the plate he use for a

palette. he adore the colour and amuse himself with paint smooth

strip of blue, yellow, green, red, orange, grey, for the sheer

delight of handle the delicious stuff. it be a new pleasure, the

joy of colour in themselves without reference to any object, or any

feeling inside himself except this simple thrilling delight. he could

forget everything in it, for it be his first taste of childish glee.

nothing would ever be the same again. nothing could ever again so

oppress and overwhelm he as distasteful and even pleasant thing have

do in the past. he would be an artist, a wonderful artist, like W.

P. Frith, R.A.



so when he be call into the kitchen one night and they tell he he

be to go into Mr. Jacobson's office, he look as though their word

have no meaning for he, and he said:--



"I want to be an artist."



an artist? nobody know quite what that mean. Golda think it mean

paint picture, but she could not imagine a man devote all his

time to it--a child's pastime.



"he mean the drawing!" say Abramovich. "I have a friend at home who

use to paint the flower on the cup."



"I'm go to be an artist," say Mendel.



"but you've get to make your money like everybody else," reply Issy.



Mendel retort with detail of what he could remember of the career

of his idol. Issy say that be a _Christlicher kop._ there ben't

such thing as jewish artist; whereon Harry throw in the word

"Rubinstein." ask to explain what he mean, he do not know, but have

just remember the name.



Abramovich say he think Rubinstein be a conductor at the Opera,

and there be jewish singer and actor.



"my father," say Harry, "willn't hear of that. he willn't have a son of

his make a public show of himself."



Mendel by this time be white in the face, and his eye be glare

out of his head. he know that not one of they have understand his

meaning, and he feel that Issy be bent on have his way with he. he

be in despair at his helplessness, and at last, when he could endure

no more, he fling himself down on the floor and howl. Issy lose his

temper with he, pick he up, and carry he, kick and bite,

upstairs, and fling he on his bed.



the subject be drop for a time, but Mendel refuse to eat, or to

sleep, or to leave the house. he be afraid that if he put his nose

outside the door Abramovich would pounce on he and drag he off to

Mr. Jacobson's office.



however, the matter could not be postpone for long, because money be

very scarce and the boy must be put into the way of provide for

himself. Golda ask Abramovich to find out what an artist be and how

much a week could be make at the trade. Abramovich come in one evening

with a note-book full of fact and figure. he have read of a picture

be sell for ten of thousand of pound, and this have make a great

impression on he. Mendel be call down from the room in which he

have exile himself.



"well?" say Abramovich kindly. "so you want to be an artist? but

how?"



"I don't know. I shall paint picture."



"but who will feed you? who will buy you paint, brush?"



"I shall sell my picture."



"where, then? how?"



"to the shop."



"where be the shop? tell I of any shop near here, for I don't know

a single one."



again Mendel feel that they be too clever for he, and he be on the

brink of another fit of despair when, fortunately for he, Mr.

Macalister, a commercial traveller in fur, come in. when he be in

London he make a point of call on the Kühlers, whom he like, much

as he like strong drink. he be a man of some attainment, a student

of Edinburgh, who have find the ordinary walk and the ordinary people

of life too tame for his chaotic and vigorous temper, and he go from

place to place collect just such strange people as these Polacks,

as he use to call they. he look for passion in man and woman, and

accept it gratefully and even greedily wherever he find it. . . .

he have red hair and a complexion like a white-heart cherry, with

little twinkle eye as blue as forget-I-not.



he kindle at once to the passion with which Mendel be burst,

stoop over Golda's hand and kiss it--for he know that be how

foreigner greet a lady--and then he sit heavily wait for the

situation to be explain to he. Mendel instinctively appeal to

he. . . . oh yes! he know what an artist be, and some painter have

make tidy fortune, though they be not the good of they. there be

Reynolds, and Lawrence, and Raeburn, and Landseer, and some young

fellow at Glasgow, and Michael Angelo--a tidy lot, indeed. never a

Jew, that he have hear of.



"I tell you so!" say Abramovich.



Golda show Mr. Macalister the boy's picture, and he be genuinely

impressed, especially by a picture of three orange in a basket.



"it's not," he say, "that they make you want to eat they, as that

they make you look at they as you look at orange. I'll look close at

every orange I see now. that's talent. yes. that's talent. aye."



Mendel be so grateful to he that he forget the other and begin to

point out to he how well the orange be paint, with all their

fleshiness and rotundity bring out. and very soon they be all

laugh at he, and that make the meeting happy.



Mr. Macalister explain that in old day artist use to take boy

into their studio, but that now there be Schools of Art where only

very talented people could survive. he certainly think that Mendel

ought to be give a chance, and if it be a question of money, he,

poor though he be, would be only too glad to help. Golda would not

hear of that, and Abramovich protest that, in an unhappy time like

this, he regard himself as the representative of his unfortunate

friend.



the corner be turn. feeling be now all with Mendel, and he go to

bed singe in head and heart: "I'm an artist! I'm an artist! I'm an

artist!"



* * * * *



so the ball be set roll. Jacob, see behind the bar, raise no

objection. he have have time to think, and, to the extent of his

capacity, avail himself of it. when he be tell that his young

son want to be an artist and weep at the suggestion of anything

else, he think: "who be I to say 'yea' or 'Nay'?" and he say "Yea."

"let the boy have a little happiness while he may, for the Christians

be very powerful and will take all that he cherish from he."



the question of way and mean be consider, and here Abe

Moscowitsch take charge. his business have prosper during his

enforce absence, and his bankruptcy have be very profitable. he be

a decent man, and anxious to make amend to his young wife and her

family for the trouble his adventurousness have bring on they. to

please she he take a new house with bow-window and a garage, and to

please they he jump at the opportunity of help Mendel, and

offer to pay his fee at a School of Art. when the boy hear this he

run to his brother-in-law's office and, before all his workman, fling

his arm round his neck and embrace he.



"that'll do. that'll do," say Moscowitsch. "don't forget we if you're

a rich man before I be."



"I shall never leave home," say Mendel. "I shall never marry. I shall

live all my day with my mother, painting."



there arise the difficulty that no one have ever hear of a School of

Art. Mr. Macalister be depute to look into the matter. he inquire,

and be recommend to the Polytechnic as be cheap and good, and

the Polytechnic be decide on.



Mr. Kuit come in at the tail of all this excitement, and add to it

by say that he be just off to America, first-class by the Cunard

Line, for he be go to start in style, live in style, and come back

in style. he be delighted to hear of the brilliant future opening up

before Mendel, and tell wonderful story of famous picture that have

be steal, cut out of their frame and take away under the very

nose of the owner. he be wonderfully overdress, not loudly or

vulgarly, but through his eagerness to be and to look first-class. he

produce a pack of card and show how he could shuffle they to suit

himself, and three time out of five, through the fineness of the

touch, he could "spot" a card. he be a wonderful man. the Kühlers

gape at he, and Moscowitsch, in emulation, be lead on to brag of his

smartness in business, and how he have thrice burn down his

timber-yard and make the insurance people pay up. yet, though he

warm up as he boast, he lack the magic of Mr. Kuit and could not

conceal the meanness of his deed behind their glamour. he lumber

along like a great bear behind Mr. Kuit, and be vex because he

could not overtake he, and when the glitter little Jew, who seem

more magician than thief, say he would give Mendel a new suit of

clothe for his entry into the world of art, Moscowitsch promise to

provide a new pair of boot. Mr. Kuit counter with two new hat,

Moscowitsch with underclothe. on they go in competition until

Mendel be magnificently equip, and at last Moscowitsch lay two

new sovereign on the table and say they be for the boy's

pocket-money. not to be outdo, Mr. Kuit produce a five-pound note

and give it to Golda to be put into the Post Office Savings Bank.



in her inmost heart Golda be alarm. for the first time she begin to

realize the vast powerful London with which she be surround. at

home, in Austria, people steal because they be poor, because they

be starve. she herself have often send Harry and Issy out into the

market with a sack and a spiked stick with which to pick up potato

and cabbage and bread, but here the old simplicity be lack. the

swagger and the magnitude of Mr. Kuit's operation and her

son-in-law's fraud alarm she, and she feel that no good could come

of it. they belong to some power which move too fast for she, and

it be be invoke for Mendel, her youngest-bear, her treasure.

truly it be a black day that take Jacob from she. where he be, there

be simplicity. everything be keep in its place when he be in

authority. everything be keep down on the earth. there be the good

smell of the earth in all his dealing, all his emotion. never in he

be the easy fantastic excitement of Kuit and Moscowitsch . . . they

be mad. surely they be mad. their excitement infect everybody.

Golda could feel it creep in her vein like a poison. it come from

the world to which these man belong, the world of prison. that one

word express it all for Golda. she have only be out into it to go

to the prison, and to she that seem to be the cold empty centre of

it all. the bustle and glitter of the street lead to the prison, and

she have always to fight to get back into her own life, where thing

be simple and definite--ugly, maybe--but clear and actual. . . . and

now into that world of hectic excitement play about the prison and

about Mendel be to go, to be she know not what, to learn to play with

brush and colour, to practise trick which seem to she not

essentially different from Mr. Kuit's sleight with the card. she be

sure no good could come of it; but for the present the boy have his

happiness, and to that she yield.







IV



FIRST LOVE



for Mendel every day become romantic, though he suffer torture of

shyness and use to bolt like a rabbit through the door of the

Polytechnic, rush upstairs to his easel, and never raise his eye from

it except to gaze at the object place before he. he work in a

frenzy, convince that it be his business to translate the object on

to the canvas. when he have do that he feel that the object have no

further existence. it ought to vanish as completely as his consume

interest in it. as a matter of fact, it never do vanish, but it be

lose in the praise of Mr. Sivwright, and the young woman and old

lady who attend the class. the first task set the class after he

join it be a ginger-beer bottle, of which his rendering be

declare to be a marvel, even to the high light on the marble in the

neck of the bottle.



he be rather small for his age and be almost absurdly beautiful,

with his curly hair, round austrian head, and amusing prick ear.

his eye be set very wide apart. they be blue. his nose be

straight, and very slightly tip-tilt, and his lip be as

delicately model as the petal of a rose. they be always

tremulous as he shrink under the vivid impression that pour in on

he in bewildering profusion. he begin to grow physically and

spiritually, though not at all mentally, and he live in a state of

bewilderment, retain shrewdness enough to cling to the necessary

plain fact that he be at the school to be a success, for if he fail

he would sink back into the already detestable world inhabit by Issy

and Harry.



he create quite a stir at the school. Mr. Sivwright, a Lancashire

Scotsman, whose youthful revolt against commerce and grime have carry

he in the direction of art only so far as the municipal school, say

he be an infant prodigy and make a show of he. to Mendel's disgust

Mr. Sivwright assure the other pupil that he be a Pole. this be

his first intimation that there be, in the splendid free Christian

world, a prejudice against Jews. he be rather shocked and disgusted,

for never in his life have he find occasion to call anything by other

than its right name. it take he week to conquer his shyness

sufficiently to protest.



"I be a Jew," he say to Mr. Sivwright. "why do you call I a Pole?"



"well," say Mr. Sivwright, "there's Chopin, you know, and Paderewski,

don't you know, and Kosciusko, and the Jews don't stand for anything

but money. and, after all, you do come from Poland."



"but I be a Jew."



"you don't look it, and there's some swing about be a Pole. there's

no swing about be a Jew. it stop dead, you know. I don't know why

it be, but it stop dead."



the word frighten Mendel. how awful it would be if he be to stop

dead, to reach the Polytechnic and to go no far!



he be soon take beyond the Polytechnic, for Mr. Sivwright lead he to

the National Gallery and show he the treasure there. the boy be

at once prostrate before Greuze. ah! there be softness, tenderness,

charm: all that he have lack and long for. it be in vain that Mr.

Sivwright take he to the Van Eycks and the Teniers and the Franz

hal, strike an attitude and say: "fine! dramatic! that's the

real stuff!" the boy would return to his Greuze and pour out on the

pretty maidens all the longing for emotion with which he be fill,

and the yearning seem to he to be the irresistible torrent of art

which carry those who feel it to the pinnacle of fame. . . . yet he

know that Mr. Sivwright be a shoddy failure of a man, and he know

that Mr. Sivwright's ecstasy be force and have small connection

with the picture before he. he also know that he have not the least

desire to paint like Greuze, but he could not resist the fascination

of the pretty maidens and the gush of feel he have in front of they.

the Italians he do not understand and Velasquez and El Greco repel

he. also, the picture as a whole excite he so that they run into

each other and he could not extricate they, and Greuze become his

stand-by. he feel safe with Greuze.



every day he use to go home and tell his mother of the day's doing,

from the moment when he mount the bus in the morning to the time

when he walk home in the evening. he give her minute account of all

the people in the class, of the cheap restaurant where he have lunch,

of the marvel of the street: the old woman sell flower at Oxford

Circus; the gorgeous shop-window; the illuminated sign and

advertisement, green, red, and yellow; the theatre; the poster of

the comic man outside the music-hall; the rich people in their

motor-car; the marvellous lady in their silk and their fur; the

poor man sell match; the scarlet soldier and blue sailor; the

big policeman who stop the traffic with their white hand; the

awful, endless desolation of Portland Place, with tree--actually

tree--at the end of it; the whirl, the glitter, the roar, the

splendour of London. and he use to mimic for she the strange people

he see, the mince lady and the lordly shopwalker, the titter

girl and the man work in the street. the more excited he be the

more depressed be Golda. what be it all for? why could not people

live a decent quiet life? why be all this whirligig revolve round

the prison? . . . but she smile and laugh and applaud he, and

believe he when he say none of the Christians could draw as well as

he.



he begin to win prize. it become his whole object to beat the

Christians. what they tell he to paint he would paint well than any

of they. and by sheer will and concentration he succeed.



Mr. Sivwright say there be no hold he, and very soon declare he

have nothing more to learn.



this be take by Mendel and his family to mean that he be now an

artist. in all good faith he establish himself in a room below the

workshop at home, call it his studio, and set to work. for a few

month he paint apple, fish, orange, portrait of his mother,

brother, and sister, and for a time be able to sell they among his

acquaintance. he have one or two commission for portrait and could

always make a few shilling by paint from photograph. but

appreciation of art among his own people be limited; he soon come to

an end of it, and there be that other world call to he. art lie

beyond that other world. he feel sure of that. it lie beyond Mr.

Sivwright. if he stay among his own people he would stop dead; for

he know now that it be true that the Jews stop dead.



and then to his horror he stop. for no reason at all his skill, his

enthusiasm, his eagerness leave he. he force himself to paint,

transfer innumerable idiotic face from photograph to cigar-box

lid, make his mother neglect her work to sit to he, bribe Lotte to

be his model, but hate and loathe everything that he do. he be

listless, sometimes feverish, sometimes leaden and cold. often he

think he be go to die--to die before anything have happen,

before anything have emerge from the chaos of his painful vivid

impression.



to make thing bad, his father come home and say that he would give

he six month in which to make his living, and at the end of that

time, if he have fail, he would have to go into the workshop.



he feel hopeless. he go to see Mr. Sivwright and pour out his woe

to he, who write a letter to Jacob say that his son be a genius

and would be one of the great of painter. Jacob say: "what be a

genius? I do not know. I know what a man be, and a man work for his

living. in six month, if you can make fifteen shilling a week I will

believe in this painting. if not, what be there to believe? what will

you do when you be to marry, heh? tell I that. will your little

tube of paint keep a wife, heh? tell I that."



Mendel could say nothing. he could do nothing. he give up even try

to paint, for he might as well have play with mud-pie. he borrow

money from his brother and prowl about the street, and go to the

National Gallery. Greuze mean nothing to he now. he begin to feel,

very faintly, the force of Michael Angelo, but the rest only fill

he with despair. he know nothing--nothing at all. he could not even

begin to see how the picture be paint. they be miraculous and

detestable. . . . he go home and comfort himself with a little

picture of some apple on a plate. he have paint it two year before

in an ecstasy--a thrilling love for the form, the colour, the texture

of the fruit and the china. it be good. he know it be good, but he

know he could do nothing like it now--never again, perhaps.



and how disgusting the street have become! such a litter, such a

noise, such aimless, ugly people! he could understand his mother's

horror of they. ah! she never fail he. to she his word be always

music, his presence be always light. half-dead and miserable as he

be, she could know and love the aching heart of he that live so

furiously behind all the death and the misery and the ashe of young

hope that crust he. she be like the sky and the tree. she be

like the young grass spring and wave so delicately in the wind.

she be like the water and the rolling hill. . . . he have discover

these thing at Hampstead, whither he have go out of sheer

aimlessness. he have never be in the Tube, and one day, with a

shilling borrow from Harry, it seem appropriate to he to plunge

into the bowel of the earth. the oppression of the air, the roar of

the train, the flash of the station as he move through they, suit

his mood, fantastic and futile. he get out at Hampstead.



it be his first sight of the country. he could hardly move at first

for emotion. he find himself laugh, and he stoop and touch the

grass tenderly, almost timidly, as though he be afraid of hurt

it. he be fearful at first of walk on it, but that seem to he

childish, and he stride along with his quick, light-footed stride and

lose himself in the willow grove. he make a posy of wild-flower and

take they back to his mother, carry they unashamedly in his hand,

entirely oblivious of the smile of the passer-by. he know he could

not tell his mother of the happiness of that day, and the flower

could say more than any word.



yet the happiness only make his misery more acute. he suffer

terribly from the pious narrowness of his home, the restrict,

cramp life of his brother and sister, who seem to he to be
