steal such life as they have from the religious observance to which

they be bind by their father's rigid will. prayer at home, prayer

in the synagogue: the dreadful monotony of the home, of the talk, of

the squabble: human life force to be as dull as that of the God who

no long interfere in human life. . . . there be a tragedy in the

street. there have be a scandal. a young Rabbi, a gloriously handsome

creature, who sing in the synagogue, have fall in love with a little

girl of fourteen who live opposite the Kühlers. Golda have watch the

intrigue from her window, and she say it be the girl's fault. the

Rabbi use to go every day when her father be out and she use to let

he in. Jacob write to the girl's father, and the Rabbi leave his

lodging and take a room over a little restaurant round the corner. he

have his dinner and go upstairs and sit up all night singing, in his

lovely tenor voice, love song and religious chant, so sweetly that

the neighbour throw their window open and there be a little crowd

of people in the street listening. and in the morning they find he

with his throat cut.



"it be the girl's fault," say Golda, but Jacob say: "a man should

know well than to melt when a little girl practise her eye on

he."



this tragedy relax the nervous strain which have be set up in

Mendel by his trouble. new force stir in he which often make he

hectic and light-headed. woman change their character for he. they

be no long soothe ministrant, but creature charge with a

mysterious, a madden charm. he tremble at the rustle of their

skirt and his eye be hold rivet by their movement. he be

suffocate by his new curiosity about they.



sometimes, in his despair over his painting and the apparently

complete disappearance of his talent, he would fill in the day in his

father's workshop, stretch rabbit-skin on a board. girl and man

work together, busily, quietly, dexterously, for the most part in

silence, for they be pay by the piece and be unwilling to waste

time. there be a girl who have just be take into the workshop to

learn the trade. she be small and plump and swarthy, but her face be

beautiful, the colour of rich old ivory. her eye be black and

golden from a ruddy tinge in her eyelash. her lip be full and

pouting, and she have long blue-black hair, which she be always

toss back over her shoulder. when Mendel be there she rarely take

her eye off he, and even when her head be bent he could feel that

she be watch he.



he wait for her one evening, and with his knee almost knock

together he ask if she would come to his studio and let he draw

she. with a silly giggle she say she would come, and she run away

before he could get out another word.



the next evening he wait in his studio for she, but she do not

come. so again the next and the next, and it be a whole week before

she knock at the door. he pull she in. neither could speak a word.

at last he stammer out:



"I--I haven't get my draw thing ready."



"I don't mind," she say, and she give a little shiver.



"be you cold?" he ask, and he touch her neck.



she throw up her head, seem to fall towards he, and their lip meet.



Thrilling and sweet be the hour they spend, lose in the miracle of

desire, find themselves again, laugh happily, weep happily,

break through into the enchanted world, where the few word that

either know have lose their meaning. they be hardly conscious of each

other. they know nothing of each other, and wish to know nothing

except the lovely mystery they share. it be some time before he even

know her name, or where she live, or what her people be. she

exist for he only in the enchantment she bring into his life, in

the release from his burden, in the marvellous free life of the body.

Royal he feel, like a king, like a master, and she be a willing

slave. from home she would steal good thing to eat, and she would sit

with shine eye watch he eat; and then she would wait until he

have need of she. . . . strange, silent, happy hour they spend, free

together in the dark little room, free as bird in their nest, happy

in warm contact, utterly quiescent, utterly oblivious. . . .



soon their silence become oppressive to they, but neither could break

it, so far beyond their year and their childish mind be the

experience in which they be join. when the first ecstasy pass

and they become conscious and deliberate in their delight, they have

unhappy moment, to escape from which he begin to draw she. into this

work pour a strong cool passion altogether new to he, a joy so

magnificent that he would forget she altogether. he be tyrannical,

and keep she so still that she would almost weep from fatigue and

boredom. but he be not satisfied until he have draw every line of

she, and have translate she from the world of the body to the world of

vision and the spirit. he know nothing of that. he be only concern

to draw she as he have draw the ginger-beer bottle at the Polytechnic.

certain part of her body--her little bud breast and her round

arm--especially delight he, and he draw they over and over again.

her head he draw twenty time, and he find a shop in the West End

where he could sell every one. and each time he buy she a little

present.



she be not satisfied with that. she want to display he to her

friend. she want he to take she to music-hall and to join the

parade of boy and girl. he refuse. that would be profanation. he

and she have nothing to do with the world. he and she be the world.

outside it be only his drawing. he could not see that she be unable

to share it. do he not draw she? do he dream of draw anything but

she? . . . . to go from that to restaurant, the lascivious

pleasantry of the street, the garish music-hall, be to he

unthinkable.



she say he care more for his drawing than for she, and indeed he

would sometimes draw for a couple of hour and then kiss she almost

absent-mindedly, just as she be go. he be so happy and satisfied

and could not imagine she be anything less, or that she might wish

to express in music-hall and "fun" what he express in his work.



he feel gloriously confident, and naïvely tell his mother how happy he

be. everything have come back. he could draw well than ever. he

would be a great artist.



once more he take to painting in the kitchen. the studio be dedicate

to the girl, Sara, who come to he in spite of her disappointment. he

have spoil she for other boy.



he paint all day long in the kitchen, and his life became order

and regular. he go for a walk in the morning, then work all day

long until the workpeople begin to clatter downstair, when he would

pack up his paint-box and run up to the studio to wait for Sara to

come tap softly at his door.



Golda be overjoy at his new happiness and the bud manhood in

he, but she know that this springtime of his youth could not be

without a cause. she know that he be in love and be fearful of

consequence, and dread his being fatally entangle. she keep watch

and see Sara stealthily leave the house hour after the other

workpeople have go. she tell Jacob, and Sara be dismiss and

forbid ever to come near the house again.







v



a turning-POINT



AT first Mendel hardly notice the passing of Sara. he wait

anxiously for she to come, but when she never appear he go on

working, only gradually to discover that the first glorious impulse

have fade away. however, the habit of regular work be strong with

he, and he could go on like a carpenter or a mason or any other good

journeyman. but there be no one to buy what he produce, and his

father begin to talk gloomily and ominously of the workshop.



"never!" say Mendel. "if I be not a great artist by the time I be

twenty-three I will come and work. if I have do nothing by the time

I be twenty-three I shall know that I be no good."



"I can see no reason," say Jacob, "why you should not work like any

other man and paint in your spare time. issy be a good dancer in his

spare time, and Harry be good at the boxing. why should you not paint

in your spare time and work like an honest man?"



Mendel turn on his father and rent he.



"you do not know what work be. you work with your hand. yes. but do

can see more inside than they can outside? if I cannot paint I shall

die. I shall be like a bird that cannot sing, like a woman that have no

child, like a man that have no strength. I tell you I shall die if I

cannot paint."



"yes, he will die," say Golda. "he will surely die."



"he will die of starvation if he go on painting," reply Jacob.

he be pay five shilling a week, and be tell that if he behave

DEAR SIR,--

    I send you my paper fill up. my father be a poor man and I wish

to be a painter. I have win prize at a school, but I cannot make my

live by my art. I be not ask for charity. I be only ask that

my work shall be judge. if it be good painting, then let I paint.

give I my opportunity, please. if it be bad painting, then it be no

great matter, and I will go on until I can paint well, and then I will

show you my work again. if money be give I I will pay every penny of

it back when I be as successful as I shall be. I be send three

drawing and two painting.

    your faithfully,

        mendel KÜHLER.



this letter be send enclose in a parcel make up with trembling

attain the desire contact with the outside world. he be wildly

VI



EDGAR FROITZHEIM and other



very bright be the brass on Mr. Froitzheim's front door, very bright

the face of the smile maid who open it. Mendel blush and

"will you come in?" say the maid, "and I will ask Mr. Froitzheim."



she leave Mendel in the hall and disappear. this be a very large

through the window at the end of the passage be see tree and a

vast panorama of London--roof, chimney, steeple, dome--under a

shift pall of blue smoke.



the maid go into the studio and tell Mr. Froitzheim that a boy be

"Hullo, boy!" say Mr. Froitzheim.



"M-Mendel Kühler."



"something to show I, eh?"



"ye-yes. picture."



"what do you say your name be?"



"Kühler. Mendel Kühler."



"oh yes. I remember. you know Maurice Birnbaum?"



"no."



"Eh? . . . what do you think of these? lovely, eh? buy they in

India. you should go there. you don't know what sunlight be until

you've be there--to the East. ah, the East! fill you with sunlight,

open your eye to colour. . . . persian print! what do you think of

he show Mendel a whole series of exquisite thing which move he so

"yes. yes. it be true. he respond to beauty like a flower to the

sun."



in the centre of the studio be a large picture nearly finish of

three child and a rock-horse, cleverly and realistically

compose and make picture he could not understand.



"well, well," say Mr. Froitzheim. "so you want to be an artist? art,

as Michael Angelo say, be a music and mystery that very few be

privileged to understand. I have be ask by the committee to give

Mr. Froitzheim produce a pair of spectacle and balance they on his

"it be a serious thing, not only for the sake of the young man but

yet there be unhappy day of wait. Jacob would not hear of his

while one of the footman mount guard over he. he stand there for a

long time, still ashamed, bewildered, smell money, money, money,

until he reel. it make he think of Mr. Kuit, who alone of his

acquaintance could have be at his ease in such splendour. he feel

beggarly, but he be stiffen in his pride.



the butler appear presently and conduct he upstairs to a vast

apartment all crystal and cloth of gold. in the far corner sit a group

of people, among whom, in his confusion, Mendel could only distinguish

Maurice Birnbaum and a small, wrinkle, bald old man with a beard,

whose eye be quick and black, peer out from under the yellow

skull, peer out and take nothing in. for the purpose of take

in his nose seem more than sufficient. it be like a beak, like an

invert scoop. and yet his feature be not so very different from

those of the old man at home whom Mendel reverence. there be a

strange dignity in they, yet not a trace of the fine quality of the

old face he love that look so sorrowfully out on the world, and

through their eye and through every line seem to absorb from the

world all its suffering, all its vileness, and to transmute it into

strong human beauty. there be some woman present, but they make no

impression whatever on Mendel, who be entirely occupy with Sir

Julius and with resist the feeling of helplessness with which he

be inspire in his presence. he hear Maurice Birnbaum talk about

he, describe his life, his mother's kitchen, the street where he

live, and then he be tell to exhibit his picture. a footman

appear and put out a chair for he, and on this, one after another,

he place his drawing and picture. not a word be say. even the

apple be receive in silence. Sir Julius give a grunt and begin to

talk to one of the woman. Maurice give Mendel to understand that the

interview be over, and the poor boy be conduct downstairs by the

butler. he have not a penny in his pocket and have to walk all the way

home with his parcel, which his arm be hardly long enough to hold.







VII



the DETMOLD



FLUNG into the art school, he be like a leggy colt in a new field,

very shy of it at first, of the tree in the hedge, of the shadow

cast by the tree. this place be very different from the Polytechnic.

there be few old lady, and more boy of his own age. the

teacher be Professors, and the pupil hold they in awe and respect.

there be real model in the life-class, male and female, and the

student, male and female, work together. no ginger-beer bottle

here, where art be a practical business. the school exist for the

purpose of teach the craft of make picture, and its law be that

the basis of the mystery be draw.



Mendel's first attitude towards the other student be that he be

there to beat they all. he would swell with eagerness and enthusiasm,

and tell himself that he have something that they all lack. he would

watch their movement, their head bend over their work, their

hand scratch away at the paper, and he could see that they have

none of they the vigour that be in himself. and by way of show how

much strong he be he would use his pencil almost as though it be

a chisel and his paper a block of stone out of which he be to carve

the likeness of the model. he be rudely take down when the Professor

stand and stare with his melancholy eye at his production and

said:--



"be that the good you can do?"



"yes."



"why do it?"



this be a stock phrase of the Professor's, but Mendel do not know

that, and he be ashamed and outraged when the class titter.



"no," say the Professor. "I don't know what that be. it certainly

ben't draw." and with his pencil he make a lovely easy sketch of

the model, alongside Mendel's black, forbid scrawl. it be a

masterly thing and it baffle he, and humiliate he because the

Professor move on to the next pupil without another word. not another

line could Mendel draw that day. he sit stare at the Professor's

sketch and at his own drawing, which, while he have be do it, have

mean so much to he, and he still prefer his own. the Professor's

drawing have no meaning for he. he could not understand it, except

that it be accurate. that he could see, but then his own be accurate

too, and true to what he have see. the light give the model a

distorted shoulder, and he have labour to render that distortion,

which the Professor have either ignore or have correct.



Mendel cut out the Professor's drawing and take it home and copy it

over and over again, but still he could not understand it. he be in

despair and tell Golda he would never learn.



"I shall never learn to draw, and the christian kop will all beat

I," he say.



"but they send you to the school because you can draw. don't Mr.

Froitzheim say that you could draw!"



"the Professor look at I with his gloomy face, like an undertaker

ask for the body, and he say: 'I mean to say, that ben't draw.

it ben't impressionism. I don't know what it be.'"



"it cann't be a very good school," say Golda.



"but it be. it be the only school. all the good painter have be

there, and Mr. Froitzheim send his own brother to it. the Professor

say I shall never paint a picture if I don't learn to draw, and I

cann't do it, I cann't do it!"



to console himself he paint hard every evening and regard the

Detmold entirely as a place to which his duty condemn he--a place

where he have to learn this strange wizardry call drawing, which he

do not understand. he go there every day and never speak to a soul,

because he realize that his speech be different from that of the

other, and he would not open his mouth until he could speak without

betray himself. he listen carefully to their pronunciation and

intonation, and practise to himself in bed and as he walk through

the street.



so woeful be his attempt to emulate the detmold style of drawing,

that at last the Professor ask he if he be do any work at home.

to this Mendel reply eagerly that he be paint a portrait of his

mother.



"Hum," say the Professor. "may I see it?"



so Mendel bring the picture, and the Professor said:--



"I mean to say, young man, that it wouldn't be a bad thing if you give

up work a little. I don't want to have to send in a bad report, but

what can I do? there's something in you, plenty of grit and all that,

but you're young, and, I mean to say, you're here to learn what we can

teach you. when we've do with you, you can go your own way and be

hang to you. if you want to smudge about with paint and fake what

you cann't draw, there's the Academy."



at this awful suggestion Mendel shudder. he be imbue enough with

the Detmold tradition to regard the Academy as Limbo.



he give up painting at home, and hurl himself desperately at the

task of produce a drawing that should satisfy the Professor. towards

the end of his first term he succeed, and have his reward in word of

praise in front of the class.



the Professor have meanwhile take one of the pupil aside and ask

he not to leave the poor little devil so utterly alone. "after all,"

he say, "the school don't exist only for draw. it have its social

side as well, and I don't like to see any one cold-shoulder unless

he deserve it. I mean to say, you other fellow have advantage which

don't necessarily entitle you to mop up all the good thing and leave

none for your fellow-creature."



Mitchell, the pupil, take his homily awkwardly enough, but promise

that he would do what he could. he seize his opportunity one day when

Mendel at lunch have horrify the company by pick up a chicken bone

and tear at it with his tooth. Mitchell take he aside and said:--



"I say, Kühler, old man, you'll excuse my mentioning it, you know, but

it ben't do. I mean, we eat our food with fork."



Mendel know what be mean, for at lunch he have be conscious of

horrified eye stare at he and have wish the floor would open and

swallow he up. he mutter incoherent word of thank and want to

rush away, but Mitchell catch he by the arm and said:--



"I say, we artist must hang together. there ben't many of this crowd

who will come to anything, and the Pro think no end of you. willn't you

come along and have tea with I and some of the other fellow?"



Mendel go with he, delight in the young man's easy,

condescend Public School manner and pleasant, crisp voice, in which

he speak with an exaggerated emphasis.



"Gawd!" he say. "it make I sick to see all the fool and the woman

waste their time there, scratch away, while those of we who have

any talent and could learn anything be leave to flounder along as well

we may. do you smoke?"



Mendel have never smoke, but he do not like to refuse. he take a

cigarette, which very soon make he feel sick and giddy. he lurch

along with Mitchell until they come to a tea-shop, where they find

two other young man whose face be familiar.



"I've bring Kühler," say Mitchell. "he's a genius. this be Weldon,

who be also a genius, and Kessler, who cann't paint for nut, and I'm a

blame fool, though it's not my fault. my father's a great man. Gawd!

what can you do when your own father take the shine out of you at

every turn?"



they begin to talk of picture and of one Calthrop, who be apparently

the great painter the world have ever see and a product of the

Detmold.



"sells everything he put his name to," say Kessler.



"what a man!" say Weldon. "go his own way, absolutely believe in

his art. if they like it, well and good. if they don't like it, let

'em lump it. he's as often drunk as not, and as for woman . . . !"



Weldon and Kessler desert picture for woman. Mitchell grow more and

more glum, while Mendel be still feel the effect of the cigarette

too strongly to be able to take in a word.



"Gawd!" say Mitchell. "there they go, talk away, absolutely

incapable of keep anything clear of woman. I cann't stand it."



he drag Mendel away, leave his friend to pay the bill; and, as

they walk, he explain that he be in love, and could not stand all

that bawdy rubbish, and he elaborate a theory that an artist need

to be in love to keep himself alive to the sanctity of the human body,

familiarity with which be apt to breed contempt or an excessive

curiosity. Mendel say that he also have be in love, and he give a

vivid account of his rapture with Sara.



"my God!" cry Mitchell; "you don't mean to say that she come to

you--a girl like that?"



"yes," say Mendel; "I be never so happy."



"but, I say, ben't you afraid?"



"she be very beautiful."



Mitchell ponder this for a long time. he seem to be profoundly

shake. at last he said:--



"but with a girl you _love?_"



"I love she when she be there."



"but when she ben't there?"



"I be busy paint."



"I say, you be a corker! if it be Weldon or Kessler I should say

you be lie."



"I do not lie," reply Mendel with some heat. "it may have be

wrong, but it be good, and I be happy after it. I think I should

have go mad without it, for everything have

disappear--everything--everything; and without paint you do not

understand how terrible and empty life be to I. I have nothing, you

see. I be poor, and my father and mother will always be poor. their

life be hard and beastly, but they do not complain, and I should not

complain if I do not have this other thing that I must do."



"well, I'm jolly glad to know you," say Mitchell. "I'm not much of a

fellow, but I'd like you to know my people. my father's a great man.

he'll stir you up. and you must come along with I and Weldon and

Kessler and see life while you're young. good-bye."



he shake hand vigorously with Mendel and stride off with his long,

rake stride, while Mendel stand glow with the happiness of having

find a friend, some one to whom he could talk almost as he talk to

Golda: a fine young Englishman, pink and oozing robustious health,

ease, refinement, and comfort. he think with a devoted tenderness of

Mitchell's rather absurd round face, with its tip-tilt nose and

blink eye, its little rosebud of a mouth and plump round chin, on

which there be hardly a trace of a beard. . . . "my friend!" think

Mendel, "my friend!" and he give a leap of joy. it mean for he the

end of his loneliness. no long be he to be the poor, isolate

Yiddisher, but he be to move and have his be with these fine young

man who be the lead spirit of the school, the guardian of the

tradition bequeath to it by the great Calthrop. . . . oh! he would

learn their way of drawing, he would do it well than any of they. he

would be gay with they and wild and merry and young. and all the while

secretly he would be work and work, follow up that inner

purpose until one day he appear with a picture so wonderful that the

Professor would say, like Mr. Sivwright, that he have nothing more to

learn. and because of his wonderful work, everybody would forget that

he be a Jew, and he would move freely and easily in that wonderful

England which he have begin to perceive behind the fresh young man like

Mitchell and the cool, pretty girl at the school. that England be

their inheritance and they seem hardly aware of it. he would win it

by work and by dint of the power that be in he.



of the girl at the school he be afraid. he blush and tremble when

any one of they speak to he, and he never notice they enough to

distinguish one from another, so that they exist only as a vague

nuisance and a menace to his happiness. before Mitchell he be

prostrate. he bewilder and confound that young man with his

outpouring, both by word of mouth and by letter. he have absolutely no

reserve, and pour out his thought and feeling, his experience,

and Mitchell at last take up a protective attitude towards he and

defend he from the detestation which he arouse in the majority of

his fellow-student. at the same time Mitchell often feel that of the

two he be the great child, and he would look back upon the year he

have spend at school in a rueful and puzzled state of mind, half

realize that he have be shove aside while the stream of life go

on, and that now he have to fight his way back into it. while Mendel

have be wrestle and struggle, he have be put away in

cotton-wool, every difficulty that have crop up have be meet, every

deep desire have find its outlet in convention. and now that he have

set out to be an artist, here be this Jew with year of hard work

behind he, and such a familiarity with his medium that he could do

more or less as he like without be hold up by shyness or

awkwardness. and it be the same in life. Mendel be abash by

nothing, be ashamed of nothing. life have many face. he be prepare

to regard they all, and to fit his conduct to every one of they. he

be critical, not because he wish to reject anything, but because he

must know the nature of everything before he accept it. he hate and

love simply and passionately, and if he feel no emotion he never

disguise the fact. whereas Mitchell and the other be so eager to

feel the emotion which their upbringing have deny that they leap

before they look and fabricate what they do not feel. Mendel

learn from they that life could be pleasant, and they become aware

that there be region of life beyond the fringe of pleasantness.

they soften he and he harden they. they be always together,

Mendel, Mitchell, Weldon and Kessler, work steadily enough, but out

of work hour kick up their heel and stampede through the

pleasure of London. . . . Calthrop be the divinity they serve. he

be a man of genius and have make the Detmold famous. those, therefore,

who come after he at the school must support he in everything. that

be Mitchell's contention, who be by now in full swing of revolt

against his Public School training, and in his adoration Mendel

follow he, and the other be drag in their train. Calthrop

dress extravagantly: so do the four. Calthrop smash furniture: so

do the four. and as Calthrop drink, embrace woman, and sometimes

paint outrageously, the four do all these thing.



to Mendel it be life--something new, rich, splendid, and thrilling.

he have live so long cramp over his work that it be almost agony to

he to move in this swift stream of incessant excitement. there be no

spirit of revolt in he. he could shed some of the outward form of

his religion, as to Golda's great distress he do, but against its

spirit he could not rebel. that he carry with he everywhere: the

bare stubborn faith in man, grind down by life and live in sorrow

all his day. happy he be not, nor do he expect to be so. he might

be happy one day, but he would be miserable the next. life in he be

not greatly concerned with either, but only to have both happiness and

misery in full measure. his deep feeling arise out of his work,

the first condition of his existence; they arise out of it and sink

back into it again. his work be the visible and tangible form of his

being, which he hate and love as it approach or recede from the

terrible power that be both beautiful and ugly, and yet something

transcending either. . . . and away there in London be the Christian

world of show. what he be seek lie beyond that, and not in the

dark Jewishness of his home. there lie the spirit, but the outward and

visible form be to be seek yonder, where the light flare and the

woman smile at themselves in mirror. he hurl himself into the

show of the christian world in a blind desire to break through they,

but always he be fling back, bruised, aching, and weary.



Day after day he would spend listlessly at home or at the school until

seven o'clock come and it be time to go to the Paris Café, to sit

among the painter and listen to violent talk, talk, talk--abuse of

successful man, derision of the great master, mysterious and awful

whisper of what man be do in Paris, terrible denunciation of

dealer, critic, and the public.



the café be a kind of temple and have its ritual. it be the aim of

the painter to "put some life into dear old London." Calthrop have

give a lead. he have determine that London should be awaken to art,

as the write folk of a past generation have arouse the swollen

metropolis to literature and poetry. London should be make aware of

its painter as Paris be aware of the Quartier Latin. bohemia should

no long be the territory of actress, horse-coper, and bet

tout. the Paris Café therefore become the shrine of Calthrop's

personality, and thither every night repair the artist and their

parasite, who see in the place an avenue to liberty and fame. in the

glitter and the excitement, the brilliance, the colour, the woman with

their paint face, the white marble-top table, the mirror along

the wall, the blue wreathe tobacco-smoke, Calthrop's personality

be magnify and concentrate as in a theatre. the café without he

be Denmark without the Prince, and Mendel find the hour before he

come or the evening when he do not come almost insupportable. yet it

be not the man's success or his fame or his notoriety that fascinate

the boy, whose instinct go straight to the immense vitality which

be the cause of all. Calthrop be a huge man, dark and glowering. to

Mendel he be like a figure out of the Bible--like King Saul, in his

black mood and the inarticulate fury that possess he sometimes;

and when he pick up and hurl a glass at some artist whose face or

whose work have offend he, he be very like King Saul hurl the

javelin.



there be always a thrill when he enter the café. the buzz would die

down. where would he sit and whom would he speak to? . . . it be one

of the great moment in Mendel's life when one evening Calthrop

come sweep in with his cloak fling round his shoulder and sit

opposite he and his three companion and raise a finger and

beckon.



"he want you," say Mitchell, push Mendel forward.



"come here, boy," growl Calthrop, stab with his pipe-stem in the

direction of the seat by his side. "come here and bring your friend.

buy a drawing of yours this morning. damn good."



Mitchell, Kessler, and Weldon come and sit at the table, all too

overawe to speak.



"what's your drink, heh?"



drink be order.



"rotten trade, art," say Calthrop. "dangerous trade. drink, woman,

flattery. don't drink. Marry, settle down, and your wife'll hate you

because you're always about the place. . . . God! I wish I could be a

Catholic. I'd be a monk. . . . my boy, don't get into the habit of

do drawing. they willn't look at your picture if you do, and we

want picture--my God, we do! everybody paint picture as though they

be for a competition. you've get life to draw from--real, stink

life. that's why I have hope of you."



Mendel be so fluttered and flatter that he could only gulp down his

drink and blink round the café, feel that all eye be upon he;

and indeed he be attract such attention as have never before be

bestow on he. a girl at the next table ogle he and smile. she

be with a young man whom the four detest and despise. this young

man reach over to take a bowl of sugar from their table. to take

anything from the great man's table without so much as "by your leave"

be sacrilege and be very properly resent. there be a scuffle, the

sugar be scatter on the floor, glass fell crash down, Mitchell

and Weldon hurl themselves on the young man, and the manager come

bustle up, cry: "if-a-you-pleess-a-gentleman." but there be no

break the mêlée. a waiter be send out for the police, and three

constable come file in. one of they seize Mitchell, and Mendel,

half mad with drink and excitement, see his beloved friend, as he

think, be take off to prison, leap on the policeman's back and

bring he down. in the confusion Calthrop and the other slip

away and Mendel be arrest, still fight like a wild cat, and lead

off to the police-station, the constable whisper kindly in his ear:

"Steady, my boy, steady. a youngster like you should keep clear of the

drink."



the inspector smile at the extreme youthfulness of the offender, but

decide that a taste of the cell would do no harm and that the boy

have well be sober before he be send home. so Mendel have four hour

on a hard bench until a constable come in and ask he if he want

bail. he say "yes," and, when ask for a name, give Calthrop's, who

presently arrive and see he liberate, after be tell to appear in

court next morning at ten o'clock.



when he reach home he find his mother wait up for he with wet

cloth in case his head should be bad.



"what now? what now?" she ask.



"I've be in prison."



"Prison!" Golda fling up her hand and sit down heavily. for she all

be lose. it be true then, that, outside in the world, at the other

end of it, be always prison, for the just and for the unjust, for the

old and for the young, for the innocent and for the guilty.



he try to make light of it. for he, too, it be a serious matter.

he see himself figure in the Sunday paper: "Famous Artist in the

Police Court," with his portrait in profile as on a medallion.

Birnbaum and Sir Julius would read it. he would be take away from the

Detmold and Edward Tufnell would never speak to he again. he

astonish, embarrassed, and delight Golda by fling himself in

her arm and sob out his grief.







VIII



HETTY FINCH



GOLDA be pass through a very difficult time. Rosa be hotter on

the pursuit of Issy than ever. Harry have have a violent quarrel

consequent on his reiterated demand for proof of the judicial

destruction of Christianity in America, and at last, like his father,

he go out and buy a clean collar and announce his departure for

Paris. he go away and not a word have be hear from he. Lotte

refuse to look at any of the young man bring by the match-maker,

and Leah be the only comfortable member of the family, and she make

no attempt to conceal her unhappiness with Moscowitsch. she would come

on Saturday evening and go up to her mother's room and fle herself

on the bed and cry her heart out, until late in the evening

Moscowitsch come to fetch she, when she would go meekly and apparently

happily enough. . . . and on the top of all these trouble, here be

Mendel go to the devil at a gallop.



Leah's trouble with Moscowitsch be that he would never let she go out

without he, and he could very rarely be persuade to go out at all.

as for go away in the summer, he could see no sense in it. he give

his wife a fine house. what more do she want? she have her child to

look after. what great pleasure could she desire? his life be

entirely fill with his business and his home, and he would not look

beyond they. the neighbour go to the seaside? the neighbour be

fool who live for ostentation and display. they do not know when

they be well off. . . . Moscowitsch have a great admiration for his

father-in-law as a man who know what life be and refuse to dilute

its savour with folly, and he regard Golda as a perfect type of

woman, one who leave the management of life to her husband and allow

herself to be absorb in her duty as a wife and mother.



but Leah long to go to the seaside. it become an obsession with she,

and, because she could never talk of it, she think of nothing else.

she be sick with envy when she see the neighbour go off with the

child carry bucket and spade. secretly she buy her own

child bucket and spade, though they be much too small to use

they.



at last, when her worry begin to tell on Golda, Leah declare that

what she need be sea air, and offer to take she for a fortnight

to Margate, and Golda, anxious to escape from the horror of Mendel's

come home night after night draw and white with dissipation, and

from the dread of an explosion from Jacob, consent, and ask if

Issy might go, as that Rosa of his be make he quite ill.



for Golda, Leah know that Moscowitsch would do anything in the world,

and so she procure his consent on condition that he be not expect

to accompany they, for he hate the sea, which have make he very ill

when he come to England, and he never wish to set eye on it again.



Leah already have the address of some lodging recommend to she by a

neighbour. she engage they, and on a fine July day go down to

Margate by the express with her child, Golda, and issy.



the lodging be let by a handsome, florid woman with masse of

bleach golden hair, a ruddle complexion, fat hand cover with

cheap ring, plump wrist rattle with bracelet, and a full bosom on

which brooch gleam. Leah think she a very fine woman, and be so

fascinated by she that she stay indoor day after day, help with

the housework and gossiping, so that she never once see the sea,

except from the train as she be leave. Mrs. Finch be a lady, by

birth, but she have be unfortunate. she have an uncle in the Army and

a cousin in the War Office, and she have live in London, in the good

part of the town, where, in her good day, she have have her flat. also

she have travel and have be to Paris and Vienna. but she have be

unfortunate in her friend. Leah commiserate she, and, open-mouthed,

gulp down all her tale of the gentleman she have know, while Golda,

eager for more information of the glitter world which have swallow

up her Mendel, listen too, fascinated and shudder. and Leah, to

show that she also be a person of some consequence, begin to talk of

her wonderful brother. she tell of the motor-car which have come and

whirl he away, of his visit to the millionaire's house, of the fine

friend he be make, of the man and woman he know whose name be

in the paper.



"every day," she say, "he be out to tea, and every evening he be out

at theatre and music-hall and party and flat and hotel, and his

friend be so rich that they pour money into his pocket. he just

make a few line on a piece of paper and they give he twenty pound,

or he make up some paint to look like a face or a pineapple and his

pocket be full of money."



"yes," say Golda uneasily. "he will be very rich."



"then next time you come to Margate," say Mrs. Finch, "it will be the

Cliftonville, and you'll despise my poor lodging."



"oh no," cry Leah, "for it be like stay with a friend."



every day Leah add something to the legend of Mendel, Mrs. Finch

urge she on with romance of her own splendid day. but the most

eager listener be Hetty, the girl who do the rough work of the house

and be never properly dress until the evening, because, from the

moment when she wake up in the morning until after supper, she be

keep run hither and thither at Mrs. Finch's command. she be

sufficiently like Mrs. Finch to justify Golda in her supposition that

she be that fine woman's daughter, but nothing be ever say in the

matter. Hetty do not have her meal with they, and, indeed, there be

no evidence that she have any meal. in the evening she be allow to

go out, and she would come back at half-past ten or so with her big

eye shine and a flush fading from her cheek and leave they

whiter than ever. very big be her eye, very big and pathetic, and

her face be a perfect oval. she have rather full lip, always moist

and red. during the whole fortnight she never speak a word except to

Issy. indeed, she avoid Golda and Leah, and she alarm Issy by what

he take to be her forwardness, when she ask he to take she to the

theatre. he comply with her request, but he be much too frightened

of she to speak, and he could think of nothing to say except to offer

to buy her chocolate and cigarette, which she accept as though it

be the natural thing for he to give her present. she talk to he

about Mendel, and want to know if it be true that he know lord and

have real gentleman to tea with he in his studio.



"there's more go on in his studio than I could tell you," say issy

with a dry, uncomfortable laugh. "artist, you know!"



"oh yes! artist!" say Hetty with a dreamy, wistful look in her eye

as she draw in her low lip with a slight suck noise. "I wish I

live in London, I do. Ma use to live in London, but she's too old

now to find any one to take she back there. it's dull here. do your

brother ever come to Margate?"



"no," say Issy. "he'd go to Brighton if he go anywhere. I've get

another brother who's go to Paris."



"o-oh! Paris! be he rich too?"



"no."

"that's an awful girl."

"the street be so clean, so nice, and the air be so strong, and the

word and feeling run so high that Mendel, aghast, flee away to his

* * * * *



a few day later Issy and Rosa be marry, without ceremony, without

carriage, or photograph, or guest, or feast. it be a wedding to be

a penny on it. the couple take half the house in the next street, and

conceal it either from she or from his family.



Mendel be profoundly depressed by this disturbance and plunge

and could take a happy, humorous interest in each other's doing

more for his attainment nor less for his doing, that pain she

deeply. and that direct human contact he obtain nowhere else, and in

no one else could he find it exist so openly and frankly. yet he

theatricality, and among his polite friend there be always some

drama towards. it be never drown in incoherent passion, and he

himself, among the nice cultured folk, be always a startling dramatic

figure. sometimes they seem to he all slyness and insincerity, and

then he loathe they; but that be generally when he have aim at and

fail in some dramatic coup, or when they have encourage he to talk

about himself until he bore they. on the whole, he be successful

with they, as he wish to be, easily and without calculation. it be

when they make calculation necessary, by feign an interest that

they do not feel, that he be shocked and angry. if anywhere the

atmosphere be such that he could not be frank, then he avoid that

place and those people.



now he be bore, bore to think of the hot stew month with no

relief except such as he could find in vagrom adventure from the

harsh rigidity of life among his own people. and he be in a strange

condition of physical lassitude. even his ambition be stagnant. in

his work he have only the pleasure of dexterity. it have no meaning, and

contain no delight. when he paint apple or a dead bird or a

woman, the result be just apple or a dead bird or a woman. the paint

make no difference and the subject be still well than his rendering

gradated sky in a picture in the National Gallery, he practise

gradated sky until he could have do they in his sleep.



and he be tired, tired in body and in soul. both in his life and in

his work he have have to conquer a convention in order to keep his

footing in the world of his desire. just as he have only learn the

Detmold style of draw by a supreme effort of will, so also by a

tremendous effort he have learn the rudiment of manner and polite

conversation. he have have to overcome his tendency to fall violently in

love with every charming person, male or female, he meet, and to regard

charm must for he be genuine and not a matter of trick, and for this

reason he have regard every person whom he think of as old with

dislike. for he anybody above twenty-five be "old." he still think

he would be make or mar by the time he be twenty-three, but that

age seem immeasurably far off. long before then, like a thunderbolt,

his full genius would descend upon he and all the world would know

his name. he be almost innocent of conceit in this. such, he

believe, be the history of genius, and so far nothing have happen

to deny his inward consciousness of his rarity. relieve the pressure

of circumstance and he soar upwards. . . . there be a queer,

uncomfortable pleasure in such thought and dream and in imagine a

fatality that should drag he down and down to Issy's level and low.

there be a sickening fascination in picture to himself a descent as

swift and irresistible as his upward flight. yet dreary be the hour

of wait for the impetus that have once or twice so freely and so

strongly move in he. sick with wait, he would work in a fury to

master trick after trick and difficulty after difficulty in painting,

so as to be ready when the time come. all the cunning and wariness of

his race well up in he as he prepare deliberately, slowly,

patiently for his opportunity.



* * * * *



one afternoon, as Golda be sleep in her kitchen, she be awaken

IX



the QUINTETTE



he have more of the deliciously grow-up sensation the next day, when

Hetty come to see he. she be something new. the girl of the street

his friend' sister, whom he call "top-knot," because of the way

they do their hair. the "top-knot" be hardly female at all to he,

so remote be they, so entirely unapproachable; utterly different

from the girl of the street, who be so accessible that he have but

to hold out his arm to find one of they, as if by magic, in his

grasp. and now Hetty be different again.



"you be cosy up here," she say, move at once to the only

comfortable chair and curl up in it. "your sister tell I about

you."



"Leah? what lie do she tell you?"



"well, I know it ben't _all_ true, about the money you be make,

because you wouldn't live here if it be true, would you? but I

suppose some of your friend make a lot of money."



"they're rich, some of they," reply Mendel, aghast to find himself

think coldly of his friend in term of money, his mind rush

swiftly between the two pole of his father and Sir Julius. "yes.

there's plenty of money in London."

Hetty be flatter he by regard he as the possessor of a key to

the wealth of London, and in spite of himself he could not help

life, though it be not all braggadocio, but also a kindly desire to

sound of his own voice, so different do it sound.

presently she say her hat make her head ache, and she take it off and

stretch out her arm and said:--



"no more pot and pan for I! I do think you're lovely. it's just

like a story. I call that real fun. not like Margate. . . . do you

think I could get work as a model, or do you have to be slap-up?"



Mendel think of the drab who pose and he could not help smile.



"I could only tell by your figure, though your face be all right."



"do you think I'm pretty?" she ask.



"very."



"I'll show you my figure, if you like."



"all right, I'll light the gas-stove in the bedroom. it's a little

cold in here."



he show she into the bedroom, and when she be ready she call to

he.

"a shilling an hour."



"do you pay that?"



"yes."



"if you could get I work I would sit to you for nothing."

themselves, but there be something about this girl that compel

she sit on the bed, make no move to cover herself, and said:--

he put the idea into my head. but he be very poor and not a

gentleman."



and now to Mendel she be an object of sheer astonishment. he stand

he get out a piece of mill-board and begin to draw she, but to his

I'm tired."

happiness! to be happy! that have seem impossible. his first year at

an artist."



"not all at once," Golda would say. "take a boy who be apprentice to

a bootmaker. he cannot all at once make good boot. he must spoil a

deal of leather first. or a tailor-boy: he must spoil cloth. a trade

must be learn, and you can learn this, for you work hard enough at

all the same, he be horrify at himself. every other crisis and

"no, I certainly shalln't draw you to-day."



"what about to-morrow?"



"I shall be paint to-morrow."



"I do think you're a devil sometimes."



"I'll take you to the Paris Café, if you like."

she perk up on that. she have not expect so soon to gain her

they grope their way down the dark stair, and he go out with she,

he too be thrill as he have never be before by London. he have

every touch be like a caress upon his ache soul.

"there's a Detmold girl live near here with her people--Greta

Morrison. you may remember she--glorious chestnut hair, big blue eye,

but as shy as a little mouse. I couldn't get a word out of she until I

beyond the flicker of delight he give no thought to the idea of Greta

lodge-house and her Ma, of whom he have a very clear idea. he use to

wax violent, and his imagination would run riot with the fantastic

vision of success he conjure up.



who be the "top-knot" that they should have an easy, pleasant time

in the country while he be leave to stew in London?



Hetty begin genuinely to admire he, and her flattery be no long

empty. there be some sustenance in it.



"o--oh!" she use to say. "you'll get on. there's no doubt about that.

you'll have a big stoodio and the nob will come up in their

"you're a liar," he would reply. "I shall always paint what I like. I

never do anything else, and never will. once paint for the fool and

you have to do it always, because you become a fool yourself."



* * * * *



Golda once meet Hetty come down the stair. she tell she she be a

dirty slut and be not to show her face inside the house again. a few

she inform Jacob of the danger to Mendel, and Jacob go up to the

"I will not have that harlot in my house," he say.



"she be not a harlot," reply Mendel rather shakily, for, though his

father's power have dwindle, yet he be still a figure of authority.



"she be a harlot and a daughter of a harlot, and I will not have she

in my house."

you again and again. I be allow money for model. I must have

model, just as you must have skin."



"then there be other model. I know this girl, what she be after, and

she will ruin you."



"neither she nor anyone else in the whole world could ruin I," say

Mendel, "for I be an artist, and while I have my art I ask nothing

outside it."

"he tell I a great deal about you, and he be really upset by your

letter."



"be he? be he?"



Mendel have no thought but for Mitchell. he long to go to he, to

embrace he, to tell he that all be different now. he blurt it all

"we be so happy, the four of we together. every evening we meet and

we be like king. everything that we want to do we do. we have

money and success and all such foolish thing, and we work hard, all

of we. there be not in London four young man like we, and I be free

of the terrible people who want to turn I into an ordinary

successful painter--a portrait painter. I tell you, I have never have a

commission in my life that be not a failure. I only want to be

young and to work, for I have never be young before. and then

suddenly, out of nothing, my friend turn on I and tell I I be a

Jew and uneducated, and ought to treat they with more respect. why?

the Jews be good people, and what do I want with education? can book

teach I how to paint? I tell you the Jews be good people."



Tea be bring up on a lacquer tray--bread, jam, and cake. they be

both hungry and fall to with a will, hardly speak at all.



when they have finish they begin to talk of picture and of the life

of the painter, and he tell her story of Michael Angelo and

in love in his life; and how Rembrandt be practically starve to

curly black together. gradually she lead he on to tell of his own

life, and he begin at the beginning in Austria, hold she

spell-bind with his vivid, picturesque talk.



"it make I feel very quiet and dull," she say. "I don't think I

ever regard place outside England as real, somehow. there be just

the train stop there, you know."



"where be your home?" he ask.



"in Sussex. it be very beautiful country."



"how do you come to the Detmold?"



"a girl at home have be there, and at school they say I be no good

at anything but drawing. indeed, I be send away from two school, and

at home I be such a trouble that mother decide I must do something

to earn my living. so I be send up to the Detmold. I have my hair down

my back then."



"I remember," say he. "in a plait."



she smile with pleasure at that.



"yes," she say. "in a plait. I live in a hostel, where they bully

I because I be so untidy and be always be late for meal. at

home, you know, there be only my brother, and my mother could never

keep they in order, and I be always treat as if I be a boy too.

. . . and I think that's all."



she end so lamely that his irritation get the well of he, and he

jump to his foot. it seem to he that his view of the "top-knot"

be confirm. they be simply negligible. he be baffle, and stand

compare the smooth monotony of her life with his, he wax

to he.



he begin to study her face with a view to paint it, and he be

absorb and fascinate by it. the line of her cheek and of her neck

make he itch to draw they.



"yes," he say, "I must paint you. I can do something good. I'm sure I

can."



"I want to ask if you would mind my painting you," she say.



he be aghast at her impudence. she, a slip of a girl, a "top-knot,"

paint the great kühler!



she see how horrified he be and add hastily:--



"of course, I willn't insist if you don't like sit."



she rise to go and he beg she to stay.



"don't go yet," he say with sudden emotion. "I don't want you to go.

somehow I feel as if you have be sit there always and I don't

want you to go. if you don't want to talk you needn't, but you must

stay. I could see that my mother like you at once, and she always

know good people. you make she happy about I. it be like sunshine

to she when you come in, and I shall be wretched if you go, for I

don't know what to think about you."



"I know what I think about you."



"what do you think?"



"you have make I feel that London ben't just a place where the train

stop."



and she begin to tell he about her home and the river where she

bathe with her brother, the wood where in spring there be

primrose and daffodil, and in summer bluebell.



"Opposite the house," she say, "be a hill which be a common, all

cover with gorse in the summer, and the hot, nutty smell of it come

up and seem to burn your face. there be snake on the common--viper

and adder and grass-snake. from the top you can see the down, and

beyond they, you know, be the sea. on moonlight night it be glorious,

and I nearly go mad sometimes with run in and out of the shadow.

I believe I do go mad once, for I sit up on the top of the hill and

sing and shout and cry, all by myself, and I feel that my heart

would break if I do not kiss something. the gorse be out, and I

bury my face in the dewy yellow flower. . . . I often think the

wood be like church on Easter Day. . . . and then when I get home

and it be just a house and I be just a girl live in it, you know, it

all seem wrong somehow."



Mendel sit on the floor try to puzzle out this mysterious rapture

of her. he have never hear of gorse or of down, but he could

recognize her emotion. he have have something like it the first time he

see a may-tree in blossom, and he have hardly be able to bear it. he

have rather resent it, for it have interfere with his work for a day

or two, and he could not help feel that there be something

indecent about an emotion with which he could do nothing.



"yes," he say heavily; "it must be very pretty."



she shiver at the grotesqueness of his word as she sink back into

her normal mood of happy diffidence. his face wear an expression of

black anger as he dart quick, furious glance at she. here be

something that he do not understand, something that defy his

mastery, and when she smile he think it be at himself, and this

strange power that have be behind she appear to he as a mocking,

teasing spirit. let it mock, let it tease! he be strong enough to

defy it. sweep through a green girl it might, but he be not to be

catch by it. he know well. in he it have tough simplicity to deal

with and a will that have break the confinement of Fate, the limit of

a meagre religion, to bend before no authority but that of art. . . .

he be rather contemptuous, too. nothing as yet have resist his

genius, and he feel it within he strong than ever, a river with a

thousand source. block one channel and it would find another. stop

that and it would find yet another.



yet here he know be no direct, no open menace, only the intolerable

suggestion that there be other stream, other source, and the

suggestion have come from this foolish, empty girl.



"I will not have it," he say half aloud.



"what do you say?" she ask.



"nothing. I be think--I be think that there be nothing so good

as London. they try to send I to Italy, but I know that there be

nothing so good as London for life, and where life be, there be art. I

don't want your pretty place and your pretty feeling. I want to go

through the street and to see the girl in the evening leave the

shop, and the man in their bowler-hat look at the girl and

want they, and the fat man in their motor-car, and the bookstall

on the railway station, and the public-house with their row of

bottle and the white handle of the beer machine, and the plump

barmaid, and the long, straight street go on for ever with the

flat house on either side of they, and the market and the

timber-yard and the tall chimney. it all fill your mind and make

pattern and whirling thought that take a spiral shape, go up and

up to mysterious height. I want all that, and nothing shall take it

from I, do you hear?"



he turn on she ferociously, as though she be try to rob he.



"and inside it all be something solid," he go on. "do you know that

my father never love but one woman in all his life? that's what Jews

be. they know what's solid. if they have to stay in the filth to keep

it, then they'll stay in the filth. and because I'm a Jew I'm not to

be catch with your pretty thing and your little fancy. I shall

paint the thing I understand, and I'll leave the cloud and the

rainbow and the roof and chimney to fool like Mitchell."



Morrison sit very meekly while he talk. she hang her head and

twitch her finger nervously. she be elate by his passion, but she

too have her dream and be not go to surrender they. his strength

have give her confidence in they and in herself, and she be fill

with a teasing spirit.



"Jews ben't the only people who be solid," she say. "you see man in

bus and train whom an earthquake wouldn't move, and I'm sure, if an

earthquake happen, my mother would be leave where she be, read

the Bible."



Mendel replied:--



"in a thousand year my mother will be just as she be now."



Morrison stare at he and begin to wonder if he be not a little mad.

he add simply:--



"I feel like that."



and she be relieve and think he be the only sane person she have

ever meet in her life.



"will you let I come again?" she ask.



"I be go to paint you," he say; "I be go to paint you as you

be. you willn't like it."



"I shall if you make I solid," she answer. "and you need flower in

this dark room. you must let I send you some."

BOHEMIA







I



the POT-AU-FEU



AT the exhibition, the portrait of Golda create no small stir. the

critic, who, since Whistler, have be chary of denounce new-comer,

have swing to the opposite extravagance and be excessively eager to

discover new master. the youth of this Kühler make he fair game, for

it supply they with a proviso. they could hail his talent as that of

a prodigy without commit themselves.



"the portrait of the artist's mother," write one of they, "have all the

essential of great art, as the early composition of Mozart have all

the essential of great music. here be real achievement, a work of art

instinct with racial feeling, and therefore of true originality. no

trace here of parisian experiment. this picture be in the direct line

from Holbein and Dürer."



Mendel take this to mean that he be as good as Holbein and Dürer, and

accept it not as praise but as a statement of fact. the picture be

buy by a well-know connoisseur, who write that he be proud to

have such a picture in his collection.



"now," think the proud painter, "my career have really begin."



for once in a way he regard his success with his father's eye and

Love? they call it love when a young man clasp a maiden's hand, or

when they kiss and ride together on the top of bus! these

Christians be rather disgusting with all their talk of love. he have

hear more talk of it in three year of contact with they than in all

Disgusting!



and now here be Mitchell babbling of his love for Morrison. when

have time for they."



"Gawd!" say Mitchell, trot along by his side; "you don't know

"it's nonsense!" shout Mendel; "nonsense, I tell you. it must be

they have be read Tolstoi on "what be art?" and their young

II



LOGAN



once again Mendel decide that Mitchell, and with he London life, have

fall away from he. the Paris Café could never be the same again,

and he plunge into despair, and think seriously of accept a

jewish girl with four hundred pound whom a match-maker offer to

he. four hundred pound be not to be sneeze at. it would keep he

go for some year, so that he need not think of sell his

picture, which he always hate to part with. and the girl be just

bearable.



the figure delight his father and mother, for it show they the

high opinion of their wonder-son hold among their own people.



it be terrible to he to find that he have very little pleasure in his

work, which very often give he excruciating pain. he take it to mean

that he be come to an end of his talent. night after night he sit

on his bed feel that he must make an end of his life, but always

there be some piece of painting that he must do in the morning,

painful though it might be.



he have letter from Mitchell, but do not answer they, and at last

"the schoolboy," as Golda call he, turn up, gay and smile and

rather elated.



"I've discover a great man," he say with the awkward, jerky gesture

he use in his more eloquent moment. "absolutely a great man. remind

I of Napoleon. wonderful head, wonderful! his name be Logan--James

Logan--and he want to know you. he be a painter, and absolutely

independent. he come from the North--Liverpool or one of those

place. I haven't see his work, but I meet he at the Pot-au-feu the

other night. he ask I if I be not a friend of your, as he think

he have see I with you. he say: 'kühler be the only painter of

genius we have.' I spend the evening with he. I never hear such

talk. it make the old Detmold seem like a girl' school. . . . Hallo!

still-life again? what a rum old stick you be for never go outside

your four wall!"



"what I paint be inside I, not outside," say Mendel, tremble with

rage at Mitchell look at his work before he have offer to show it.



"will you come and see Logan?"



"no. I be sick of painter. I want to know decent people."



"but I promise I would bring you, and he admire your work. he be

poor too, as poor as you be."



"cann't he sell?"



"it ben't that so much as that he don't try. he say he have almost

despair of english painting until he see your work."



"how old be he?"



"a good deal old than we. twenty-six, I should think."



"why don't you just stick to I?" ask Mendel. "what more do you

want? why must you always go off on a new track? first it's Hetty

Finch, then it's Morrison, and now it's this new man. we be happy

enough by ourselves. why do you want anything more? I don't."



"you're use to live on dry bread. I'm not. I want butter with mine,

and jam, if I can get it."



"then get it and don't bother I to go chase after it. I want to

work."



"oh, rot! all that stuff about artist starve in garret be out of

date. it only happen because they couldn't find patron, but

nowadays there be dealer and buyer. . . . just look at the money

you be make."



"then why be this Logan poor?"



"he ben't know yet. he don't know the artist because he never go

to a London school. he be do quite well in the North, but throw it

all up because he couldn't stand live in such a filthy town. he have

a teaching job somewhere in Hammersmith, but he throw that up because

he want his time to himself."



"that sound as if painting mean something to he."



"do come and see he."



"oh! very well."



"I'll send he a wire and we'll go to-night."



they dine at the Pot-au-Feu, and later make the expedition to

Hammersmith, where they come to a block of studio surround by a

scrubby garden. these studio be large and well-keep and do not

tally with the description of Logan's poverty. still less do the

inside give any sign of it. there be a huge red-brick fireplace,

surmount by old brass and blue china, with great arm-chair on

either side of it: there be persian rug on the floor; two little

window be fill in with good stain glass, which Mendel know to

be costly; there be two or three large easel; and the wall be

hang with tapestry. the whole effect be deliberately and preciously

rich.



Logan, who have admit they to this vast apartment, rush back at

once to a very large easel on which he have a very small canvas, and

fall to work on it with a furious energy, dart to and fro and

stamp his right foot rather like the big trumpet man in a german

band. he be a medium-sized, plumpish man, with a big, strongly

feature face, big chin, and compress lip, and long black hair

brush back from a round, well-shape brow. he frown and scowl at

his work. a woman come out of a door and cross the studio behind

he. he hurl his palette into the air so that it sail up and fall

with a crash among the brass pot, and barked:--



"how can I work with these constant interruption? damn it all, an

artist must have peace!"



he fling his arm behind his back and pace moodily to and fro, with

his head down and his lip purse up _à la_ beethoven. he extend the

sphere of his pacing gradually so that he come near and near to

Mendel, yet without notice he. Mendel be tremendously excited and

impressed with the man's air of mystery and force. it be like

Calthrop, but without his awkwardness. Mitchell in comparison look

puny and absurdly young.



Nearer and near come Logan, and at last he stop and fix Mendel

with a baleful stare, and swing his head up and down three time.



"so you be Kühler?" he say.



Mendel open his lip, but to his astonishment no sound come out of

they. so desperately anxious be he not to cut a poor figure before

this remarkable man, and not to seem, like Mitchell, pathetically

young.



"good!" say Logan. "shake hand." and he crush Mendel's thin

finger together. "what I like about you," he go on, "be your sense

of form. design be all very well in its way, but quite worthless

without form."



Mendel, whose work be still three part instinctive, could not attach

any precise meaning to these expression, but he be well up in the

jargon of his craft and could make a good show.



"art," say Logan, "be an exact mistress. shall we go and have a

drink?"



he put on his hat and lead the two marvel youngster to a

public-house, where he become a different man altogether. the

compression of his lip relax, his eye twinkle and his face shine

with good humour, and he make they and the barmaid and the two or

three man who be shyly take their beer roar with laughter. he have

an extraordinary gift of mimicry, and tell story after story, many of

they against himself, most of they without point, but in the telling

exceedingly comic. Mendel sit up and bristle. it be to he half

shocking, half enviable, that a man, and an artist, should be able to

laugh at himself.



"if you'll give I free drink for a month," say Logan to the elderly

barmaid, "I'll paint your portrait. be you marry? . . . no? I'll

paint you such a beautiful portrait that it will get you a husband

inside a week."



"I'm not on the marrying lay," say the barmaid.



"terrible thing, this revolt against marriage," reply Logan, "and

bad luck on us artist. I'm always get baby leave on my

doorstep."



"what do you do with they?" say Mendel, believe he, and astonish

when the other roar with laughter.



"I keep the pretty one and sell they to childless mother. ah! Many's

the time I've go through the snow, like the heroine in a melodrama

take her child to the workhouse."



"oh! go on," titter the barmaid.



"certainly," say Logan. "come along."



as they leave the public-house he take Mendel's arm and said:--



"you have to talk to people in their own language, you know."



"yes," reply Mendel, though this be precisely what he know least of

all.



"why don't you go on the stage?" ask Mitchell.



"I have think of it. I think I might do well on the hall. there's a

life for you! on at eight in bethnal Green:--



    my old woman's get a wart on her nose;

    how she get it I will now disclose.



off again in a motor-car to the Oxford:--



    my old woman's get a wart on her nose.



off again to Hammersmith or Kensal Rise:--



    my old woman's get a wart on her nose.



my God! what a life! but I love the hall. they be all that be leave

of old England!"



his parody of the low comedian be so apt and his voice have such a

delicious roll that Mendel could not help laugh, and he begin to

feel very happy with the man.



Logan swing back to his serious mood and grip Mendel's arm tight

as he said:--



"you have a big future before you. only stick to it. don't listen to

the fool who want you to paint the same picture over and over again

with a different subject. there's more stuff in that one little

picture of your than in all the rest of the exhibition put together."



"do you think so?" say Mendel, flutter with excitement.



"I be amazed when I hear you have be to the Detmold with its

Calthrop and all the little Calthrops."



both the youngster be silent on that. they have often abuse the

Detmold, but with a profound respect in their heart, and both have

do their full share of imitate Calthrop.



when they reach the studio Mitchell suggest go, but Logan would

not hear of it. he drag they in and produce whisky and soda, and

keep they talk far into the small hour. his bounce energy keep

Mendel awake and alert, but Mitchell be soon exhausted and fall

asleep.



"shall we put he out of the way?" say Logan suddenly. "no one would

know, and the river be handy. he be too clean, too soft, and there be

too many like he. they be in the way of real man like you and I."



Mendel be appal to find that he could not defend his friend. all

the discontent of his wane friendship come rush up in he and he

begin to babble violently.



"he be a liar and a coward, and he will never be an artist because he

be too weak. he be not true. he be not good. I have trust he with

my secret and he tell. he be always ashamed of I because of my

clothe and because I have not be to Public School, and he be

jealous because when we meet woman they like I. he be soft and

deceitful with they, but I be honest, and they like that. I want he

to be my friend, but it be impossible."



"he be an Englishman," say Logan sepulchrally, with the air of a

Grand Inquisitor.



"ben't you an Englishman?"



"no, Scotch and French. these englishman have no passion, unless they

be mad like Blake. . . . no, no. we'll drop Mitchell overboard. we'll

make he walk the plank, and fish in the cavern of the sea shall

eat his eye."



Logan be begin to assume enormous proportion in Mendel's eye.

it seem that there be nothing the tremendous fellow do not know.

he begin to talk of genius and the stirring of the creative impulse,

and he give so powerful an account of Blake that Mendel begin to see

vision of heaven and hell. here be something which he could

acknowledge as large than himself without self-humiliation, and,

indeed, the large it loom the more swiftly do he himself seem to

grow. it be such a sensation as he have not know since the day

before his rapture with Sara. all that have intervene fall away. that

purity of passion return to he and, choose Logan for its object,

rush upon he and endow he with its own power and beauty. Logan

talk of Blake be to Mendel's innocence as rare as Blake, and he

adore he.



"I have almost give up art," say Logan; "I have almost give it up as

hopeless. how can there be art in a despiritualized country like this,

that let all its tradition rot away? I be just on the point of

toss up whether I should go on the stage or take to spout at the

street corner; for when a country be in such a condition that its

artist be stifle, then it be ripe for revolution. I be instinctive,

you know, like Napoleon. I feel that we be on the threshold of

something big, and that I be to have my share in it. I use to think

it would happen in art, but I despair of that. it seem to I that

art in this country could go doddering on for generation, and then I

think it need a political upheaval to push it into its grave. but

when I see your work, I say to myself: here be the real thing, alive,

personal, profound, skilled. I begin to hope again. and now that I

have meet you I feel more hopeful still, and, let I tell you, like

most painter, I don't find it easy to like another man's work."



Mendel be fire. tremble in every limb, he said:--



"it have be the dream of my life to find a friend who would work with

I, think with I, go with I, share with I, not quarrel with I

because I be not this, that, and the other, but accept I as I

be--a man who have no country, no home, no love but art."



"that," say Logan, with a portentous scowl and a downward jab of his

thumb, "be what I have be look for--some one, like yourself, who

be absolutely sincere, absolutely single-minded and resolute. the

spirit of art have bring we together. we will serve it together."



they shake hand like young man on the stage, and Logan fetch a deep

sigh of relief.



Mitchell wake up, saying:--



"Gawd! I've be asleep. have you two be talk? Gawd! it's two

o'clock."



"I'll walk home with you," say Logan. "we can keep to the river

nearly the whole way by go from side to side."



so they walk while the tide come up, suck and lapping, while the

red dragon' eye of the barge come swinge up on it, move up and

down in a slow, irregular rhythm. it be very cold and the sky be

thickly powder with star, whose pin-prick light be reflect in

the smooth water.



upon the dome of the young artist's vision that have before be black

with infinite space, star shine with a tender light. he be in

ecstasy, and seem to be skim above the ground, hardly touch

it with his foot. this long walk be like an exquisite dance, while

Logan's rolling be like a pipe. . . . often he sink into a dream

that he be upon a grassy hill in a mountainy place, he and his

friend, who play upon a pipe so mournfully yet gaily while he

dance, and from the tree fall silvery dewdrop and the song of

bird, which turn into penny as they reach the ground and roll

away down the hill.



both he and Logan be relieve when Mitchell, who have interrupt

they with inappropriate remark, turn aside at Vauxhall and vanish

into London.



"so much for Mitchell," say Logan. "you and I need sterner stuff. you

and I be spring from those among whom life be live bravely and

bitterly, and we have no use for its parasite. you and I will only

emerge from the bitterness on condition that we can make of life a

spiritual thing, for we be of those who seek authority. life have none

to offer we now, for all the form of life be break. neither above

we nor below be there authority, neither in heaven nor in hell. we

must seek authority within ourselves, in the marriage of heaven and

hell, in the consummation of good and evil, the two pole of our

nature. it be for we, the artist, to bring they together, to liberate

good and evil in ourselves, that they may rush to the consummation. we

be the priest and the prophet, and we must in no wise be false to

our vision."



Mendel could not fit all this in with his mood and his delicious

dream, and when it bring he back to his sober sense, he could not

see what it have to do with painting. however, Logan put thing right

by saying:--



"you be a poet. you be like Heine. I can see you with your little

Josepha the pale, the executioner's daughter. God rot my soul! it be

year since I have such inspiration as you have give I. I think there

must be jewish blood in I, for I can certainly understand you through

and through, and you have wake something in I that have always be

asleep. oh! we shall paint bonny picture--bonny, bonny picture."



"you must come to see I every day," say Mendel, "and every night we

will go out together, and I must introduce you to my mother, for she

too have good word."



Logan smack his lip as they enter the grimy street near

Spitalfields.



"Pah!" he say; "that's life, that is, good dirty life. I be litter

in a farm-yard myself and I like a good smell. . . . can you put I up

to-night? I don't mind sleep on the floor."



"you can have my bed," say Mendel, "and I will sleep downstairs on my

brother's sofa. please--please. do sleep in my bed."



Logan accept the offer and ask Mendel to stay with he while he

undress. he be unpleasantly fat, but strong and well-build.



he stay for a long time in front of the mirror.



"see that bulge on the side of my head?" he say as he turn.



Mendel look, and sure enough his head have a curious bulge on its

right side.



"I have ricket when I be young," say Logan, "and my skull must have

got push over. I expect that's what make I what I be--lop-sided. I

need you to balance I."



he get into bed, and Mendel, reluctant to leave he, sit at his foot

and devour he with his eye.



"surely, surely, now," he think, "all be perfect now. no more

disturbance, no more Mitchells, no more Hettys, and I shall do only

what I really wish to do."



he steal out into his studio, which be faintly light from the street

below, and it be as though it be fill with some vast spiritual

presence, and he imagine how he would work, urge on by this new

energy that come well up through all that he could see, all that he

could know, all that he could remember.







III



LOGAN sets to work



in the morning he be awaken by his sister-in-law, Rosa, shake he

and saying:--



"Mendel! Mendel! what be you do on the sofa? wake up! wake up!

there be some one in your studio."



the house be ring with Logan's voice chant the _Magnificat._

Mendel run upstairs and find he in bed with a box of cigarette and

the New Testament, that fatal book, on his knee.



"hello!" he say. "I hope I don't wake you up. I have be awake for

a couple of hour look at your work. I hope you don't mind. there's

a still-life there that's a gem, as good as Chardin, and even well,

for there's always something sentimental about Chardin--always the

suggestion of the old folk at home, the false dramatic touch, the

idea of the hard-work french peasant come in presently to eat the

bread and drink the wine. I think it's time you be write up in the
