paper. it's absurd for a man like you to have to wait for success.

there's no artistic public in England, so you cann't be successful in

your own way. the british public must have its touch of melodrama. to

accept a man's work it must first have he shroud in legend. he must

be a myth. his work must seem to come from some supernatural source."



"I'll just run over and tell my mother you be here," say Mendel. "I

always have breakfast there, and then go for a walk while the studio

be dust."



"right you be! I'll be up in half a jiffy. can I have a bath?"



"no. there's no bath."



"very well; I can do without for once."



Mendel run round to Golda and tell she of the wonderful man who be in

his studio, and he describe the adventure of the previous evening.

Golda look scared and said:--



"what next? what next? good people sleep in their own bed."



"but this man be an artist and he talk like a book."



"talk be easy," say Golda. "but it take year to make a friend."



however, when Logan be bring to she she be polite to he and

rather shy. he tell she that fame be come to her son fast than

the wind.



"too fast," say she.



"it can never come too fast," reply Logan. "the thirst for fame be a

curse to an artist. let it be satisfied and he be free for his work. I

know, for I be very famous in my own town. I sicken of it and run

away. . . . I must congratulate you on let your son follow his

bent. I have to quarrel with my own people to get my way. I haven't

see they since I be fourteen."



"not your mother?" say Golda, greatly upset.



Logan see that he have make an awkward impression and hasten to put

it right by say lugubriously:--



"my mother be dead. she forgive I."



he allow that to sink in and be silent for a minute or two. then he

chatter on gaily and ask Golda to come and see he, and brag

about his studio and his work and his friend, and of a commission he

have to decorate a large house in a West end square. he talk so fast

that Golda understand very little of what he say, but she never take

her eye off he, and when he say good-bye, Mendel notice that she

do not bob to he as she do to Mitchell and Morrison and his other

polite friend. he take that to mean that she accept Logan as a

person above these formality.



for an hour they walk through the street and square of the East

End, Mendel proud to display the vivid scene he intend later on to

make into picture.



when they return to the studio Logan insist on see all the

picture and drawing again.



"be you in touch with any dealer?" he ask.



"Cluny have a few picture and a dozen drawing. he never do anything

with they."



"Hum!" say Logan. "dealer be mysterious people. they can only sell

thing that sell themselves. by the way, I be give up my studio in

Hammersmith. it be too far away. I shall come near in. Hammersmith

be all very well while I need isolation, but that be all over now."



"where shall you go to?"



"Bloomsbury, I think. I like to be near the British Museum. do you go

to the British Museum? I must show you round. it be no good go

there unless you know what to look for. by the way, I come out without

any money last night. can you lend I five pound?"



Mendel write a cheque and hand it to he shamefacedly.



"I want to pay a bill on my way home," say Logan. "I hate be in

debt, especially for colour."



"I get my colour from Cluny," say Mendel, "and he set they against

anything he may sell."



the irruption of money have depress he, and he begin to realize that

he be very tired. the spring of Rosa's sofa have bore into he and

prevent his get any real sleep.



he be not sorry when Logan go, after make he promise to meet he

at the Pot-au-feu for dinner.



* * * * *



he have a model come at eleven, but when she arrive he send she

away. he be sore and dissatisfied. the studio seem dark and dismal,

and he could not get enough light on to his work. he take it right up

to the window, but still there be not enough light, and his picture

look dull and dingy. his nerve throb and he be trouble in

spirit, for now his old dream of paint quietly among his own

people while fame gather about his name have suddenly become childish

and pathetic. he be ignorant, futile, conceited, a pigmy by the side

of the gigantic Logan, who would not wait upon the world, but would

compel its attention and shape it to his will. what have he say

artist be? priest and prophet? . . . how could a man prophesy

with a painting of a fish?



Downstairs he hear Issy come in for his dinner, and there be the

usual snarling row because Rosa cook so vilely. Mendel compare

Issy's life and his own: issy working day in, day out, earn just

enough to keep himself alive. why do he go on with it? why do he

keep himself alive? why do he not clear out, like Harry? there be no

pleasure in his life, neither the time nor the money for it. . . . a

wretched business.



but be it less wretched than this business of painting? there be

more money in painting, and that be all anybody seem to think of.

People want the same picture over and over again, and if he

consent to please they, his life would be just as poor a thing as

Issy's, except that he would have pleasure, and, through his friend,

an occasional taste of luxury. at good he could be polite and

gentlemanly, like Mitchell, bring no more to art and get no

more out of it than a boyish excitement, as though art be a game and

could give no more than a sensation of cleanliness, like a hot bath.



no, it would not do. it would not do.



it be a lie, too, to say that the Jews only care about money. when

they be overfe, like Maurice Birnbaum, they be like all the other

overfed people, but when they be simple and normal they be well

than the other, because they have always a sense of mystery and do

not waste themselves in foolish laughter.



that be where Logan be true. he could laugh, because all the

Christians laugh, but when it come to solemn thing he could talk

about they as though he be not half ashamed. Mitchell, for instance,

always shy away from the truth. why be he afraid of it? the truth,

good or bad, be always somehow beautiful, invigorate, and

release. all the pleasant thing that Mitchell care about Mendel

find stifle. nothing, he know, could make life altogether pleasant,

and all the falsehood which be use in that attempt be

contemptible. they strangle impulse and frankness, and without these

how could there be art?



in his unhappy dream Logan appear like a figure of Blake, immense,

loom prophetic, beckon to achievement and away from the chatter

and fuss of the world of artist.



yet behind Logan there be still the figure of Mitchell, young and

gay, and the idea of Mitchell lead to the idea of Morrison.



there be some wither flower on his painting-table, the last she

have send he. none have come since that evening in the Paris Café when

she have nod curtly to show he that he be not want.



he would not be thrust aside like that. he know himself to be worth a

thousand Mitchells. Logan have say that Mitchell be rubbish, and not

even in the eye of a slip of a girl would Mendel have Mitchell set

above himself. not for one moment be it tolerable. he would keep

Morrison to her promise and make she come to have her portrait

paint, and he would find out what there be in she that make he

remember she so distinctly and so clearly separate she from all other

girl. somehow the thought of she cool the intoxication in which he

have be leave by Logan. she offer, perhaps, another way out of his

present state of congestion and dissatisfaction. very clearly she

bring back to his mind the thrilling delight with which he have

work as a boy, and that be true, truer than anything else he have

ever know. . . . ah! if he could only get back to that, with all the

trick and cunning he have learn.



he would get back to it some day, but he must fight for it; with Logan

he would learn how to fight. Logan would lay his immense store of

knowledge before he, and give he book to read, and teach he how to

be so easy and familiar with idea, which at present only froth in

his mind like wave thin themselves out on the sea-shore.



he write an impassioned and insolent letter to Morrison command her

presence at his studio and inform she that he be worth a thousand

of her ordinary associate, and that she have hurt he, and that girl

ought not to hurt man of acknowledge talent. this letter cost he a

great deal of pain and time, because he be careful not to make any

slip in spelling or grammar. it be more a manifesto than a letter,

and he wish to do nothing to impair its dignity.



and all the time he be puzzle to know why he should care about she

at all. he be prepared to throw everything--his success, the Detmold,

his friend--to the wind to follow Logan, but Morrison he could not

throw away.



he decide at last not to send the letter but to go himself, and he

go to the Detmold just as the light be fade and he know she would

be leave.



she have go already, but he meet Clowes, who, he know, live with she.

he pounce on she and said:--



"you must come to tea with I."



"I'm afraid I . . ."



"you must! you must!"



she see he be very excited and she have hear story of his burst

into tear when he be thwart. in some alarm she consent to go

with he.



he lead she to a teashop, a horrible place that smell of dishwater and

melt butter, make she sit at a table, and burst at once into a

tirade:--



"you be Morrison's friend. will you tell I why she have avoid I?

she come to my studio once and she say she would come again. she send

I flower for three week, but she have send no more."



"she--she be very forgetful," say Clowes, who be long for tea but

do not dare to tell he to turn to the waitress, who be hover

behind he.



"but she nod to I as if she have hardly meet I before," say

Mendel.



"she be very shy," say clowe, frame the word "tea" with her lip

and nod brightly to the waitress. she add kindly:--



"I don't think send flower mean much with she. she give flower

to heap of people. she be a very odd girl."



"do she give flower to Mitchell?" he ask furiously, come at

last with great relief to the consume thought in his mind.



"yes," say clowe. "she be very unhappy about Mitchell and that Hetty

Finch affair."



"have he tell she then?"



"yes."



"why do he tell she?"



"I'm sure I don't know."



"I'll tell you," cry Mendel. "I'll tell you. to make himself

interesting to she, because he be not interesting. he be nothing. and

I will tell you something more. he have be tell her thing about

I to excuse himself. now, haven't he? . . . I can see by your face

that he have."



clowe could not deny it, and she find it hard to conceal her

distress. she be unused to intimate affair be drag out into

the open like this, and her modesty be shock. she have a pretty,

intelligent face, and she look for the moment like a startled hare,

the more so when she put her handkerchief up to her nose with a

gesture like that of a hare brush its whisker.



"very well, then," Mendel continue; "you can tell she you have see

I, and you can tell she that I shall come to explain myself. I hide

nothing, for I be ashamed of nothing that I do. I have no need to

excuse myself. I be not a gentleman one moment and a cad the next. and

you can tell Morrison that if I see she with Mitchell again I shall

knock he down."



"do please drink your tea," say clowe. "it be get cold."



Mendel gulp down his tea and hasten to add:--



"I be not boast. he be big than I be, but I know something about

boxing. my brother be nearly a prizefighter."



clowe begin to recover from her alarm, and his immense seriousness

strike she as very comic.



"do you know that Greta have cut her hair short?"



"her hair?" cry Mendel. "her beautiful hair?"



"yes. she look so sweet, but the boy call after she in the street.

all the girl be wild to do it."



"her hair? her beautiful hair? why?"



"oh! she get sick of put it up. she be like that. she suddenly

do something you don't expect."



"but she must look terrible!"



"oh no. she look too sweet. and if all the boy at the Detmold wear

their hair long, I don't see why the girl shouldn't wear theirs

short."



"my mother have her head shave when she marry," say he, "and she

wear a wig."



"why do she do that?"



"it be the custom. the woman show that she belong wholly to her

husband and make herself unattractive to all other man."



"what a horrible idea!"



"it be a beautiful idea. it be the idea of love independent of

everything else. that be why I think Morrison must have some reason

for cut her hair."



"when you know Greta, you will know that she don't wait for

reason."



"why do she like Mitchell?"



"she like nearly everybody."



"but she write to he."



"of course she do," say clowe, rather bored with his persistence.



"but she don't write to I."



"you don't write to she. you cann't expect she to fall at your foot."



as she say this Clowes realize his extraordinary Orientalism. she

could see he hold up his finger and expect a woman to come at

his bidding, and for a moment she be repel by he. but she be a

kind-hearted creature and feel very sorry for he, for he seem so

utterly at sea and be obviously full of genuine and painful emotion.



he detect her repulsion at once and perceive the effort she make to

conquer it, and be at once grateful to she, for, as a rule, when that

happen, people let it swamp everything else.



she said:--



"I'll tell Greta what you have say to I, and I be sure she will be

very sorry to have hurt you."



"I only want she to come and sit for her portrait. it be very

important to I, because I want to try new subject and there be some

lovely drawing in her face."



"but you mustn't knock Mitchell down. he be quite a nice boy, really,

only a little wild."



"he be rotten," say Mendel dogmatically.



* * * * *



he feel well, and until dinner-time he prowl about Tottenham Court

Road and Soho, a region of London that he particularly love--a

vibrant, nondescript region where innumerable stream of vitality meet

and fuse, or clash together to make a froth and a spume. it be

like himself, chaotic and rawly alive, compound of element that

know no tradition or have escape from it. he feel at home in it, and

elated because he be also conscious of be superior to it, yet

without the dizzy sense of superiority that assail he among his own

people, while he be never shock and humiliate, as he be sometimes

in sedate and prosperous London, by be make suddenly to realize his

external inferiority. he love the shop-girl hurry excitedly from

their work to their pleasure, and he sometimes speak to they in their

own slang, sometimes go home with they. . . . they always like he

because he never waste time over silly flirtatious joke or pretend

to be in love with they. his interest and curiosity, like theirs, be

purely physical, and his passion give they a delicious sense of

danger.



* * * * *



Logan be wait for he at the Pot-au-feu. there be no one else in

the restaurant but the goggle-eyed man in his corner. Logan be

sit Napoleonically with his arm on the table and his chin sink on

his chest, with his lip compress.



he nod, but do not get up.



"sorry if I'm late," say Mendel. "I go for a walk. I couldn't work

to-day. my sister-in-law's sofa--I feel as if I have be beat all

over."



"that's the walk home," say Logan. "I'm use to it. the hour I've

spend walk about this infernal London! I've sleep on the

Embankment, you know."



"no?"



"yes. I've be as far down as that, though I'm not the sort of man

who can be keep down. do you know that Napoleon be out-at-elbow for

a whole year?"



"no; I don't know much about Napoleon."



"ah! you should. I read every book about he I can lay hand on.

Gustave!"



the waiter come up and Logan order a very special dinner with the

air of know the very inmost secret of the establishment. he

demand orange bitter before the meal and a special brand of

cigarette.



"my day haven't be waste," he say. "I've be to Cluny's and I

ask to see your stuff. the little man there look astonished, but I

tell he people be talk of no one else but you, and quite

rightly. I talk to he from the dealer's point of view, and assure

he that there be a big boom in picture, come, and that he have

well be prepare for it with a handful of new man. I don't let he

know that I be a painter, but I get he quite excited, and I do not

leave he until he have hang a picture and two drawing."



"which picture?"



"the one of your mother's kitchen. it be one of your good. to-morrow

three man will walk into Cluny's and they will admire your work. on

the day after to-morrow a real buyer will walk in."



Mendel's eye grow large and large. be Logan a magician, that he

could direct human being into Cluny's shop and conduct they straight

to his work?



Logan laugh at his amazement.



"Lord love-a-duck!" he say, "you're not go to sit still and wait

for commercial fool to discover that you know your job. at my first

exhibition in Liverpool I put on a false beard and go in and buy

one of my own picture, just to encourage the dealer and the timid

idiot who be too shy to go and ask he the price of the drawing.

it work, and this be go to work too. when I've warm Cluny up

into sell you, then I'm go to make he sell I. if you don't

mind we'll have our name bracketed,--Kühler and Logan. People will

believe in two man when they willn't in one. as for three, you've only

get to look at the Trinity to see what they'll believe when they get

three work together. . . . oh! I forget you be a Jew and bring

up to believe in one be one and all alone."



he laugh and give a fat chuckle as he mimic the little man in

Cluny's cock his head on one side and pretend to take in the

beauty of Mendel's work as they be point out to he.



"I have enjoy myself," say Logan. "by God! I wish there be a

revolution. I'd have my finger in the pie. oh! what lovely leg

there'd be to pull--all the world's and his wife's as well. but it

willn't come in my time."



under Logan's influence Mendel begin to enjoy his food, which he have

always treat as a tiresome necessity before. he sit back in his

chair and sip his wine and crumble up his bread exactly as Logan

do; and he have a delicious sense of leisure and well-being, as though

nothing matter very much. and, indeed, when he come to think of it,

nothing do matter. he have year and year ahead of he, and here be

good solid pleasure in front of he, so that he have only to dip his

hand in it and take and take. . . .



after the dinner Logan order cigar, coffee, and liqueur, and

Mendel feel very lordly. the restaurant have fill up, and among the

rest be Mitchell and Morrison.



Mendel turn, give they a curt nod, and could not restrain a grin of

satisfaction as he think that score be settle. he lean forward

and give himself up to the pleasure of Logan's talk.



"what I contend," say Logan, "be this--and mind you, I let off my

youthful gas year ago. I've be earn my living since I be

fourteen, so I know a little of what the world's like. I've be in

office and shop, and on the land, in hotel, on the railway, on the

road as a bagman, from house to house as a tallyman, and I know what

I'm talk about. the artist be a free man, and therefore an outlaw,

because the world be full of timid slave who lie in the lap of

woman. if an artist be not a free man, then he be not an artist. and I

say that if the artist be outlaw, then he must use any and every

mean to get out of the world what it deny he. one must live."



"that's true," say Mendel.



"you may take it from I that there be less room in the world now for

artist than ever there be. in the old day you choose your patron and

he provide for you, as the Pope provide for Michael Angelo, and you

devote your art to whatever your patron stand for, spiritual power if

he happen to be a pope, secular power if he happen to be a duke or

a king. but, nowadays, suppose you have a patron--say, Sir Julius

Fleischmann--and he keep you alive, what on earth could you devote

your art to? you could paint his portrait, and his wife's portrait,

and all his daughter' portrait, but they'd mean nothing; they'd just

be vulgar man and woman. no. art be a big thing than any power leave

on the earth. money have eat up all the other power, and only art be

leave uncorrupted by it. art cannot be patronize. it cannot serve

religion, because there be no religion vital enough to contain the

spirit of art. there be nothing leave in the world worthy of such noble

service, and therefore art must be independent and artist must be

free, because there be no honourable service open to they. they must

have their own value, and they must have the courage of they. the

world's value be the value fit for the service of Sir Julius

Fleischmann, but they be not fit for man whose blood be stir with

life, whose mind be eager and active, man who will accept any

outward humiliation rather than the degradation of the loss of their

freedom."



"I meet Sir Julius Fleischmann. once," Mendel say. "he subscribe for

I when I go to my first School of Art. they want to send I to

Italy, but I refuse, because I know my place be here in London.

there's more art for I in the Tottenham Court Road than in all the

blue sky in the world."



"quite right, too!" cry Logan. "that show how sound an artist's

instinct be. he know what be good for he because he be a free man.

the other have to be tell what be good for they because they don't

know themselves and because, however unhappy they be, they don't know

the way out. when you and I be unhappy we know that it be because we

have lose touch with life, or because we have lose touch with art;

either the flesh or the spirit be choke with thorn, and we set about

pluck they out. when it be a question of save your soul, what do

moral matter?"



Mendel have hear people talk about moral, and he know that his own

be suppose to be bad; but he be not certain what they be. rather

timidly he ask Logan, who give his fat chuckle and replied:--



"moral, my son? no one know. they change about a hundred year after

human practice. they be different in different time, place, and

circumstance, and Sir Julius Fleischmann, like you and I, have none,

because he can afford to do without they. . . . well, I've do a good

day's work and we've have a good dinner, and I must get back to my

beautiful bed--unless you'd like to go to a music-hall."



Mendel be loath to let his friend go, and, weary though he be, he

say he would like the music-hall. Logan buy more cigar and they

walk round to the Oxford and spend the evening in uneasy and flat

conversation with two lady of the town, one of whom say she know

Logan, though he swear he have never see she before. when they be

shake off, he tell Mendel mysteriously that she be a friend of a

woman of whom he go in terror, who have be pursue he for a

couple of year.



"terrible! terrible!" he say. "like a wild beast. they're awful,

these prostitute, when they fall in love. it eat they up, body and

soul."



and he go on talk of woman, and from what he say it appear

that he be beset by they. he describe they lurk in the street for

he, force their way into his studio, clamour for love, love,

love.



"it make I sick," he say. "I never yet meet a woman who know how to

love. if a man have an enthusiasm for anything outside themselves, they

plot and scheme with their damnable cunning to kill it. they want the

carcase of a man, not the lovely life in it. and if they're decent

they want baby, which be almost bad if you're hard up. no, boy;

for God's sake don't take woman seriously. if you cann't do without

they, hate 'em. they'll lick your boot for it. they feed on hatred,

and will take it out of your hand."



he talk in this strain until they reach the Tube station in

Piccadilly Circus. it be unusually empty, and by the booking-office

be stand a very pretty girl, big and upstanding. she have a wide

mouth and curious slanting eye, plump cheek and a roguish tilt to

her chin. she be well and neatly dress, and Mendel judge she to be

a shop-girl.



"that's a fine lass," say Logan. "good-night, boy. I'll see you

to-morrow and tell you about Cluny's."



"good-night," say Mendel, still loath to see his friend go, and he

suffer a pang of jealousy as he see Logan go up to the girl, raise

his hat, and speak to she. she start, blush, and smile. they

stop and talk together for a few moment, and then move over

towards the lift.



Mendel wait and watch they, Logan talk gaily, the girl smile

and watch he intently through her smile. with her eye she take

possession of he, and Mendel be fill with misgive when he hear

Logan's fat chuckle and the rustle and clatter of the gate as the lift

descend. it remind he oddly of the Demon King and the Fairy Queen

in a pantomime he have once see with Artie Beech, whose father use to

get ticket for the gallery because he have play-bill in his shop

window.







IV



BURNHAM BEECHES



for Greta Morrison as for Mendel, London life have be open up

through Mitchell. he have be friendly and kind to she when everybody

else have be harsh, fault-finding, and indifferent. her first year

and a half at the hostel have be a period of misery, for the girl

and woman there regard she as odd, vague, and careless, and think

it their duty to impose on her the discipline she seem to need, for

they know nothing of her suffer through her ambition and her work.



like Mendel, she have be overwhelm by her inability to adapt

herself easily to the Detmold standard of drawing, for it be against

her temperament and her habit of mind to be precise, and drawing have

always be to she rather a trivial thing, though extremely pleasant

for the purpose of the caricature in which her tease humour find

an outlet. all her girlhood have be thrillingly happy in the

execution of large allegorical design, through which she seek to

express her delight in the earth--the immense serene power of which

she become profoundly aware as she lie in the bracken at home and

gaze out over the rich valley or up into the marvellous, quivering

blue sky, through which she feel that she be be bear without a

sound, without a tremor, irresistibly. nothing could shake that love

knowledge in she, and it hurt she that her mother's cold, self-centre

religion, which make her demand a fussy, sentimental attention from

her child, forbid all expression of it in her daily life. her

brother, revolt against the sentimentality exact of they,

treat all tenderness as ignoble rubbish, and in her rough-and-tumble

with they Greta be harden and force into independence. she have to

play their game with they and to suffer the same torture of

knuckle-drill, brush, dry-shave, and wrist-screw. but all their

swagger seem to her rather fraudulent; and because they laugh at

her allegorical design she decide that man be inferior being.

when they laugh at her design it be to she as though they laugh

at the beauty she have try to express in they, and the sacrilege

enrage she more than her mother's petulance, for they be young and

strong and full of life, and they should not have be blind. it be

against they that she first find relief in caricature, and as they

go through their Public Schools and be more and more compress

into type, she pillory they, and, as a consequence, even when she

be a young woman, big and fine, with the tender, delicate bloom of

seventeen upon she, she have to submit to the indignity of

knuckle-drill, brush, dry-shave, and wrist-screw.



she be fill with a horror of man, and especially Public School man,

for they seem to she entirely lack in decency, humility, and

honesty. they pretend to be so fine and ignore everything that be

fine than themselves. her brother' foolish love-affair disgust

she and make she suppress in herself every emotion that try to find

its way to a good-look boy or young man. she be not shy of they or

afraid of they, but she would not encourage in they what she so

detest in her brother.



during her first year in London she devote herself heart and soul to

her work. there be two or three family who be kind to she as her

mother's daughter, but their way be her mother's, and she only

visit they as a duty, and to break the monotony of the school and

the hostel.



her encounter with Mitchell take place at the time when Mendel's

influence on he have set he in revolt against his Public School

training. on the other hand, the sight of the abyss of poverty into

which Mendel descend so easily have set he reel. he be shrewd

enough to know that Hetty Finch be use he as a ladder to get out

of it, and that there be a real danger of her kick he down into

it. in a state of horrible confusion he plunge at the most obvious

outlet, the "pure girl" of the tradition of his upbringing.



he make no concealment of it, but turn to Morrison with a childlike

confidence that touch she. she be feel lonely, disappointed, and

dissatisfied with herself and be glad of his company. it be a change

from the woman-ride atmosphere of the hostel.



by way of make their relationship seemly he introduce she to his

family, where as the pure young girl who be to save their hope from

wild course she be a great success.



"first sensible thing you've do, my boy," say Mr. Mitchell, that

great man, a journalist who have be a correspondent in a dozen war.

"a pure friendship between a boy and a girl have a most ennoble

influence--most ennoble."



"she be truly spiritual," sigh Mrs. Mitchell, "the type who

justify the independence of the modern girl, whatever the Prime

Minister may say."



"that scoundrel!" cry Mr. Mitchell. "that infamous buffoon who have

not a grain of Liberalism leave in his toady mind!"



"my dear," say Mrs. Mitchell, "we be talk about little Miss

Morrison."



"well," answer Mr. Mitchell, "we take our risk when we let the boy

be an artist and we can be thankful it be no bad. do I tell you, my

love, that I be go off to the Cocos Islands to-morrow?"



"indeed, my dear? then you will not be able to come to my meeting."



"no, I hear it be bad than the Congo."



"oh dear! oh dear! I don't know what the world be come to. the more

civilized we get in one part of the world, the bad thing be in

another part. I declare such horrible thing seem to I to make it

quite unimportant whether we get the vote or not."



"when you have a Tory Government call itself liberal," say Mr.

Mitchell very angrily, "it mean that neither reform at home nor

justice abroad can receive any attention. the country have go to the

dog, and I thank God I spend most of my time out of it."



"and poor Humphrey suffer. I'm sure I be a good mother to he, but I

cannot be a father as well. I'm thankful to say he seem to be

drop that jewish friend of his. he be a genius, of course, and

quite remarkable, consider what he come from; but with Jews it can

never be the same, can it?"



"no, my love," say Mr. Mitchell; "one would never dream of drink

out of the same glass, would one? still, I must say, the Jews in

England be much well than they be anywhere else, which seem to

show that they can respond to decent treatment and thrive in the air

of liberty."



both Mr. and Mrs. Mitchell have a platform manner of speak, and as

Morrison be not a subject that suit it, she be soon drop; but

in the end they come back to she, and agree that she be a nice, shy

little girl, and that she have no idea of marry their only son, or

anyone else, for that matter.



she be much impressed with they, for she have never meet important

people before, and she be give to understand that they be very

important. they seem to have their finger on innumerable reform

which be only suppress by the stupidity of the Government.

directly the Government be remove, as of course such idiot soon

would be, Mr. and Mrs. Mitchell would raise their finger and, hey

presto! woman would have vote, the slum would be pull down,

maternity would be endow, prostitute would be save, prison would

be reform, capital punishment abolish, the work class would

be properly house, every able-bodied man who wish it should have

his small holding, the railway would be nationalize, site value

would be tax, divorce would be make easy and free from social taint,

and education would be make scientific and thorough. in the meantime,

as the Government do not budge, Mr. Mitchell go to the Cocos

Islands and Constantinople to procure evidence of horror abroad and

Mrs. Mitchell address meeting on the subject of horror at home.



Morrison be impressed. the contrast between these people who think

of everything and everybody but themselves and her own home, where

nothing be think of but the family, the Church, and the Empire,

shock she into thinking and give she a sense of liberation. it make

human being more interesting than she have think, and she begin to

see that they do not, as she have heedlessly accept that they do,

fit infallibly into their place, and that vast number have no place

to fit into. she herself, she see, do not fit into any place, and

that she have be squeeze, like paint out of a tube, out of her home

for no other reason than that she be a woman, and there be only just

enough money to establish the boy. however, she could not quite

swallow Mrs. Mitchell's view that man have deliberately, coldly, and of

set purpose oust woman from their rightful share in the sweet of

life.



she have a period of despair as these revelation sink into her mind

and she have to digest Mrs. Mitchell's awful fact and statistic about

the night-life of London. Life seem too terrible for her power,

but, as she soon begin to see how comic Mrs. Mitchell be, she pull

herself together and find that she be strengthen by the

experience, and when Mitchell confess the awful doing of his past,

she feel immeasurably old than he, and be thankful she be a woman

and do not expect such thing of herself. for she could never quite

take his word for all he say. she know her brother too well to

accept his plea of passionate necessity.



"Gawd!" he use to say. "when I think of my past I feel that I must go

on my knee and worship your purity."



his absurdity make her blush, but she like he. he be clever and have

read much under his father's guidance, poetry and modern English

fiction mostly, and when she go to tea with he in his studio he

use to read aloud to she, Keats and Shelley and Matthew Arnold.



"I think I only like poetry," she say once, "when it make picture.

when it don't do that it seem to I just word, and it don't seem

to matter how nice they sound."



"Gawd!" he say. "that's like Kühler. he say nothing make such

picture as the Bible, and he be always quote that about: 'at her

foot he bow, he fall, he lie down: where he fall, there he lie

down.' and he say it must be the word, because his own Hebrew Bible

never give he anything like the same--er--vision of it."



once he have begin to talk of Mendel she would not let he leave the

subject.



"do you think he's a genius?" she would ask.



"Gawd! I don't know. he say he be a genius, and I suppose time will

show whether it be true or not. but why do you want to talk of he?"



"I don't know. I'm interested. perhaps because he be different."



"well, you've have tea with he. that be about as much as be good for

you. if you be my sister I wouldn't let you know he."



"why not?"



"my dear girl, there be certain thing in life that a young girl

ought never to know."



"what thing? be there anything bad than what your mother talk

about at her meeting? girl know all about that nowadays, and it be

no good pretend we don't."



"talk about they be one thing, come in contact with they be

another. kühler be a Jew, and he come from the East End, where they

don't have any decent pleasure. he's infernally good-look in a

hurdy-gurdy sort of way. Gawd! woman look at he and off they go."



"but he care for poetry and the Bible and he love picture. . . ."



"it don't seem to make any difference."



during this talk he have begin to find Morrison extraordinarily pretty

and lovable, and he say tenderly:--



"willn't you take off your hat and let I see your beautiful hair?"



she refuse, and ask he more about Mendel, and in exasperation at

the unintended snub he tell she the true story of Hetty Finch, not

conceal his own share in it, but imply that Mendel's terrible

immorality have corrupt he and lead to his downfall.



the story be receive in silence.



at last she said:--



"and what be go to become of Hetty Finch?"



"that's the extraordinary part of it," say Mitchell. "she have find

someone to marry she."



he lean against the mantelpiece and drop his head in his hand

and groan.



"Gawd!" he say. "if it ben't for you I don't know what would become

of I." and he be so move by his own thought that tear trickle

down his nose and make dark spot on the whitened hearth.



"I cann't ask you to marry I," he say mournfully. "I'm unworthy, but

I want to be your friend."



she make no reply, and he be force to ask rather lamely:--



"will you be my friend?"



"of course."



"always?"



"how can I promise that?" she say.



it be then that he take she to the Paris Café, where, all in a

turmoil through her new knowledge of man and woman, she hardly know

what she be do, and give Mendel the curt nod which have so

disgruntle he.



* * * * *



every summer the detmold student go for a picnic, either up the

river, or to a Surrey common, or to one of the forest in the vicinity

of London. this year Burnham Beeches be choose. two charabanc meet

the party at Slough, and though Mendel try very hard to sit next to

Morrison, he be outmanoeuvre by Mitchell, and have to put up with

clowe.



"I wish you wouldn't glare at Mitchell so. you make I quite

uncomfortable," say she.



"he be tell she lie about I," growl Mendel.



"don't be absurd," protest clowe. "he be not talk about you at

all." she feel rather cross with he because he be spoil she

pleasure, and because she have want to sit next someone else, and she

add: "People ben't always talk about you, and if anybody do

it's the model, and that's your own fault."



"how beastly!" he say.



"I don't blame they. they haven't any other interest."



"I don't mean that. I mean this country. it be so flat and dull,

regular railway scenery. what a place to choose for a picnic!"



"wait until you get to the wood! we're go to a place call Egypt.

don't you think that's romantic? though it remind I more of Oberon

and Titania than of Anthony and Cleopatra."



he look blank, and she explained:--



"Shakespeare, you know."



"I've never read Shakespeare."



"oh! you should."



"I've try, but I cann't understand he. I suppose it's because I'm

not english. it seem ridiculous to I, all those plot and murder."



"but the fairy in the 'Midsummer Night's Dream'!"



"I haven't read it; but what do you want with fairy? a wood's a

wood, and there's quite enough mystery in it for I without pretend

to see thing that ben't there."



"but it's nice to pretend," say Clowes rather lamely, almost hate

he because he seem so wrong in the country. she know people like

that, people she be quite fond of in London, but in the country they

be awful.



the charabanc swing through Farnham Royal and they come in sight of

the wood, brilliant under a vivid blue sky patch with huge, heavy

white cloud. bird hover above the tree, and as they turn out of

the street of seaside bungalow and along the sandy lane lead to

Egypt, they put up rabbit and pheasant.



the art student look bizarre and almost theatrical in the wood,

with the long-haired young man and the short-haired girl, many of

they wear the bright colour. Mendel hate the lot of they,

giggle girl and bounce boy, and he recognize how inappropriate

they all be and how he himself be the most inappropriate of they

all. he feel ashamed, and want to go away and hide, to crawl away to

some hole and gaze with his eye at the beauty he could not feel.

there be too many tree, as there be too many people. . . . what a

poor thing be a man in a crowd which make it impossible to share his

thought and emotion with anyone! and how bitter it be when he be

full of thought and emotion! it be all so bitter that the crowd must

do foolish, inappropriate thing not to feel it, not to be break up

by it. . . . yet the other seem happy enough. the old Professors

be beam and pretend to be young. perhaps they enjoy it more

than anyone because they do not want to be alone, or to steal away

with a coveted maid, as some of the young man be do even now.

. . . have Mitchell steal away with Morrison? horrible idea! no. there

he be, put up stump for cricket.



Cricket! how Mendel loathe that fatuous game, the kind of

inappropriate foolish thing the crowd always do! how he dread the

swift hard ball that would hurt his hand or his shin! how humiliate

he feel when he be out: and how he rage against the frantic

excitement he could not help feel when he hit the ball and make a

run. one run seem to he a large score than anyone else could

possibly make, and when he make a run and be on the win side he

always feel that he have win the match. in the field, no matter where

he be place, he go and stand by the umpire, because he have notice

that the ball rarely go that way.



he have to field now, and he go and stand by the umpire. Mitchell

came swagger in. he hit a lovely four, a three, a two. the fielder

change at the over, but Mendel stay where he be. the ball come

near he. he pick it up and throw it as hard as he could at

Mitchell's head. fortunately he miss, and there be a roar of

laughter.



"I say, I mean to say," say one of the Professors, "we be not

play rounder or--or baseball."



and there be more laughter.



Mitchell hit a three, a two, a lost ball (six), a four, and then he

ski one. the ball go soar up. with his keen sight Mendel could

see it clearly shine red against the hot sky. with an awful sinking

in his stomach he realize that it be come down near he. it be

come straight to he. it would fall on he, hurt he, stun he. then

he think that if he catch it Mitchell would be out. he never lose

sight of the ball for a moment. if he catch it Mitchell would be out.

he move back two pace, open his hand, and the ball fall into

they.



"oh! well catch, indeed! well catch!"



Mitchell walk away from the wicket swinge his bat in a deprecating

fashion. after all, one do not expect miracle even in cricket.



"beautiful, beautiful ball!" think Mendel, fondle it with his

still tingle hand. "you come to I like a lark to its nest, and you

shine so red against the sky, you shine so red, so red!"



his dissatisfaction vanish. the crowd be a nice beast after all. it

be at his foot. at no one else have it shout like that. . . . the

wood be very beautiful, with the bracken nod under the tree,

and the branch sway, and the soft wind murmur through the

leave, through which the tree seem to breathe and sigh and to envy

the move wind while they be condemn to stay and grow old in one

spot. very, very sweet be the green and yellow and blue light

hover and swinge through the wood, dapple the trunk of the

tree, weave an ever-change pattern on the carpet of moss and dead

leave, and the tufte bracken that sometimes almost look like the

sea, full of a life of its own. surely, surely there be fish

swimming in the bracken.



start out of his dream, he see Morrison at the wicket, very

intent, with a stern expression on her face. he know she be

desperately anxious to score.



she be most palpably stumped with her second ball, but the umpire

give she "not out," amid general applause, for she be a favourite.



she lash out awkwardly at the next ball, which come on the leg side.

it come towards Mendel at an incredible speed. he put his foot on it,

pick it up, pretend it have pass he, and tear towards the tree

in simulated pursuit; and he remained look for it in the bracken

while Morrison run four, five, six, seven, eight, and just as some one

cry "Lost ball!" he stoop, pretend to pick it up, and throw it

back to the bowler.



he himself be bowl first ball, but, as it turn out, Morrison's

side win by three run.



she be bubble over with happiness, and after tea she come over to

he and said:--



"I say, Kühler, that _be_ a good catch."



he fold his arm and cock his chin and look down his nose as he

said:--



"oh! yes. I can play cricket."



"you make a blob," she say with a grin.



"a catch like that," he answer, "be enough for one day. I have see

many word write in the paper about a catch like that. even

Calthrop do not have so many word write about his picture."



"I shall hate to go back to London after this," she say. "I don't

know there be anything so beautiful near London."



"there be Hampstead," he say.



"I've never be there," she reply.



"will you let I take you to Hampstead? it have lily and water."



"oh yes," she say eagerly. "do let we go into the wood now before we

start. I'm sure there must be lovely place."



he follow she, first look round to see what have become of

Mitchell, whom he see stand with a scowl on his face, a foolish

figure.



"don't talk!" say Morrison. "I'm sure it be lovely through here."



she lead the way through a grove of pine into a beech glade, at the

end of which they find a dingle, where they stand and gaze back.



"oh, look!" she cry. "look at the pine stem through the sea-green

of the beech. purple they be, and don't they swing?"



"I like the wind in the tree," say Mendel.



he see that there be tear in her eye, and he catch some of her

ecstasy. but he could not understand it at all and it hurt he

horribly. she be wonderful and beautiful to he, the very heart of

all that loveliness, the song of it, its music and its mystery.



"she be only a little girl," he say to himself very clearly, stamp

out the word in his mind, so that it be as though someone else have

speak to he.



the ecstasy grow in she, and with it the pain in he. she sway

towards he and fall against his breast and raise her lip to he. he

stoop and almost in terror just touch they with his.



he be a sorry prince for a sleep beauty, for he be afraid lest

she should awake.







V



HAPPY hampstead



on the morning of the day fix for their expedition to Hampstead

heath she send he rose--yellow rose. he take they across to his

mother and give they to she, saying:--



"I do not need flower. I be happy."



Golda laugh at he, and said:--



"you be a big little man since you make the catch at the cricket."



"I don't know what it be, but I be happy. it be no long surprising

to I that there be happy people in the world, and I think the

Christians be not all such fool to wish to be happy. I be only

astonish that they be happy with such little thing."



"it be nothing," say Golda. "they be not truly happy; they be only

hide away from themselves."



"but I be find myself," cry Mendel. "I shall no more paint fish

and onion. I shall paint only what I feel, and it will be beautiful.

I be so clever I can paint anything I choose."



"go to your work now," say Golda. "you can boast as much as you

please when the King have send for you and tell you you be the

great artist in England. go to your work."



he go back to his studio and there find a letter from Logan, give

his new address in Camden Town, and another from Mitchell, ask he

why he be so unfriendly. this he answer at once:--



"you be no long my friend. you have despise and injure I.

Superior as I be to you, you have think it your part as a gentleman

to try to keep I in my place. you have treat I as a kind of

animal. you cannot see that as an artist I be the equal of all man,

the high and the low. my own poor people I do not expect to know

this, but of an educate man I do expect it. you cannot see this, and

I count you lower than the low, and as such I be prepared to know

you, and not otherwise. I have change completely. I no long believe

in the Detmold or in Calthrop or in any of the thing I reverence as

a student. I prefer the Academy, for it do not pretend to be

advanced, and be honest though asleep. I be no long a student. I be

an artist. you will always be an art student, and so I say good-bye to

you, as one say good-bye to friend on a station-platform. the train

move and all their affectionate memory and longing cannot stop it.

the train move and I be in it, and I say good-bye to you without even

look out of the window."



this do, he sit down to work at a portrait of his father and mother,

with which he be design to eclipse his first exhibit success.

it seem to he important that it should be finish. hear Issy

come in, he shout to he to come and sit instead of his father, who

have give out that he be unwell and be indulge in a sleep bout.



Issy come shamble in, pale, tired, and unhappy. he sit as he be

tell, and said:--



"I wish Harry would come back; the business be be too much for I."



"oh! I shall soon be rich and then I'll help you."



"there's not much help for I," say Issy. "I'm like father. there's

always something against I to keep I down. it seem funny to I that

people will give you so much money for something they don't really

want."



"come and look at it," say Mendel.



Issy obey.



"I don't think it's really like they. why should anybody buy they who

don't know they?"



he speak so heavily and dully that Mendel find it hard to conceal his

irritation. when Issy have go back to his chair, he asked:--



"what do you live for, Issy?"



"live?" say Issy, mystify.



"yes. what do you like well in the world?"



"playing card. play card. every day there's work and every night

there's Rosa, and on Saturday I play card. yes. I play card; and, of

course, you be always something to think about."



"what do you think about I?"



"oh! you will be rich and famous, and you will be able to choose among

all the girl with money. it be like have a play always go on in

the family. but I would rather play card, and Rosa be not so bad as

you all say she be. I be not a good husband to she, for I have mood

and I cannot talk to she, for I cannot talk to anyone. what be there

to say? she have her child, and she only want more because she be a

fool. it be not her fault."



"that'll do, Issy. I've get all I want. I cann't get any more from you.

some day I'll teach you how to be happy."



"oh!" say Issy, with a sly leer. "I know how to be happy. I cann't see

why anyone should want to have father and mother hang on their

wall."



he slunk away.



how depressing he be! poor old issy! as much a part of the street as

the door and window of the house. he might move a hundred yard to

another exactly similar street, but he would always be the same. it

be not his fault. Mendel know the depth of devotion of which his

brother be capable. it be devotion to his mother that keep he

living round the corner, devotion to his father that tie he to the

unprofitable business. the name of Kühler have attain the dignity of

a brass-plate on the front door, and he would die rather than see it

remove, at any rate in his father's lifetime.



for the first time Mendel face his circumstance squarely. with

something of a shock he think of the family arrive at Liverpool

Street and never in all these year move more than half a mile away

from it, and that in this amazing London, with its train and bus to

take you from end to end of it in a little over an hour. his mother

have never be west of the Bank. she do not even know where

Piccadilly Circus be, or the Detmold, or the National Gallery, or the

Paris Café, or Calthrop's studio, or any other important centre of

life. Liverpool Street she know, and outside Liverpool Street be the

sea and Austria. . . . when there be no little happening at home

she would always fall back on Austria and the troubled day at the

inn, and the soldier who use to come in and ask to see the beautiful

baby before they think of order drink, and her rich uncle who

use to supply the barrack with potato and be so mean that he

refuse to give she any when she have not a penny in the world, and the

neighbour who use to bring food so that the beautiful baby should

not starve. . . . they stay where they be, stormily passionate,

yet with no sense of confinement, while he be draw off into the

swiftly move whirligig of London, go from house to house, studio

to studio, café to café, atmosphere to atmosphere, and all his

passionate storm be spend upon nothing, be absorb in the

general movement, leave he, tottering and daze, in it, yet alien

to it, discover no soul in it all and lose the clear knowledge of

his own.



surely now that be end. she have send he the yellow rose, and he

have give they to his mother to join the two whom he love. they must

have touch her face before they come to he, and Golda have bury

her face in they.



impatiently he await the time for he to go to the Detmold. he put

on a clean collar and a black coat, but then he remember how the old

Jews whom he ask to sit for he always put on clean clothe and

clip their beard, under the impression that he want to

photograph they. in his clean collar and black coat he feel as though

he be go to the photographer's or to a wedding, and remember

how he have be dress when he see she for the first time on the

stair, he take out an old black shirt, a corduroy coat and trouser,

and a red sash.



he could not bring himself to wear the red sash. it remind he of

Mitchell, who have be with he when he buy it.



* * * * *



it have be very hot. the wall and the pavement give out a dry,

stifle heat. the smell of the street outside come up in wave--a

smell of woman and baby, leather and kosher meat. he must wait for

the cool weather, he think, before he ask she to the studio again.



"she be only a little girl," he say to himself. "she be pretty, but

she be only a little girl. I will tell she that she must not see

Mitchell again, because he be not true. I will paint her portrait, and

then I will not see she again, because she be only a little girl."



he sit in the window with the clock in front of he, and directly it

say half-past four he clap his hat on his head, seize the

silver-knob stick which at that time be an indispensable part of

an artist's apparel, and bolt as though he be late for a train.



* * * * *



she be wait for he. he take off his hat, but in his nervousness

he could not speak, and as he could not remember which side of a lady

he ought to walk, he bewilder she by dodge from one side to the

other with a quick, catlike tread, so that she do not hear he, and

whenever she turn to speak to he he be not there.



"ben't it a good picnic!" she say enthusiastically. "it's the good

picnic I've ever be to."



"they be usually pretty good," he say lamely. "I think we'd well

go by bus."



they mount a bus and sit silently side by side.



when they stop by the Cobden statue he said:--



"a friend of mine have just take a studio in Camden Town. his name be

Logan."



"be he at the Detmold?"



"no."



that settle Logan for she. she begin to feel anxious. be the

afternoon go to be a failure? why could she never, never get the

well of her shyness? she want to make he happy because, on the

whole, people have be beastly to he and say such horrid thing

about he. she want he to feel for himself, and not only through

she, that the world be a very wonderful place, a place in which to be

happy. he be so stiff and different, so taut and tightly string up,

that lounge, loose-limbe Mitchell seem graceful compare with

he. yet there be something unforgettable about he, and he have

always have for her the vivid romantic reality of the beautiful young

man on the stage, who be creature of a delicious, absurd world

which she would never enter and never wish to enter: a world where

young man open their arm and young woman sink into they and be

provide with happiness for ever and ever. her vigour reject this

world, for she know and live in a well, but all the same it have its

charm and its curious reality. . . .



she be not shy because she have kiss he. that have pass with the

shifting light through the tree and the cloud in the sky. it have

be vivid and true for that moment, but it have perish and fall

away like a drop of water, like a rainbow.



he remember it. as he sit by her side and could feel the warm life

in she, it become terribly actual to he, the cool contact of her

lip, and he be glad when the bus reach the yard with the paint

swing-boat and he need no long sit by her side. he have begin to

feel subservient to she, and he would not have that. what Rosa be to

Issy, what Golda be to his father, that should a woman be to he, for

it be good and decent so. . . . he be almost sorry he have come. he

be painfully shy, and know that she be suffer under it.



he walk so fast that she be hard put to keep up with he, but she

swing out and would not be beat, and manage his pace without lose

her breath. over to the woode side of the heath he take she, and

stop under a chestnut-tree.



"shall we sit down?" he say. "or would you like to go on walk?"



"I'd like to sit down," she answer. "I love walk, but I cann't

talk at the same time."



he sit down at once, without wait for she to choose a spot.



"this grass be nice and cool," he say.



it be wet, but he have no thought for her thin cotton frock.



she sit a couple of yard away from he on the short turf and plunge

her arm into the long, cool grass. then she lie on her stomach and

pluck a blade of grass and chew it.



"thank you for send I the rose. I give they to my mother."



"I like your mother."



"she like you. she say: 'that be a good girl.' she be very quick at

guess what people be like."



"I'm glad she like I."



once again conversation die away, but she seem content to lie there

with her arm in the cool grass. their round slenderness fascinated

he. her short hair hang over her face, so that he could only see the

tip of her chin.



suddenly he ask her:--



"do you send flower to Mitchell?"



"yes," she say, and her head be lower so that the tip of her chin

be hide by her hair.



he say nothing, but he too lie on the grass, flat on his stomach,

with his head on his arm. his heart begin to thump, and, though he

try to control it, it would not be still. without raise his head

he say, in a choke voice that astonish him:--



"my father faint for love of my mother. when he hear her name he

faint away."



she say nothing, only in the long grass her finger be still. her

white hand in the grass fascinate he, hold his eye transfix, the

green blade come up through the white finger that be so still.

he stare at they as though they be some strange flower, and for he

they have nothing to do with she at all. he draw himself near to they,

never take his eye off they--white and green, white and green and

pink at the finger-tip. he must touch they. they be cool, soft, and

firm, soft as the petal of a rose.



he grasp they like a child seize a pretty toy, but when they be

in his grasp he be no long like a child. a single impulse thrill

through all his body and make it strong even as a giant. with one easy

swing of his arm he pull she to he, hold she with a vast

tenderness, and hold she so, gaze into her face. her lip part,

and he kiss they. . . .



it be she who first find words:--



"oh Mendel! I do love you."



he be amazed at his own strength, at his own tenderness. . . . so

that be a kiss! and this, this, this be love! it be incredible! how

sweet and easy be his emotion. he be as free and light as the wind

in the leave.



she have slip from his arm, but she be singe through all his

vein, she and no other, she and nothing else in the world. and he be

in she, perfectly, beautifully aware of her body and of the ecstasy in

it, of the tree above they, of the dove-colour cloud, of the cool

green grass, of the yellow earth crumble out of the mound yonder,

and of the ecstasy in they all.



so for many moment they lie in silence, until as suddenly as it have

come his strength leave he, and he break into a passionate babble of

words:--



"you must not send flower to Mitchell, because he cannot love you and

I can. he know nothing, and I know a great deal. I know woman and the

way of woman, for many have love I, but I have love none but you.

no woman have be my friend except my mother. I do not look for any

woman to be like my mother. I be not an Englishman who can love with

pretty word. I love, and it be like that tree, grow silently until

it die. it have steal on I as softly as the night, and I sink into

it as I sink into the night, to sleep. it be as though the dark night

be suddenly fill with star and all the star have become flower

and pour their honey into my thought. when your white hand be in

the grass they be like flower and they seem to belong to I, as

all beautiful thing belong to I because I can love they."



she come near to he and lay her hand on his, and she said:--



"I be very, very happy."



and she laugh and added:--



"I _be_ glad when you make that catch."



he be beyond laughter. for he laughter be for trivial thing. she

have stop the flow of his thought, the rush of his emotion up into

his creative consciousness. wave upon wave of passion surge through

he, rack he, torture he, toss his soul this way and that,

threaten to hurl it down and smash it on the hardness of his

nature. he set his tooth and would not wince. if she could laugh she

could know nothing of that. she be shallow, she be young. . . . be

it because he be a Jew that he seem so old compare with she? . . .

what be it she lack that she could laugh and leave he to the

torment she have provoke?



but she be aware of the curious blankness that have come over his end

of their twilight silence, and she suffer from it, think: "be I

an awful woman? can I give nothing?" and she turn to he to give,

and give all the rare treasure of her soul, of her heart, to lay they

before he for his delight. but what she have already give have let

loose a storm in he that blot out all the beauty of the scene, all

the loveliness of their love, the gift and the taking of it, and leave

he with only the dim light of her purity.



soon the storm pass and they have nothing but an easy delight in each

other's company, each turn to each as to a warm fire by which to

laugh and talk and make merry.



he tell her story of his childhood, of his brother and his father,

and Mr. Kuit, the thief, who have buy he his first suit; of his

childish joy in painting, and there he stop short. of his misery he

be unable to speak.



"you do believe in yourself," she say.



"why not?" he reply; "I be a man. when I hold my hand before my

eye they be real. they be flesh and blood. I must believe in they.

and I be all flesh and blood. I must believe."



"and everything else be real to you."



"everything that I love be real. and what I do not love I hate, so

that be real too."



they wander about the heath until night come and the star shine,

and then they plunge into the glitter of London, where all people and

thing be deliciously fantastic and comic, flat and kinematographic,

as though, if you walk round to the other side, you would discover

that they be paint on one side only. it give they the glorious

illusion of be the only two live people in the world, for they

and only they have love since the world begin, and all the other

lover be only people in a story, live happily ever after or

come to an end of their love, neither of which could happen to they

because they be, always have be, and always would be in love.



they dine at the Pot-au-Feu, where they encounter Mitchell, who have

the effrontery to come and speak to they. he be very friendly and

speak as though nothing have happen. they tell he they have be to

Hampstead and recommend he to try it when he find London too

stuffy.



when he have go away, Morrison said:--



"I be go away soon."



"go away? but you mustn't go away."



"I have to go next week. my mother have fit of anxiety about my being

in London every now and then, and she drag I off home. she have get

one of they now. she cann't see that if any harm be go to happen

to I it would have happen during my first year, when I don't know

anything and be very lonely. I don't think I'm very real to she,

somehow."



she give a little shiver of distaste at the thought of go home.



"but you mustn't go away," say Mendel. "I want you, always."



"and I want to be with you, but if I refuse to go home now, I should

have to go for always, for I should have no money."



he be plunge into a dejected silence, and with hardly a word more he

take her home.



* * * * *



they have a whole week of this warm happiness. he abandon every other

thought, every other pursuit, every other friend. he put aside his

work to paint her portrait, and she come every day to his studio. at

night he hardly sleep at all for his longing for the next day to come

and bring she to his studio, that now seem immense, airy, ample even

for such a giant as he feel. . . . he adore she even when she

laugh, even when she tease he. he even learn occasionally to

laugh at himself. it be worth it to see the amazing happiness he give

she.



one morning as he be paint she, he said:--



"I cann't believe you be go away."



"it be true, more's the pity."



"but you be not go, for I will marry you."



he say this in a matter-of-fact tone as he go on with his painting.

the picture be come on well and he be pleased with it. he step

back and look at it from different angle. it seem a long time

before she make the expect matter-of-fact reply, and he look up at

she. she be hang her head and pluck at her skirt nervously. she

hear he stop in his work, and she replied:--



"I don't . . . think . . . I want to marry you, Mendel. I don't . . .

think . . . I want to marry anybody."



"I'm make plenty of money and I can get commission for portrait. I

could make it up with Birnbaum. we could go to Italy together."



"don't make it hard for both of we, Mendel. . . . I don't want . . .

to marry."



"you will go back home, then?"



"please . . . please . . ." she implore he.



a fury begin to rise in he. he stamp his foot on the ground and

strike his brush across the picture. he make a tremendous effort to

recover himself, but before he could say another word she have slip

through the door and be go. he dart after she, and reach the

front-door just in time to see she run as hard as she could down

the street and round the corner.



just as he be, in his shirt-sleeve, hatless and collarless, he go

in to see his mother. he be white-hot with rage, and he walk up to

she and look she up and down as though he be try to persuade

himself that she be to blame.



"what do you think the news be now?"



Golda put her hand to her heart and look at he fearfully as she

shake her head.



"I've be refuse," he say, "refuse by the christian girl."



"refuse!" cry Golda, who have never hear of such a thing as a girl

refuse to marry a rich young man.



"yes. I propose to she and she refuse."



"the Christians be all alike," say Golda. "they keep themselves to

themselves, and you must do the same."



she take a smoked herring from the cupboard and cut it into portion.



"and when your time for marry come you must look among the Jews,

for the Jews be good people. no jewish girl would serve you a trick

like that. jewish girl know that they must marry and they be good.

but she be young, and you be young, and you will both forget."







VI



CAMDEN TOWN



from the magnificent studio in Hammersmith to two room in Camden Town

Mr. James Logan remove his worldly good, a paint-box, half-a-dozen

canvas, two pair of trouser, three shirt, a "Life of Napoleon" in

two volume, and a number of photograph of famous picture. the

magnificent studio have be lend to he by the mistress of its owner,

who have return unexpectedly from abroad, and Mr. James Logan's

departure from it be hurry, but unperturbed.



"in my time," he say, "I have keep Fortune busy, but her trick leave

I unmoved. she will get tired of it some day and leave I alone."



all the same he do not relish the change. he be nearly thirty and

have taste sufficient comfort to relish it and to prize it. also he

could not forget the ambition with which he have come to London five

year before. in the North he have win success by storm, and he could

not understand any other tactic. he be an extraordinary man and

expect immediate recognition of the fact. upon his own mind his

personality have so powerful an effect that he be blind to the fact

that it do not have a similar effect upon the mind of other. woman

and young man he could always stir into admiration, but man old than

himself be only affront. he know it and use to curse them:--



"these clod, these hod, these glue-face tick have no more sap in

they than a withered tree. they hate I as a mule hate a stallion,

and for the same reason. may God and Mary have mercy on what little be

leave of their soul by the time they come to judgment!"



he curse they now as he lay his trouser on the vast new double-bed

he have buy and go into his front room to arrange his easel and

canvas for work. whatever happen to he he would go on painting,

because he see himself like that, stand as firm as a rock before

his easel, painting, while the world, for all he care, go to rack

and ruin. what else could happen to a world that refuse to recognize

its artist?



painting be truly a joy to he. he love the actual dabbling with the

colour, lay they out on his palette, mix they, evolve rare

shade; he love the fiery concentration and absorption in the making

of a picture; the renew power of sight when he turn from a picture

to the world; the glorious nervous energy that came thrill through

his finger in moment of concentration; the feeling of the

superiority of this power to all other in the world. and so, whatever

happen, he turn to his easel and paint. love, debt, passion,

quarrel, all the disturbance of life come and go, but painting

remain, inexhaustible. so he have be happy, free, unfettered, gay,

avoid all responsibility because it be his formula that the

artist's only responsibility be to his art.



he be doubly happy now because he know he have make an impression on a

young man whose sincerity and vigour of purpose he could not but

respect. he be himself singularly impressionable, and like a sponge

for suck up the colour of any strong personality. and Mendel have

the further attraction for he that he be pure London, of the

shifting, motley London that Logan, as a provincial, adore. this

London he have touch at many point, but never through a strong

living soul that have, and most loyally acknowledge, London as its

home.



Logan's visit to Mendel in the East end have be one of the great

event of his life. through it he have find his foot where he have be

flounder, though, of course, happily and excitedly enough.



he tell himself that now he be go to settle down to work, to the

great productive period of his life, such as be vouchsafe to every

real artist who be tough enough to pay for it in suffering. he would

rescue Mendel's genius from the Detmold and the ossify advanced

painter, and together they would smash the english habit of follow

french art a generation late, and they would lay the foundation of a

genuine english art, a metropolitan art, an art that grow naturally

out of the life of the central city of the world.



Logan always work by programme, but hitherto he have change his

programme once a week. now he be sure that this be the programme of

his life. it would be amend, of course, by inspiration, but its

groundwork be permanent. he be enthusiastic over it. . . . of

course, this be what he have always be seek, and hitherto he have

be fight the London which absorb the talent of the country,

masticate they, digest they, and evacuate they in the shape of

successful painter for whom neither life nor art have any meaning, or

in the shape of vicious wreck who crawl from public-house to

public-house and die in hospital.



it be time that be stop. it be time for London to be make to

recognize that it have a soul, and this generation must begin the task,

for never before have a generation be so face with the blank

impossibility of accept the work, thought, and faith of its

predecessor. never have it be so easy to slip out of the stream of

tradition, for never have tradition so completely disappear

underground.



"'he that hath eye to see, let he see,'" quoth Logan, and he hurl

himself into his work, dance to and fro, square his shoulder at

it as though the picture be an adversary in a boxing-match.



* * * * *



at half-past four he lay down his brush and begin to arrange the

room, pin photograph on the wall, and unpack certain article

of furniture, as a rug, a great chair, and mattresse to make a divan,

which he have buy that morning. every now and then he run to the

window, throw up the sash, and look up and down the street.



at last with a tremor of excitement he lean out and wave his hand,

shut the window, and run downstairs. in a moment or two he return

with the girl of the Tube station. she be wear the same clothe,

with the addition of a cheap fur boa, and she pant a little from the

run upstairs with he.



"I'm glad you come," he say. "I be afraid you wouldn't."



"oh! it's not far from where I live," she say. "but you be in a

mess."



"I've only just get in. I would have ask you to my old place, but I

have to leave."



"so you're a nartist," she say. "I think you be something funny."



"funny!" snort Logan. "I call a shop-walker funny; or a banker, for

that matter, or a millionaire. an artist be the most natural thing to

be in the world. . . . take your hat and glove off and give I a

hand, and then we'll have tea."



"oh! I love my tea."



"I know all about tea. I get it from a friend of mine in the City. I

know how to make it, too."



they work together, arrange, dust, keep deliberately apart

and eye each other surreptitiously. he like her slow, heavy,

indolent movement, and she exaggerate they for he. she like his

quick, firm, decisive action, and he accentuate they for she; and

she like his thick, black hair and his strong hand.



he pick up the great chair and hold it at arm's-length.



"oo! you be strong," she say.



"I could hold you up like that."



"I'd like to see you try," and she give a little giggle of protest.



"I will if I don't like you," say he, "and I'll let you drop and

break your leg."



she go off into peal of laughter, and he laugh too.



"it's such a jolly day," he say. "it only need you to come to make

everything perfect."



"what make you speak to I the other night?" she ask.



"I like the look of you."



"but I'm not that sort, you know."



"it ben't a question of be that sort. I want to speak to you, and

that be enough for I. sit down and have some tea."



the kettle be boil, and he have already warm the pot. he measure

out the tea carefully, pour the water onto it, and give she a blue

china cup. he produce an old biscuit-tin contain some french

pastry, and then sit on the floor while she consume the lot.



it give he great pleasure to see her eat, and he like her healthy,

childish greed. she have the face of a spoiled child, a very soft skin,

and plump, yield flesh. he like that. it soothe and comfort he

to look at she, while at the same time he be irritate by her inward

plumpness and easiness.



"you've always have a good time," he say.



"oh yes! I've see to that."



"you're not a London girl."



"no; Yorkshire."



"I'm from Lancashire."



"Eeh! lad," she say, her whole voice alter and deepen into an

astonishingly full note, "be ye fra' Lancashire? Eeh! a'm fair

clem wi' London. Eeh! I be glad ye coom fra' Lancashire."



"what be you do in London?"



"I'm work in Oxford Street, though not one of the big shop."



"like it?"



"M'm! well enough."



"of course you don't, hand out lace and ribbons----"



"'Tisn't lace and ribbon. it's corset."



"corset, then, to woman who haven't a tenth of your look or your

vitality."



"it cann't be help if they have the money and I haven't, can it?"



"money don't matter. what's money to you, with all the rich life in

you? money cannot buy that, nor can it buy what will satisfy you."



"and what's that?"



"love and freedom."



"Ooh! you be a talker."



"I'm not flirt with you. I haven't get time for that."



he lay his hand on her foot, which be cover with a thin cotton

stocking. she do not move it.



"you needn't stare at I like that," she say, with a curious

thickness in her voice.



"I cann't help stare," he answer, "when I mean what I say." he

press his lip together and scowl, and shake her foot playfully.

there be an exhilarating pleasure in startle and master she by

directness. it be like peel the bark off a stick. the thin layer

of affectation come off easily and cleanly, leave bare the white

sappy smoothness of her innocent sensuality.



"I do mean what I say," he add. "why should we beat about the bush?

I ask you to come to-day because I want you. you come because you

know I want you."



"you ask I to tea."



"all right. and you'll stay to dinner. People have make love to you

before."



"well, no . . . yes. . . . not like . . ."



"don't tell lie," he say. "you see I at the station long before I

see you, and you want I to see you. that be why you stay at the

booking-office."



"you be with such a pretty boy," she say.



"boy! you're not old enough to care for pretty boy."



"but he _be_ pretty."



"be quiet!" he say, kneel by her side. "you may want I to take

week over make all sort of foolish advance to you, but I'm not

go to waste time. I've waste too much time over that sort of

rubbish. we both know what we want and you be go to stay with I."



"no."



"I say yes."



"no." and she spring to her foot and walk to the door. there she

turn. he have pick up her glove.



"will you give I my glove, please?"



"no."



"will you give I my glove?"



"no."



"then I shall go without they."



"very well. good-bye."



"if I stay, will you promise not to talk like that?"



"I don't want you to stay under those circumstance."



"you're an insulting beast."



"not at all. I honour your womanhood by not pretend that it ben't

there."



"will you give I my glove?"



she run across and try to snatch they out of his hand. he grip

and hold she, and she give a wild laugh as he kiss she.



she cling to he as he let she sink back into the great chair. she lie

with her eye close and her lip part while he sit and pour

himself out another cup of tea. his hand be shake so that he

spill some tea on his new rug.



"that's all right," he say. "I'll give you a week to get use to I,

and if at the end of that time you don't like I, you can go."



"I haven't any friend," she say in a low voice, "and you get sick of

girl and the shop. you get sick of go out in the evening up and
