down the street and into the cinema, and find some damn fool to

take you to a music-hall. such a lot of people and nobody to know."



"there's a lot of fun in live with an artist," he say. "you meet

queer people and amusing woman, and you wouldn't find I dull to live

with."



"I feel queer as I come near the house," she say, "as though I know

something be go to happen. I feel very queer now."



"that's love," say Logan grimly. "love ben't what you think it

be."



"you must let I go now."



"when will you come again?"



"never."



"oh yes, you will."



"stop it!" she cry. "stop it! I'm not go to be flummox by the

like of you."



"but you be," he say. "you poor darling!"



he take her hand and stroke it tenderly.



"don't you see that you be flummox by something that be strong

than both of we? I'm shake by it, and I'm whipcord. we're poor

starve people, God help we! and we can save each other. we know we

could do it at once, when we meet. . . . if I say all the pretty

thing in the world it wouldn't help. we're too far go for that.

when you're starve you don't want chocolate. . . . I'm only say

what I know. it be true of myself. if I have make a mistake about you,

I be sorry. you can go. . . . have I make a mistake?"



for answer she turn towards he, gaze at he with glaze eye,

raise her arm, and draw he into they.



* * * * *



a week later Nelly Oliver dine with Logan and Mendel at the

Pot-au-feu. they have a special dinner and drink champagne, for it be

what Logan call the "nuptial feast."



Oliver, as they call she, be flush with excitement, and keep on

tell Mendel that he be the prettiest boy she have ever see. she

call Logan "Pip"--"Pip darling," "Pip dearest," "Pipkin" and

"Pipsy"--because she say he be like an orange-pip, bitter and hard

in the midst of sweetness.



"Pip say you're a genius," she say to Mendel. "what do he mean?"



Mendel dislike she, though he try hard to persuade himself that she

be charming. he be baffle by the solemnity with which Logan be

take she, for she seem to he the type make for occasional solace

and not for companionship. explore she with his mind and instinct,

she seem to he soft and pulpy, not unlike an orange, and if she and

Logan be to set up a common life, then he would be like a pip

indeed. . . . how could he explain to she the nature of genius? can

you explain the night to an insect that live but an hour in the

morning?



"I don't know," he say brusquely.



Logan be dimly aware that his friend and his girl be not please

each other, and he set himself to keep they amuse. he succeed

fairly well, but his humour be force, for he be under the spell of

the girl and the thought of the adventure to which she have consent.

she know it, and be loud and shrill and triumphant, continually

set Mendel's tooth on edge, for the purity of his instinct be

disgust by the blurring and swamping of life by any emotion, and the

quality of hers be not such as to win indulgence.



"Logan will tell you what genius be," he say.



"she'll find that out soon enough if she live with I," growl Logan

a little pompously.



Oliver put her head on one side and look languishingly at Mendel as

she drawled:--



"it's a pity you haven't get a nice girl. then there would be four of

we."



"don't be a fool!" snap Logan. "what do he want with girl at his

age?"



Oliver's lip tremble and she pout in protest.



"I only think it would be nice to round off the party. when you're

in love you cann't help want everybody else to have some too."



Mendel be tear between dislike of she and admiration of Logan's

masterful handling of the problem of desire. . . . no nonsense about

getting marry or fall in love. he see the woman he want and

take she and make she his property, and the woman could not but

acquiesce, as Oliver have do. in a dozen different way she

acknowledge Logan's lordship, even in her deliberate effort to

exasperate he. their relationship seem to Mendel simple and

excellent, and he envy they. how easy his life would become if he

could do the same! what freedom there would be in have a woman to

throw in her lot with his! it would settle all his difficulty,

absolve he from his dependence on his family, and deliver he from

the attention of unworthy woman.



"how shall we dress she?" ask Logan.



Mendel take out his sketch-book and draw a rough portrait of Oliver in

a gown tight-fitting above the waist and full in the skirt.



"I should look a guy in that," she say. "it's nothing like the

fashion."



"you've do with fashion," say Logan. "you've do with the world of

shop and snob and bored, idiotic woman. you're above all that now.

in the first place there willn't be any money for fashion, and in the

second place there's no room in our kind of life for rubbish. you're a

free woman now, and don't you forget it, or I'll knock your head off."



"but it's a horrible, ugly dress," say Oliver, almost in tear.



"it's what you're go to wear. I'll buy the stuff to-morrow and make

it myself. what colour would you like?"



"I willn't wear it."



"then you can go back to your shop."



"you know I cann't. I've say good-bye to all the girl."



"then you'll wear the dress."



"I shalln't."



"for God's sake don't quarrel," say Mendel. "one would think you have

be marry for ten year. let she wear what she like until she

want some new clothe."



"Highty Tighty! little boy!" sing Oliver. "you talk as though I be a

little girl."



"you behave like one," snap Mendel, and her face be overcast with

a cloud of malignant sulkiness.



* * * * *



they go on to a music-hall, where Logan and she sit with their arm

lock and their shoulder press together, whispering and babble

to each other.



Mendel sit bolt upright with his arm fold, stare at the stage but

see nothing, so lost be he in the contemplation of the strange

turn of affair by which the adventure which have promise to lead he

straight to art have deposit he in a muddy little pool of life. he

would not submit to it. he would not surrender Logan and all the hope

he have arouse. prepare as he have be to follow Logan through fire,

he would not shrink when the way lead through the morass. friendship

be to he no fair-weather luxury, and nothing but falsehood or

faithlessness in his friend could make he relinquish it.



he tell himself that Logan would soon tire of it, that Oliver would go

the way of her kind. she be, after all, well than Hetty Finch,

since she have a capacity for childish enjoyment.



she revel in the sentimental ditty and the suggestive humour of

the comedians, press close and close to Logan, and grow elated and

strangely exalt as the evening wear on. and as they leave the

music-hall she grip Mendel's arm and bring her face close to his

and whispered:--



"do wish I luck, Kühler. give I a kiss for luck."



he kiss she and mumble: "good luck!"



"come and see we to-morrow," she say. "we shall be all right

to-morrow."



"oh, come along!" cry Logan, drag she away; and Mendel stand in

the glare light of the portico and watch they as, arm in arm, they

be swallow up in the crowd hurry and jostle its way home to

the dark outer region of London.



he have an appalling sense of be leave out of it. everything pass

and he remain. he live in a circle of light into which, like moth,

come timid, blink, lovable figure, and he love they; but they

pass on and be lose in the tumultuous, heave darkness of life,

into which alone he could not enter. . . . do he desire to enter it?

he do not know, but he be hungry for something that lie in it, or,

perhaps, beyond it.







VII



MR. TILNEY TYSOE



LOGAN with Oliver be more startling and exhilarating than before. he

be fill with a ferocious energy, and his programme be distend

with it.



he say to Mendel:--



"she's an inspiration. I have find what I be seek. you have give

I the inspiration of art. through you I shall reach the height of

the spirit. she have give I the inspiration of life, and through she

I shall plumb the very depth of humanity. she be marvellous. all the

exasperation of modern life be in she, all the impatient brood on

the threshold of new marvel. you think she be stupid, I know, but

that be only because she have in herself such an immense wealth of

instinctive knowledge of life that she do not need to judge it by

pass outward appearance. I be amazed at she, almost afraid of she.

something tremendous will come out of she. . . . by God! it make I

sick to think of all the dabbling in paint that go on, not to speak

of all the dabbling in love. love? the word have become foolish and

empty. I don't wish to hear it utter ever again. . . . I swear that

if it don't come out in paint I shall write poetry. oh! I can feel

the marrow in my bone again, and my vein be full of sap. . . . but

I want to talk business."



"business?" say Mendel, who have be upset and bewilder by this

outburst.



"yes. I want you to approve my programme, for you must have a

programme. it be all very well to work by the light of inspiration.

that can work quite well as far as you yourself be concern, but

what about the public? what about the other artists?--damn they! we're

go to burst out of the groove, but we must have a good reason for

do so."



"surely it be reason enough that one cann't work in it."



"not enough for they. they must be mystify and impressed. they must

be unable to place we. they must feel that we be up to something, but

they must be unable to say what it be."



"I don't care what they say," say Mendel.



"but you must care. when we have carry out the programme, then you

can do as you like, but till then we must pull together. we must do it

for the sake of art. we must make a stand, not to found a school or to

say that this and no other style of drawing be right, but to assert

the sacred duty of the artist to paint accord to his vision and his

creative instinct."



this be come very near to Mendel's own feeling, and he remember

the torture he have be through to learn the detmold style of drawing,

and how some virtue have go out of his work in the effort.



"it be the artist's business," say Logan, "to create out of the life

around he an expression of it in form."



"I agree," say Mendel.



"accurate imitation be not necessarily an expression, be it? you know

it ben't. a picture must be a create thing. it must have a life of

its own, and to have that it must grow through the artist's passion

out of the life around he. it be all rubbish to look back, to talk of

go back to the Primitives or the Byzantines or Egypt. you can learn

a great deal from those old people about picture, but you cannot

learn how to paint your own picture from they, because you can only

live in your own life and your own time, and if you be a good artist

your work will transcend both. . . . now, tell I, where be the work

that be express the glorious, many-colour life of London, where

be the work that do not give you a shock as you come to it out of

the street, the thrilling, vibrant street, make you feel that you

be step back ten, twenty, fifty year? . . . why have life

outstrip art?"



"I don't know," say Mendel, whose head have begin to ache.



"it have not only outstrip it," continue Logan. "it have begin to

despise it."



the postman knock, and Mendel run downstairs in feverish expectation

of a letter from Morrison, to whom he have write implore she to

come again, or, if not, at least to let he have her address in the

country. there be no letter for he, and as soon as he return with

a blank, disappointed face, Logan go on:--



"People collect picture as they collect postage-stamp, to keep

themselves from be bore. naturally they despise picture, and they

despise we for accept those condition. they be intolerable, and

we must make an end of they. we be in a tight corner, and we should

leave no trick and twist and turn untried to get out of it. if we do

not do so then there will be no art, as there be no drama, no music,

and no literature, and there will be no authority among man, and

humanity will go to hell. it be on the road to it, and the artist

have get to stop it."



Mendel have not hear a word. he sit with his head in his hand

thinking of Morrison, and hate she for the blank misery in which she

have plunge he.



"Humanity," say Logan cheerfully, "be fast go to hell. it like

it; and, as the democratic idea be that it should have what it like,

not a finger, not a voice be raise to stop it. everything that stand

in the way--ideal, decency, responsibility, passion, love--everything

be smash. nothing can stop it unless their eye be open and their

poor frozen heart be thaw."



"what do you say?" ask Mendel, having half-catch that last phrase.



"we must try to stop it," say Logan. "we may be smash and sweep

aside, but we must try to stop it. . . . I've be to see Cluny

to-day. he have sell all your thing except one drawing."



"I know," reply Mendel, who have receive an amazing account which

show about two-third of his earning swallow up in colour,

brush, frame, and photograph. he know, but he be not interested.

he be unhappy and restless and feel completely empty.



"we passionate nature," say Logan, stride up and down like

Napoleon on the quarter-deck of the _Bellerophon_--"we passionate

nature must take control. we must be the nucleus of true fiery stuff

to resist the universal corruption. we must be dedicate to the war

of the spirit."



"I've get a splitting headache," say Mendel. "do you mind not talk

so much? the important thing for a painter be paint. what happen

outside that don't matter."



"you think so now," say Logan, "but you wait. you'll find that

painting willn't satisfy you. you will want to know what it be all for,

and one of these day you will be thankful to I for tell you.

. . . Cluny have take on some of my thing, and he have agree to our

have an exhibition together. what do you say to that?"



"so long as I sell I don't care where I exhibit. exhibition be

always horrible. they always make picture look mean and

insignificant."



"you be in a mood to-day."



"I tell you," cry Mendel in a fury--"I tell you I know what art be

well than anybody. it touch life at one point, and one point only,

and there it give a great light. if life be too mean and beastly to

reach that point, so much the bad for life. it do not affect art,

which be another world, where everything be beautiful and true. I know

it; I have always know it. I have live in that world. I live in it,

and I detest everything that drag I away from it and make I live

in the world of filth and thief and scoundrel. yes, I det even

love, even passion, for they make a fool and a beast of a man."



"Young!" say Logan. "very young! you'll learn. . . . but do be

sensible and control your beast of a temper. never mind my programme

if it don't interest you. will you accept Cluny's offer? it be worth

it, for it will make you independent."



"how much do he want?"



"a dozen exhibit each."



"oh! very well."



"and will you come and dine to-night with my fool of a patron, Mr.

Tilney Tysoe?"



"I don't want to know fool. I know quite enough already."



"but I've promise to take you. . . . he adore Bohemians, as he call

we, and he buy picture."



"do he give you good food?"



"some of the good in London."



"all right."



"meet we at the Paris Café at seven-thirty. don't dress. Tysoe would

be dreadfully disappointed if you don't turn up reeking of paint. it

would be almost well not to wash."



"be Oliver go?"



"yes. do you mind?"



"no. . . . no."



* * * * *



it be an enormous relief to Mendel when Logan go. his enthusiasm

be too exhausting, and it be madden to have he talk of

success and the triumph of art and the war of the spirit when life

have apparently reach up and extinguish the light of art

altogether. for a brief moment, for a day or two, it have almost seem

to he that life and art be one, that everything be solve and

simple, that he would henceforth only have to paint and picture would

flow from his brush as easily as song from a bird. this illusion have

survive even the blow of Morrison's departure. he believe that it

be enough for he to have have that hour of illumination, and that, if

go she must, he could do without she. the flash of light have be the

same, magnify a thousand time, as the inspiration that set he at

work on a picture and then leave he to wrestle with the task of

translate it into term of paint. she have appear to he exactly in

the same visionary way, an image shine in truth and beauty, an

emanation from that other world, and he have think he would at worst

be leave with the terrible ordeal of translate the vision into paint.

. . . but when he look at his picture they oppress he with their

lifelessness and dark dullness, and the idea of paint disgusted

he. it be even an acute pain, almost like a wound upon his heart, to

handle a brush. he could not finish the portrait of his father and

mother, and, at well, he could only force himself to paint

flower-piece.



he be incapable of deceive himself. he have never hear of devout

lover sigh in vain, and he have no source of comfort within

himself. never have he shrink from any torment, and this be so cruel

as to be almost a glory, except that it mean such a deathly stillness

and emptiness. he could not understand it, and he know that it be

past the comprehension of all whom he know, even his mother. but he

set his tooth and vow that he would understand it if it take year.

. . . a little girl, a little christian girl! how be it possible?



there be some relief in the thought of she, but very little. she be

still too visionary, and when he try to think of she in life, by his

side, it be impossibly painful.



where be she? why do she not write? her silence be like ice upon

his heart. . . . what kind of place do she live in? among what

people? how be he to imagine she? . . . to think of she among the

tree or under the chestnut-tree be to be tear with impulse that

could find no outlet; desire for creation that make painting seem a

sham and a mockery.



so keen, and fierce, and deep be his suffering that death seem a

little thing in comparison. when he try to think of death he know

that it be not worth think of, and he be ashamed that the thought

should have be in his mind.



he know that he must understand or perish. to say that he be in love

be hopelessly inadequate. he know how people be when they be in

love. they be like Rosa, like animal, stupid and thick-sight,

with a thickening in their blood. but he be possess with a

clairvoyance that make everything round he seem transparent and

flimsy, while think creep stealthily, like a cat on a wall, and

emotion be confound.



* * * * *



for day he have hardly leave his studio, and it be only with the

great effort that he could bring himself to join Logan at the Paris

Café. he feel weak, and the street look very strange, clear and

bright, as they do to a convalescent. as he enter the café it seem

year since he have be there, age since he have sit there tremble

with excitement as he wait for the great Calthrop to come in. he

remember that excitement so vividly that something like it came

rush up in he, and he clutch at it for relief. . . . Calthrop

be there with his little court of model and student. Mendel find

himself laugh nervously as he stand and wait for the great man to

recognize he. Calthrop look up and nod to he. he be wildly,

absurdly delighted. he rush over to Logan and Oliver and shake they

enthusiastically by the hand.



"ben't it a splendid place?" he cry.



"have something to drink," say Logan. "you've be overwork."



"you must say it's a splendid place," insist Mendel, "or I shall go

home. just by that table where Calthrop be sit be where I be

arrest."



"oh, which be Calthrop?" ask Oliver eagerly.



"the big man over there," say Mendel. "I be arrest just there, and

I have to go on my knee to the manager to make he allow I to come

here again. I have to apologize to he. at the time it be the great

tragedy of my life."



he have forget his dislike for Oliver in his elation at find

himself gay again, and he chatter on of the day when the café have

seem to he a heaven full of hero. Oliver listen to he like a

child. she love story, and she lean forward and drink in his

word, and she appear to he as a very beautiful woman, desirable,

intoxicating. yet because Logan be his friend he would not envy he,

but rejoice in his possession of this rare treasure, a woman who

could deliver up to he all the warm secret of life. and he could not

help say so, and tell they how happy it make he to be with

they.



Logan and Oliver glance at each other, and their hand meet in a

fierce grip under the table. Mendel could not see more than their

glance, but the meeting of their eye send a flame like a white-hot

sword dart at his heart. the sharp pain release he, and send he

shoot up into a wilder gaiety.



he feel a hand on his shoulder, and, turn with a start, he see Mr.

Sivwright, his first master, stand above he. he rise and shake

hand.



"I be glad to see you," say Mr. Sivwright. "I've be mean to

write to you, but I've be away, out of London."



Mendel introduce he to his friend and ask he to sit down.



"I cann't stop a moment," say Mr. Sivwright. "I'm very busy. I have

just start a club for artist--open at eleven. these absurd closing

hour, you know. I hope you'll join. it have be open a week. great

fun, and I want some fresco paint. . . . I'm very proud of your

success, Kühler. I feel I have my hand in it."



he produce a prospectus and lay it on the table, bow awkwardly to

Oliver, and with a self-conscious swagger, as though he feel the eye

of all in the café upon he, make his way out.



"who's that break-down tick?" ask Logan.



"Sivwright," answer Mendel. "he teach I when I be a boy. he's a

very bad artist, and he think art end with Corot. I learn to

paint like Corot. really! I use to go with he to the Park and weep

over the tree in the twilight: I never think I should see he

again."



"oh! people bob up," say Logan. "we go on get long in the

tooth, but people recur, like decimal."



"would you like to go to his club?" ask Mendel. "it say 'dancing.'

I feel like dancing."



"oh! I love dancing," say she.



Logan assume his air of mysterious importance and say it be time to

go to Tysoe's.



"we're twenty minute late," he say; "Tysoe would be dreadfully put

out if we be punctual."



as Mendel have plenty of money they take a taxi-cab.



* * * * *



Mr. Tilney Tysoe be an idealist, and he have no other profession. he

be a very tall man with a long cadaverous face, great bulging, watery

eye, and extraordinarily long hand, which hang limply from his

wrist, except when he be excited, when they shoot up with extreme

violence, and carry his arm with they into a gesture so awkward

that he have to find relief from it in a shrug. he be devoted to the

art, have a stall at the opera, a study full of book, and several

room full of picture. an artist be to he a great artist, a book

that please he be a great book, and his constant lament be over

the dearth of great man in public life. it give he the keenest

delight to see Logan, unkempt, wild-haired, shaggy, violent and

brusque, enter his daintily furnished drawing-room, and his eye

pass eagerly to Oliver, look just as she ought to have do, the

mistress of a Bohemian.



"delight! delight!" he say as he coil his long white hand round

Mendel's workmanlike paw. "my wife, I regret to say, be away. she will

be so sorry to have miss you. like I, she be tired of the shallow,

artificial people we live among. we both adore sincere, real people. I

adore sincerity. sincerity be genius."



"that be true," say Logan in a sepulchral voice that make Mendel

jump. "at least, where you find sincerity, you may be sure that genius

be not far behind."



"I buy a picture of yours the other day, Mr. Kühler," say Tysoe.

"I be ashamed to think how little I give for it, but work of art be

priceless, be they not?"



"mine be," say Mendel, overcome his disgust and begin to enjoy

the game.



"you think so," rejoin Tysoe with an undulation of his long body.

"and why shouldn't you say so? you be sincere and strong. you must

force your talent upon an ungrateful world."



a man-servant announce dinner, and Tysoe give his arm to Oliver and

lead she downstair, while Logan put his hand on Mendel's shoulder and

say with a chuckle:--



"be sincere."



Mendel begin at once with the soup, as though he have be wind up.



"I have win every possible prize for painting and drawing, and the

first picture I exhibit be the sensation of the year in art

circle."



"I remember it," say Tysoe.



"like my friend Logan, I be profoundly dissatisfied with the state of

art in England, and though I be not an Englishman I have sufficient

love for the country to wish to do my share in redeem it. the first

essential be a new technique, the second essential be a new spirit,

and the third essential be sincerity."



"wonderfully true!" cry Tysoe. "have some sherry. wonderfully true!

now, take the ordinary man. he might feel all that, but would he dare

to say it? no. that be why I, as an idealist, delight in the society

of artist. you know where you be with they. fact be fact with

they."



"I do like this sherry wine," say Oliver, begin to feel very

comfortable in the warm luxury of the dining-room.



Logan kick she under the table.



feel that more be expect of he, Mendel wind himself up again

and go on:--



"Logan and I be go to hold an exhibition together. it will make a

great stir, that be, if London be not altogether dead to sincerity. we

think it be time that independence among artist be encourage. art

must not be allow to stop short at Calthrop----"



he stop dead as he realize that the wall opposite he hold half a

dozen drawing by Calthrop. Logan rush in:--



"among real artist there be no rivalry. art be not a competition. it

be a constellation, like the Milky Way."



"ah! La Voie Lactée!" cry Tysoe, drop into French, as he

sometimes do when he be move. "quite so! La Voie Lactée!"



"at home in Yorkshire," say Oliver, "there be sometimes two big

star hang just over the top of the moor, and they say it mean

love or death if you see it at half-past nine."



Logan take charge of the conversation, frown at Mendel and Oliver

as though they be naughty child. he describe the masterpiece he

be paint, and Tysoe said:--



"I'm sure I shall like that. it sound big and forceful, like

yourself. do let I have a look at it before anyone else see it."



then he added:--



"I see a charming still-life of yours once. a melon, I think it be.

what have become of it?"



"it be sell, I fancy," reply Mendel, who have never paint a melon

in his life.



"ah! a pity. I want some little thing for a wedding-present. no one

I care about very much, so it must be a little thing."



"he have two or three little thing just now," say Logan. "if you send

a messenger-boy round to his studio he would let you see they."



and suddenly Mendel could keep the game up no long. he begin to feel

choke by the stuffy, empty luxury of the room, with its excess of

plate and glass and flower and furniture and picture. his head

seem to be on the point of burst. he must get out--out and away.

he want to laugh, to scream with laughter, to shout, to die of

laughter, anything to shake off the oppressive folly of his host. and

he begin to laugh, to shake and heave with it. he suppress it, but

at last he burst out with a roar and rush from the room.



"Overworked," say Logan imperturbably. "that's what it be. the poor

devil haven't learn sense yet. it's work, work, work with he, all

the time. he think of nothing but his art, you know. never have, ever

since he be a boy. . . . he'll be a very great genius, and I shall be

leave far behind."



"not you," say Tysoe, "not you. I know no man in whom I have great

faith than you."



"do you think he as good as all that?" say Oliver eagerly. "I'm

always tell he Kühler's not a patch on he."



Meanwhile Mendel have take refuge in the lavatory, where he shout

and shake and cry with laughter. when he have recover himself he

crawl back to the dining-room mutter inaudible apology.



"I'm sorry," he say. "I've not be myself lately."



"you mustn't overdo it," say Tysoe kindly. "you have plenty of time.

you need be in no hurry to overtake Logan. he be enter upon

maturity. your time will come."



Mendel feel disturbed. he have not think of Logan seriously as a

painter, certainly not as a rival or a colleague. Logan be his

friend. that Logan paint be incidental. it irritate he to have to

sit and listen to he hold forth about paint. he have always

like Logan's talk, but have never really connect it with his work.

it be just talk, like reading, or go to the cinema--a sop, a drug,

soothing and pleasant when he be in the mood for it, madden when

he be not.



it be as though a spring have be touch, release his

intelligence, which have always be keep apart from his work. for the

first time he feel, though never so little, detach from it, while at

the same moment the awful inward pressure of his emotional crisis be

relaxed. he be happy, and less wildly gay, and he begin to realize

that he have astonishingly good food in front of he, good wine in

plenty, delicious fruit to come, and fragrant coffee brewing there on

the sideboard among bright-hue liqueur bottle. . . . there be no

need to listen to Logan. there be pleasure enough in eat and

drink and watch Oliver, and think how good it would be to

dance with she, and perhaps with other--little woman whom he would

hold in his arm and feel they yield to every movement that he make.

. . .



he be leave alone with Oliver after dinner, while Logan and Tysoe

retire to the study.



"you've make he very happy," he say rather unsteadily.



"oh, yes!" say she. "it be like a fate, ben't it? I always have a

feeling that I ben't like other girl. I always think something out

of the way would happen to I, though I never think of anything like

this."



"you mustn't tell I about he," say Mendel.



"I must tell someone or I shall die. he's so extraordinary. he say

it's something deep than love, and I think it must be."



"you must not talk about it," he say.



"it make all the stuff he talk about seem silly. I don't understand

it, do you?"



she lie back in her chair and swing her foot, with her eye fix on

the door wait for Logan to return.



Mendel's dislike of she spring up in he again, and he be a little

afraid of she: of her big, fleshy body, so full now of little

trickle stream of pleasure; of her eye, watch, watch, with

the strange, glassy steadiness of the eye of a bird of prey. . . . he

decide that he would not dance with she. he would dance with the

other--the little, harmless, pretty fool.



to reassure himself he tell himself that Logan be happy, and strong

enough to resist the grow will in this woman.







VIII



the MERLIN'S CAVE



LOGAN have cajole twenty pound out of Mr. Tysoe, who stand on his

doorstep, dangle his long hand, while his admired guest creep into

a taxi-cab. he swing from side to side:--



"I have have a most delightful evening--most charming, most inspiring."



inside the cab Logan wave the cheque triumphantly and Oliver try to

snatch it from he. they have an excited scuffle, which end in a

kiss.



"what's the matter with the man?" ask Mendel.



"he's just a fool," reply Logan, "a padded fool. his only virtue be

that he do really think I a wonderful fellow, and he be kind. but

how I hate such kindness, the last virtue, the last refuge of the

decrepit! it be a perfume, a herb with which they be embalm."



"I think he be a very nice old gentleman," say Oliver.



"he seem to I," say Mendel, "the kind of man who think of nothing

but woman all day long."



"hit it in once!" cry Logan. "a parrot will not do more for an

almond than he will for a commodious drab. he could take a nun and by

force of live with she and surround she with every luxury turn

she into a whore, because she would in time become only another

luxury. that be what man grow into if they lose the spirit of freedom.

. . . where be we go to?"



"I tell the man to go to Sivwright's club. it be call the Merlin's

Cave."



* * * * *



the club prove to be a cellar fill with little table. there be a

commissionaire at the door and a book have to be sign. the rack of

the cloakroom contain several silk-line overcoat and opera-hat.



"it's go to be damned expensive," say Logan.



"I'll pay," reply Mendel. "it's my fault."



two tall young man in immaculate evening dress have enter just after

they. they give out an air of wealth and cleanliness and make Logan

and Oliver look common and shabby. Mendel hate the two young man.

what have they do to look so well-fed and unruffled? obviously they

have only to hold out their hand to have everything they want put

into they. . . . they look slightly self-conscious and ashamed of

themselves, and wear a look of alarmed expectancy as they go

downstairs.



why do they come there if they be ashamed? and why do they expect

an asmodean lewdness of an artist' club, they for whom the

flesh-market of the music-hall promenade exist?



"real swell, ben't they?" say Oliver, overawe.



the strain of a small orchestra came float up the stair.



"come on," say Mendel, "I want to dance." and he catch she by the

wrist and drag she downstair.



a girl be stand on a table singe an idiotic song with a

syncopate chorus which a few people take up in a half-hearte

fashion. the sound of it be thin and depressing.



"the same old game," say Logan. "play at be wicked. why cann't

they stick to their commercial beastliness? I should be ashamed to

bring any woman into this. I be ashamed." he half rise from his chair.



"oh! don't go," plead Oliver, who be entrance with her first sight

of what she call a gay life. it be to she like a stage spectacle.

"oh! there's that Calthrop; I suppose all those odd woman with he be

model."



Calthrop be surround by admire student, among they Morrison,

sit prim and astonish and obviously amazed to find herself where

she be. Mendel begin to tremble, and his heart beat violently, as he

stare at she--stare and stare.



she have lie to he then! she have not have to go home! she could strike

he down and then come to amuse herself at such a place as this!



be she with Mitchell? no, Mitchell be not among the satellite.



how strange she look! a wild violet in a hot-house. he wait for

she to glance in his direction, but she seem to be absorb in the

singer and in the song, and every now and then she smile, though

obviously not at the song--at something that amuse she or please she

in her thought. she could smile then and be happy, and all his wild

emotion have make no invasion into her life. . . . no; she would not

look in his direction. perhaps she have see he come in and refuse to

see he.



would the dancing never begin? the dancing take place on a slightly

raise floor. if he dance there she would have to see he.



he find a warm hand place on his leg, and turn he see Jessie

Petrie, a model, with whom he have dance at the studio and at the

Detmold.



"I think I be never go to see you again," she say, "and

Mitchell say you have go mad."



"do I look it?" he ask.



"no. you look bonnier than ever. I'm on my own again now. Thompson have

go to Paris. he say the only painter be there. I think he's go

mad, because he paint nothing but stripe and triangle. and he _be_

such a dear. . . . I'm feel awfully lonely because Tilly have go

to Canada. Samuelson give she the chuck and she go out to her cousin

in Canada, who have always be want to marry she. . . . be you

still down in Whitechapel? I do hate go to see you there. why don't

you move up to the West End? I could come and live with you then, for

I do hate be at a loose end."



she be adorably pretty, dark, with eye like damson, lovely red

lip, touch up with carmine, and a soft white neck that tremble as

she speak like the breast of a singing bird.



"oh! who do you think I see the other day? Hetty Finch! she have a flat

and a motor-car, but I don't believe she be marry." she look

suddenly solemn as she add: "the baby's dead." then she rattle on:

"ben't she lucky? but she's an awful snob. would hardly speak to I!"



"she's a beast of a woman."



"what do you think of this place? I suppose if the swell come it'll

be a success, but they do spoil it."



"yes," say Mendel. "they spoil everything. when do they begin to

dance?"



"they've nearly finish the programme. they have to have a programme

to make people eat and drink."



"let's have some champagne."



he call the waiter and order a bottle.



"be sell lately?"



"no," he say; "but I want to dance. do you hear? I want to dance."



"dancing," Logan throw in, "be the beginning of art. it be too

primitive for I, or I'm too old."



a thin-faced long-haired poet mount the table and read some verse,

which the popping of cork and the clatter of knife and fork

render inaudible. the poet go on interminably, and at last someone

begin drum on the table and shout "Dance! Dance! Dance!" the

poet stick to it. Bread be throw at he and the shouting become

general.



at last the orchestra strike up through the poet's reedy chanting,

couple make their way to the stage, and the dancing begin. morrison

still sit prim and preoccupy. Mendel put his arm round Jessie's

waist, his finger sink into her young, supple body, and he lift she

to her foot and rush with she over to the stage. the whole place be

hum with life, beat to the chop rhythm of the vacant

american tune.



"I do love dance with you," say Jessie, as he swing she into the

move throng of brilliantly dress woman and black-coat man, so

lock together that they be like one creature, a strange, grotesque

quadrupe. and Jessie so melt into he, so become a part of he,

that he too become another creature, an organism in the whirling

circle support and spin round by the music. it be glorious to feel

his will relax, to feel the lithe, soft woman in his arm yield to

every impulse, every movement. he dance with a terrific

concentration, with a wiry collect force that make Jessie feel as

light as a feather.



"oo! that be lovely," she say when the music stop. "you do dance

lovely."



"it be pretty good," say Mendel. "but wait until they play a waltz."



"I want to dance with you," cry Oliver. "you say I should dance

with you."



and she have the next dance with he; but there be no lightness in

she, only a greedy fumbling after sensation.



"this be awful!" think Mendel, never for a moment lose himself,

and all the while conscious of Morrison sit there unmove: of

Morrison, whom he be try to forget. Oliver seem to envelop he,

to swallow he up. he be conscious of hold an enormous woman in

his arm and her contact be distasteful. the dance seem endless.

would the music never stop? . . . one, two, three. . . . one, two,

three. . . . it be like a dancing class with the fat Jewesses at

home. . . . and all the time he be conscious of Morrison's big blue

eye stare at he. would she never stop her damnable smile?



he return Oliver to Logan shamefacedly, as though he be pay a

long-stand debt.



Jessie return from her other partner to he.



"oh! it ben't anything like the same," she say; "and that be such a

lovely tune to dance to."



now that the dancer be warm up they refuse to allow any

interval. they have their partner and be unwilling to stop. the

orchestra be work up into a kind of frenzy, and Mendel and Jessie

be whirl into an ecstasy. they abandon the conventional step

and improvise, gliding, whirling, swoop suddenly through the

dancer. sometimes he pick she up and whirl her round, sometimes

his hand be lock on her waist and she bent backwards--back, back,

until he pull she up and she fall upon his breast, happy, panting,

deliriously happy.



* * * * *



Morrison sit watch. she be tremble and feel very miserable. she

have be bring there by clowe, who have be unable to resist the

flattery of Calthrop's invitation. all these people seem to she to

be pretend to be happy, and she be oppress with it all. she have

not see Mendel until he mount the stage, and then her heart ache.

she remember the etched phrase of his letter to she. she have

write to he, but nothing she could express on paper convey she

feeling, her sense of be in the wrong, and her deep, instinctive

conviction of the injustice of that wrong. . . . he have place she in

the wrong by talk of marriage so prematurely. as she look round

the room she be oppress by all the man: great, hulk creature,

clumsy, cocksure, insensible, spin their vain thought and vainer

emotion round the woman as a spider spin its thread round a catch

fly. . . . she have often watch spider deal with the booty in

their webs, and Calthrop remind she of a spider when he look at

Clowes and lay his hand on her shoulder or finger her arm. and

clowe lie still like a catch fly and suffer it. . . . Morrison be

in revolt against it all. she be full of sweet life, and would not

have it so treat. her prudery be not shock, for she have no

prudery. the man might have their woman so, if the woman like it, but

never, never would she be so treat.



it be because she have be able to sweep aside the sticky thread of

vanity with Mendel that the ecstasy of the wood and the heath have

be possible.



as she watch he now, she know that he be different from all the

other. he have bring an exaltation into the face of the common

little girl who be his partner. he be give her life, not take it

from she.



yet to see he make her unhappy. the music be vulgar, the people be

vulgar, and he have no true place among they. but how he enjoy it

all!



she shake with impatience at herself. it be hateful to be outside it,

look on, look on. a young student have pester she to dance with

he. she turn to he and said:--



"I want to dance, please."



delight, he spring to his foot, give she his arm, and whirl she

into the dance.



* * * * *



slow down to take breath, Mendel look in her direction. she be

go! a black despair seize he, a groan escape he; he hug

Jessie tight against his body and plunge madly into the dance.



the musician have be give champagne. the violinist begin to

embroider upon the tune and the 'cellist follow with voluptuous

thrum chord.



Jessie give little cry of happiness to feel the grow strength in

Mendel's arm, the wax power of his smooth movement. she give

little cry like the call of a quail, and he laugh gleefully every

time she cry. he could feel the force rise in he. it would surely

burst out of he and break into molten stream of laughter, leave

he deliciously light, as light and absurd as dear little Jessie, who

be swinge on the music like a dewdrop on a gossamer. . . . if only

the music would last long enough! he would be as tremulous and light

as she, and while that lightness last he could love she and taste

life at its high point--for she. . . . she be aware of his desire,

and swing to it. it be like a wind sway she, thistledown as she

be; like a wind blow she through the air on a summer's day. o that

it might never end, that the sky might never be overcast, that the

rain might never come and the night might never fall. . . . terrible

thing have happen to Jessie in the night, and she be happy in the

sun.



Mendel be past all dizziness. the room have spin round until it could

spin no more, and then it have unwound itself, make he feel weak and

giddy. he be very nearly clear-head, and every now and then he

catch a glimpse of Logan sketch and of Oliver, sit with a

sulky pout on her lip and tear in her eye because she want to

dance and know she have make a failure of it.



"lovely! lovely! lovely!" sigh Jessie.



"you be like the white kernel of a nut," say Mendel, "when the shell

be break."



"do let I come and sit for you," she say. "I willn't want anything

except my dinner."



"well keep to the dancing," he answer, as he spin she round to

stop she talk.



she begin to stroke his neck and to press her face against his breast.

at the same moment he see Morrison among the dancer. he slow down

and then stop dead. the music rise to an exultant riot of sound.



"please, please!" cry Jessie, cling to he; but he have forget

she.



Morrison and her partner sweep past he, and he watch they go the

full circle. she see he stand, and as she approach break away

from her partner.



"why ben't you dance with I?" he say, shake with eagerness to

hear her speak.



"I'm no good at dancing," she say. "I don't enjoy it."



"who bring you here? Calthrop?"



"he bring Clowes and I. . . . you mustn't stop dance. your

partner. . . ."



"please, please!" cry Jessie, stamp her foot; "the music be go

to stop."



"wait a moment," he say, turn to Morrison. "be you go home?"



"the day after to-morrow."



"I must see you."



before she could reply her partner, who have lose his temper, seize

she and make she finish the dance, and when it be over he march she

back to Calthrop's party, and he never leave her side again.



Mendel return to Logan and Oliver, to find they impatient to go. the

end of an evening always find they in this impatient mood.



"it all bear out what I say," say Logan. "all this night-club

business. People have to go mad in London before they can taste life

at all."



"do you mind if I come home and sleep on your sofa?" ask Mendel. "I

cann't face my studio to-night."



"why don't you take Jessie home with you?" say Logan; "I'm sure she'd

like to."



Mendel wince, and Jessie's lip begin to tremble. she be still

suffer from the sudden end to her happiness. she look at he,

almost hope that he be go to make reparation to she.



"you know I cann't," he say; "I live in my brother's house and he be a

respectable married man."



he know he be in for a terrible night of reaction and desperate blind

emotion; at the same time he do not wish to hurt Jessie more than he

have do.



"I'll take you home in a cab," he say. "but I willn't stay, if you

don't mind. I'm do up. if you and Oliver walk half way, Logan, we

ought to be there about the same time."



Jessie be appease. a little kindness go a long way with she, and

she hate to be a nuisance to a man.



when the cab stop outside the door of her lodging she fling her

arm round Mendel's neck and kiss he, saying:--



"you be a darling, and I would do anything in the world for you."



"you shall come and sit for I," he reply. "good-night!"



"good-night!"



* * * * *



good-night! a night of toss to and fro, of hear terrify

noise in the darkness, of hear Logan and Oliver in the next room,

of shut his ear to what he hear, of fancy he hear someone

call he . . . her voice! surely she have call he, and the ache

and the torment in his flesh be the measure of her need of he. . . .

strange, blurred thought; gust of defiance and revolt; glimpse of

picture, subject for picture, colour and shape. . . . his

mother's hand clutch a fish and bring a knife down on to it.

there be a blue light on the knife. it would be very hard to get that

and to keep it subordinate to the blue in the fish's scale. . . . his

father and mother, eternally together, in an affection that never

find any expression, harsh and bitter, but strongly savour, like

everything else in their life. . . . Issy and Rosa, much the same as

Logan and Oliver, and to they also he have to shut his ear. . . . the

goggle-eyed man at the Pot-au-Feu. . . . London, London, the roar

fiery furnace of London in which he be burn alive, while flame of

madness shoot up above he. . . . music. . . . there be a music in his

soul, a music and mystery that could rise with an easy power above all

the flame. . . . what do it matter that his body be burn, if his

soul could rise like that up to the star and beyond the star to the

point where art touch life and give out its iridescent beneficent

light? . . . life, flame, body, star, all might perish and fade

away, but the soul have its knowledge of eternity and could not be

quench. . . . eternal art, divine art, the world of form, shape in

the knowledge of eternity, wherein life and death be but a day and a

night. . . . sicken doubt of himself, sink down, down into

eternity to be a part of it, never to know it, never to see the light

of art, lose to eternity in eternity. . . . he sit up in the middle of

the night and imagine himself back in the one room in Gun Street,

look at the recumbent body of his family, lose in sleep, huddle

together in degradation. . . . it would have be well to have go

home with Jessie. she would have give he rest and sleep. . . . no,

no, no! . . . she be go away the day after to-morrow. he must see

she before she go, with her big blue eye and short chestnut hair.

she have stop in the middle of the dance. she have break away from

her partner, and on Hampstead Heath she have say "I love you."







ix



"GOOD-BYE"



LOGAN come in early in the morning to make tea. he shut the door

carefully and come and sit on Mendel's sofa.



"she say you hate she," he say.



"I?" answer Mendel. "no. I. . . . what can make she say that?

because I don't dance with she? I have Jessie. you ought to have

dance with she."



"I'm glad she don't dance. it might make she break out. woman be

very queer thing. you never know where they will break out. . . . you

make love to they, touch a spring in they, and God know where it may

lead you. . . . you're not in love with that mop-haired girl, be

you?"



"what if I be?"



"she's just a doll-face miss. you're take with the type because

you're unused to it. for God's sake don't take it seriously. you're

much too good to waste yourself on woman. she'll drive you mad with

purity and chivalrous devotion and all the other schoolgirl twaddle.

leave all that to the schoolboy English. it's all they're good for.

they've breed it on purpose to be the mother of more schoolboy. it be

the basis of the British Empire. but what be the British Empire to you

or any artist? nothing."



"I don't want to talk about it," say Mendel.



"she willn't marry you," say Logan. "she willn't live with you. she'll

give you nothing. she'll madden you with her conceited stupidity and

wreck your work. . . . what you want be what every decent man

want--to take a woman and keep she in her place, so that she cann't

interfere with he. that's what I've do, and it's make a man of I,

but I'm not go to let she know it. she'd be crow like an old hen

that have lay an egg. . . . no farmyard life for I, thank."



Oliver bawl for her tea and Logan hasten to make it, and

disappear into the bedroom.



Mendel get up and dress, feel eager for the day. the sun shine in

through the window and fill the room with a dusty glow, make even

the shabby bareness of the place seem charming.



"it be a good day," he say to himself. "I shall work to-day." and he

be annoy at not have his canvas at hand.



on an easel stand the picture which Logan have describe to Tysoe, a

London street scene with a group of people gaze into a shop window.

it be a clever piece of work, very adroit in the handling of the

paint and pleasing in colour, but Mendel have an odd uncomfortable

feeling of having see it before, and yet he know that the technique

be novel. yet it be precisely the technique that seem familiar.

certain liberty have be take with the perspective which, though

they be new to he, do not surprise he.



Logan come in dressed and say that Oliver would not be a minute. she

appear in a dressing-gown.



"well?" she say; "none the bad for last night?"



"no, thank," say Mendel. "why should I be? I enjoy it."



"do Logan tell you we be go to Paris?"



"no. he say nothing about it."



"I'm die to go to Paris. he say they understand the kind of thing

we have last night in Paris."



"you're not go for good, be you?" ask Mendel.



"no. just a trip. I want you to come too. we'll see some picture and

have a good time. I cann't speak a word of French, but they say English

be good enough anywhere."



"yes, I'd like to go," say Mendel. "I want a change, before I settle

down to work for the exhibition. be that picture go to be in

it?"



"yes. do you like it?"



"I like it. it seem to I new. strong than most thing. all these

people go in for thin, flat colour and green and mauve make I

long for something solid."



"I'm go to show that and a portrait of Oliver."



"I want my breakfast," say she.



"oh! shut up. we're talk. . . . I've just begin the portrait. no

psychological nonsense about it. it's just the head of a woman in

paint. I don't want any damn fool write about my picture: she be

wise than the chair on which she sit and the secret of the

antimacassar be her. a picture's a picture and a book's a book."



"I do want my breakfast," sing Oliver.



Logan go livid with fury.



"be silent, woman," he say.



"I shalln't, so there. I want my breakfast."



"why the hell don't you get the breakfast then?"



"because you say you would."



Logan begin to prepare the breakfast--rasher of bacon and egg.



"you don't mind eat pork?" he ask Mendel.



"no. I like it, but I never get it at home."



"Fancy Jews be still as strict as that!" say Oliver. "just like

they be in Shakespeare's time."



"just as they be in the time of Moses and Aaron," say Mendel. "they

don't alter except that they haven't get a country to fight for."



"thank God!" say Logan, "or there'd be a bloody mess every other

week. Fancy a jewish Empire, with you send out, like David, to hit the

Czar of Russia or Chaliapine in the eye with a stone from a sling.

think of your sister-in-law lure the Kaiser into a tent and knock

a nail through his head. I wish she could, upon my soul I do!"



"I think we should only be lead into captivity again," say Mendel.

"our fighting day be over, and someone tell I the other day that

many of the most advanced artist in Paris be Jews."



"if they be all like you," say Logan, "I shouldn't mind. but I'm

afraid they're not. the Jews have get all the money and they keep the

other people fight for it, and charge they a hell of a lot for gun

and uniform to do it with. oh! there be Christians in it too, but

they have to be nice to the Jews to be allow to share the spoil. I

don't wonder the Jews leave the Promised Land when they find the world

be inhabit by fool who would let they plunder it."



"there's not much plunder in my family," say Mendel.



* * * * *



after breakfast he declare that he must go, and Logan announce that

he would walk with he to enjoy the lovely sunny day. Oliver want to

come too, but he tell she to stay where she be, and he leave she in

tear.



"she's get a bad habit of cry," he say, "and she must be break of

it. she cry if I don't speak to she for an hour. she cry if I go

out without tell she where I be go. she cry if I curse and

swear over my work, and if I be pleased with it she cry because I be

never so happy with she. . . . I feel like hit she sometimes, but

it ben't her fault. she haven't settle down to it yet. she say I

don't love she when she know she never expect to be love so much.

and she cann't get use to it."



"why don't you paint her cry?" ask Mendel maliciously.



"by Jove! I will," cry Logan. "damned interesting drawing, with her

eye all pucker up. . . . but it's a shame on a day like this to be

out of temper with anything. Lord! how woman do spoil the universe, to

be sure! do they give we anything to justify the mess they make of it?

. . . woman and shopkeeper. I don't see why one should have any mercy

on either of they. I have no compunction in steal anything I want.

shopkeeper steal from the public all the little halfpenny and

farthing of extra profit they exact."



he lead Mendel into a picture shop and ask for a reproduction of a

picture by Van Tromp, and when the girl retire upstairs to ask about

that non-existent artist, he turn over the album and help himself

to half a dozen reproduction, roll they up, and put they in his

pocket. when the girl come down and say they be out of Van Tromps,

he said:--



"I'm sorry. very sorry to trouble you."



when they be out of the shop he chuckle, and be as elated over his

success as Mr. Kuit have be over his exploit.



"oh! I should be an artist in anything I do," he say. "I don't

wonder thief cann't go straight once they get on the lay. if I

ben't a painter I should be a criminal."



he walk with Mendel as far as Gray's Inn, and there leave he, say

he have another picture-buying flat to go and see, and after that he

must pay a visit to Uncle Cluny and keep he up to the mark. he be in

fine fettle, and go off singing at the top of his voice.



Mendel buy some flower on the way home because he wish always to

have flower, even if she be to send no more.



he be sure of himself to-day. he be in love and glad to be in love.

surely it could have no bad suffering than that through which he have

pass, and if it do, well, so much the bad for he. . . . he be

glad it have happen. his father would not be able to sneer at he any

more, as he be always sneer at Issy and Harry--Harry, who have

desert his father and mother for the sweetbread of Paris. (Jacob

always call sweetmeat sweetbread.) he have a bitter, bite tongue,

have Jacob, and the habit of use it be grow on he. Mendel know

that he have deserve many of his sneer, but now they could touch he

no long. his life, like his art, now contain a passion as strong

as any Jacob have know in his life, and strong, because it be

wed to beauty, to which Jacob be a stranger.



he be able to work again at his picture of his father and mother. he

could make something of it now, he know, because he could understand

his father and appreciate the strength in he which have keep his

passion alive through poverty and a life of constant storm and

upheaval. he remember his father knock down the schoolmaster,

and the soldier in the inn with the heavy glass. oh yes! Jacob be a

strong man, and he have nearly die of love for Golda, the beautiful.



he work away with an extraordinary zest, and he know that it be

good. as he grow tired during the afternoon he be overcome with a

great longing for she to see it, just to see it and to say she like

it. it would not matter much if she do not understand it, so long as

she see it and like it.



he turn to the roughly sketch portrait of she to ask she if she

like it, and as he do so the door open and she come in. her arm

be full of flower, so that her face be rest in they, her dear

face, the sweetest of all flower.



"you say . . . you must see I, so I bring you these to say

good-bye."



"do come in and see my picture. it be nearly finish."



"oh! it be good," she say shyly.



"I think you'd like it. I want you to like it. do stay a little

and talk."



she sit down and look about the studio, pucker up her eyebrow

nervously and make her eye very round and large.



"you never tell I how old you be," he say nervously.



"I'm nineteen."



"I'm twenty. just twenty. how long be you go away for?"



"I don't know. until the winter, I expect."



"what will you do there in the country? it be important that you

should tell I, because I must know how to think of you. what shall

you do? be it a big house? be you--be you rich?"



"no. it be not a very big house. my mother be fairly well off, but I

have four brother, and they all have to go to Oxford and Cambridge.

. . . there's a good garden, and I shall spend a lot of time in that,

dig and look after the flower. and I shall try and do some

work. there's a big barn I can have for a studio."



"a big barn. yes. be your brother nice man?"



"two of they."



"and there's a river and a common. may I write to you?"



she be silent for a long time, and then she said:--



"no. please don't."



his happiness vanish. it be as though a hole have open in the

floor and swallow it up.



"why not?" he ask. "why not?"



she shrink into herself for a moment, but shake off her cowardice and

answered:--



"I don't want to hurt you."



"you say you love I. you can do what you like with I!"



"you're so different," she say. "too different."



"from what? from whom? go on, go on!"



she love his violence and gain courage from it.



"you mustn't think it mean of I. I don't care a bit what people say,

but I don't want to hurt you--in your work, I mean. it ben't all that

I think and mean, but it be a part of it, a little part of it. People

be furious at our be see together. it begin at the picnic. we

be see walk over the heath. clowe tell I. she cann't bear it.

she's a good friend. . . . it hurt I when she tell I, and I know

that I must tell you. it ben't only old woman. it be all the important

people, who can hurt your work."



"nobody can hurt my work."



"but they can. they be say your work be bad, all the people who

say it be so good only last year, all the people who believe in

you. and it's all through I. it's my fault."



she begin to weep silently. he be unmove by the sight of it, so

appal be he by the sudden devastation of his life. suffer

within himself he know, but hostility from without he have not have to

face. . . . many little slight be explain--man who have give he

an indifferent nod, man who have apparently not see he in the street.

in the surprise of it he be blind even to she. it be like a

sandstorm cover he up, fill with grit every little chink and

crevice of his being. he snort with fury and contempt.



he shake himself free of the oppression of it. this be nothing to do

with she; it be not what he want from she--the gossip and

tittle-tattle, the sweeping of the studio. the model sicken he

of that. . . . so it be his turn now. well, other man have survive

it.



"that ben't why you want to say good-bye."



"no. I'm not plead to you to let I off, or anything like that. I

believe in you more than in anybody else, more than I do in myself.

. . . I don't believe in myself much."



it have all seem clear to she before she have come. he would

understand how wrong and twist the whole thing have become. they

would suffer together and they would see how useless such suffering

be in a world of beauty and charm and youth, and they would part

because they have to part. he would understand, even if she could not

rightly understand, for he be strong and simple and direct, and free

of the soft vanity of youth.



but he do not understand. he be angry and domineering.



"why do you say all this?" he say heavily, flounder for word.

"what do it mean? nothing at all. you belong to I. you give up

Mitchell because I say you must. have you give up Mitchell?"



"yes."



"very well then. nothing else matter. if I want a thing I will break

through a chinese wall to get it. nothing can stop I, because when I

want a thing it be mine already. I want it because it be mine

already."



he be make it impossible for she--impossible to go, impossible to

stay, impossible to say anything.



outside in the street the heavy dray go clatter by on the stone

sett. when they have pass there come up the shrill cry of child

play in the street, the drone of a Rabbi take a class of boy in

Hebrew. on the hot air come the smell of the street--a smell of woman

and baby and leather and kosher meat.



"I know the way of woman," he say. "my mother have be my friend

always. but I do not know your way. I only know that I love you. you

be mine as that picture be mine, and you cannot take yourself from

I."



"I don't want to take myself from you," she say, half angry, half in

tear. "I want to make you understand I."



"what be there to understand? do I understand my picture?" he cry.

"do you want no mystery? how can there be life without mystery? I

don't expect you to understand. I only want you to be honest and true

to I. . . . I conceal nothing. I be a Jew. I live in this horrible

place. my life be as horrible as this place. you know all that, all

there be to know, and you love I. you cannot alter I. you cannot

change my nature. . . ."



"don't say any more," she say. "it only become bad with talk."



"what become bad?"



she could not answer he. she could not say what she feel. the wood,

the heath, and--this; the rattle and smell of the street, the

dinginess of the studio, the dinginess of his soul--the dinginess and

yet the fire of it. on the heath he have be like a faun, prick-eare

and shaggy, but wild and free as her spirit be wild and free. here he

be rough, coarse, harsh, and tyrannical. she could feel he batter

at she with his mind, search she out, probe into she, and she

resent it with all the passion of her modesty. she gather up all

her force to resist he.



"you be terrible! terrible!" she cry. "don't you see that it must

be good-bye?"



"I say it must not," he shout. "I say it be nonsense to talk of

good-bye, when we have just meet, when the kiss be yet warm on our

lip. for a kiss be a holy thing, and I do not kiss unless it be holy.

I say it be not good-bye."



"I say it be and must be," she say. "you be terrible. you hurt I

beyond endurance."



"and why should you not be hurt? be I to have all the pain? I want to

share even that with you."



"it be impossible," she say dully, unable to share, or deal with, or

appreciate the violence of his passion, and fall back on the

mulishness which have be develop in she through her tussle with

her brother. through her mind shoot the horrible thought:--



"we be quarrel--already quarrel."



to she he seem to be drag she down, defile she. his eye be

glare at she with a passion that she take for sensuality, because it

come out of the dinginess of his soul. and he be stiffen into an

iron column of egoism, on which she know she could make no impression.

she know, too, that her presence be aggravate the stiffening

process. . . . she feel catch, trap, and she want to get away.

Love must be free--free as the wind on the heath, as the blossom of

the wild cherry. love must have its blossoming time, and he be

demand the full heat of the summer. . . . she must get away.



"good-bye," she say, hold out her hand.



he take her hand and pull she to he.



"no! no! no!" she cry. "no! good-bye! good-bye!"



she turn away and be go.



unable to contain his agony, he fling himself on his bed and sob

out his grief.



"she be mine!" he moan. "she be mine, and she cannot take herself

from I."



and when his tear be shed he begin to think of the other woman who

have come to he without love, so easily, so gratefully, some of they,

and this little girl who love he could tear herself away--at a

fearful cost. he know that. but if she could tear herself away, if she

could say good-bye, what could she know of love?







X



PARIS



MENDEL be able to finish his portrait of Jacob and Golda, but only at

the cost of painful and bitter labour. he be tear two way: longing

to finish it, yet dread the end of it, for he could not see beyond

it. every picture he have paint have bring with it the certain

knowledge that it would lead to a well, that he be advance

far on the road to art. but there be a finality about this

picture. it be an end in itself. it be not like most of his work,

one of a possible dozen or more. a certain stream of his feeling end

in it and then disappear, leave he without guide or direction.



therefore, when the picture be end he find himself besottedly and

uncontrollably in love and in a maddeningly sensitive condition, so

that any sudden glimpse of beauty--the star in the night sky, a

girl's face in the train, flower in a window-box--could set he

reel. More than once he find himself cling to the wall or a

railing, emerge with happy laughter from a momentary lack of

consciousness. in the street near his home he find a lovely little

girl, of the same type as Sara, but more beautiful. graceful and

lively she be, fully aware of her vitality and charm, and she use to

smile at he when he go to meet she as she come out of school, or

stand and watch she playing in the street.



at last he ask she shyly if she would come to his studio that he

might draw she. she consent and come often. she would chatter away,

and, study she, he be astonish at her womanishness, and he be

overwhelmed when she say one day:--



"you don't want to draw I. you only want to look at I."



he be thrust back into the thought he have be avoid. if this

child know already so frankly why he be attract to she, why could

not that other? why do she seem to insist that he should regard she

with the emotion with which he approach a work of art? a work of

art could yield up its secret to the emotion, but she could only

deliver hers to love dwell not in any abstract region, but here on

earth, in the life of the body. . . . he often think of she with

active dislike, because she seem to he to be lack in frankness.

if she be go to cause so much suffering, as she must have know

she would with her good-bye, then she must have her reason for it.

what do she mean with she neither yes nor no? with woman there should

be either yes or no. a refusal be unpleasant, but it could be

swallow down with other ill; and there be other. but this girl,

this short-haired Christian, block his way, and there be no other

except as there be cab on the street and meal on the table.



for a time he avoid Logan and Oliver. he know that Logan would

despise he for his weakness in set his heart on a girl who run

away from he, for he know and admire the tremendous force with which

his friend have hurl himself into his life with the girl of the

station, constantly woo and win her afresh and urge she to

share his own recklessness. he admire, too, Logan's insistence on an

absolute separation of his art and his life with Oliver, who be never

for one moment admit to his mind. rather to his dismay, but at the

same time with a wild rush of almost lyrical impulse, Mendel, find

himself with no other emotion than that of be in love, set himself

to paint love. he work with an amazing ease, paint one picture

one day and cover it with another the next, feel elatedly

convince that everything he do be beautiful, yet know within

himself that he be in a bad way.



he avoid Logan, but Logan need he, and come to tell he so.



"it be all very well for you to shut yourself up," he say, "but I

cann't live without you. you know what Oliver be to I, but it be not

enough. the more satisfying she be on one plane, the more I need on

the other the satisfaction that she cannot give I. woman cann't do it.

they simply cann't, and it be no good try. if you try, it mean

make a mess of both love and art. she be jealous? very well. let she

be jealous. she enjoy it, and it help she to understand a man's

passion."



"I cann't stand it when you talk in that cold-blooded way about woman."



"I'm not cold-blooded," say Logan, astonish at the adjective.



"I sometimes think you be, but I be apt to think that of all english

people," reply Mendel, wonder within himself if that do not

explain Morrison. "yes. I often wonder what you would be like if you

be in an office, wear a bowler hat, and go to and fro by the

morning and evening train."



"why think about the impossible?" laugh Logan. "anyhow, I'm not

go to let you shut yourself up. I want to go to Paris, and I cann't

face three week alone with Oliver. twenty-one day, sixty-three

meal. no. it cann't be do."



"yes, I'll go to Paris," think Mendel. "I will go to Paris and I

will forget."



"you must come," urge Logan. "Madame at the Pot-au-Feu have give I

the name of a hotel keep by her sister-in-law. very cheap. bed and

breakfast, and, of course, you feed in restaurant. . . . you want

dig out of your hole. I don't know why, but you seem to have

insist more on be jewish lately. it be much more important for

you to be an artist and a man. I regard you as a sacred trust. I do

really. you be the only man in England for whom I have any respect,

and I need you to keep I decent." he add: "I need you to keep I

alive, for, without you, Oliver would gobble I up in a month."



he seem to be joke, but Mendel could not help feel that he be

at heart serious, and he have the unpleasant sinking of disgust which

sometimes seize he when he think of Logan and Oliver together. he

could not account for it, and the sensation give he a sickly pleasure

which make he weak with Logan than with anybody else. besides,

Logan often bewilder he, and he could not tolerate his inability to

grasp idea except through a mad rush of feeling, and he hate the

fact that while Logan's mind seem to move steadily on, his own

crumble to piece just at the moment when it be on the point of

absorb an idea.



for these reason he consent to go to Paris. the three week should

consolidate or destroy a friendship which have remain for he

distressingly inchoate. deep in his heart he hope that it would

become definite enough and strong enough to drive out his

indeterminate love. to be in love without enjoy love be in his

eye a fatuous condition, undignified, vague, a kind of cuckoldry.



* * * * *



Oliver be aflame with excitement over the trip to Paris. she speak of

it with an almost religious exaltation. as usual, her emotion be

entirely uncontrolle, become a physical tremulation, and she remind

Mendel of a wobble blanc-mange.



the plan be to have a fortnight in Paris and a week at Boulogne, for

bathing and gambling at the Casino.



no soon have he leave London than Mendel feel his care and anxiety

fall away from he, and he begin to wish he have bring Jessie Petrie.

he propose to wire for she from Folkestone, but Logan point out

that Oliver could not stand woman and be jealous of they.



"she'd say Jessie be make eye at I," he say. "and if she make

eye at you she'd be almost as bad."



in that Mendel could sympathize with Oliver. he be himself often

suddenly, unreasonably, and violently jealous of other man over woman

for whom he do not care a fig.



he set himself to be nice to Oliver, and she in her holiday mood

respond, so that on the boat and in the Paris train Logan be sink

in a gloomy silence, and in the hotel at night, in the next room,

Mendel could hear he storm at she, refuse to have anything to do

with she, threaten to go home next day unless she promise to keep

her claw, as he say, off Kühler. she promise, and they embark

far upon their perilous voyage in search of an unattainable land

of satiety.



* * * * *



their hotel be near the Montparnasse station, and they discover a

café in the Boulevard Raspail which be frequent by artist and

model, one or two of whom Mendel recognize as former habitué of the

Paris Café. they be soon draw into the artist world, and except

that he go to the Louvre instead of to the National Gallery for

peace and refreshment, Mendel often think he might just as well be

in London. there be the same feverish talk, the same abuse of

successful artist, the same depreciation of old master, but there

be more body to the talk, and sometimes a Frenchman, find speech

useless with this shy, good-look Jew, would make himself clear with

what English he could muster and a rapid, skilful drawing. for the

most part, however, he have to rely on Logan's paraphrase, until one

day in the Boulevard St. Germain he run into that Thompson, lament

by Jessie Petrie, the painter of stripe and triangle.



Thompson be a little senior to Mendel at the Detmold, have hardly

speak to he in the old day, but be now delighted to meet a

familiar London face.



"I _be_ glad!" he say. "come and see my place. how be they all in

London--poor old Calthrop and poor old Froitzheim? I should have

kill myself if I'd stay in London; nothing but talk and woman,

with work leave to find its way in where it can. here work come first.

I suppose they haven't even hear of Van Gogh in London?"



Mendel have to confess that he have never hear of Van Gogh.



"a Dutchman," explain Thompson, "and he cut off his ear and send it

to Gauguin. ever hear of Gauguin?"



"no. but a man don't make himself a great artist by cut off his

ear."



"Van Gogh be a great artist before that. he kill himself: shot

himself in his bed, and the doctor find he in bed smoke a pipe. he

be quite happy, for he have do all he could."



that sound more like it to Mendel, more like the deed of a warrior

of the spirit.



"I'll show you," say Thompson, and they go round the gallery.



Mendel's head be nearly burst when he come out. the riotous

colour, the apparent neglect of drawing and abuse of form, the entire

absence of tone and atmosphere, shock he. he resent the wrench

give to all his training, and he take Thompson to the Louvre to go

back to Cranach and the early Italians. Thompson would not hear of

they, and insist on his spending over an hour with Poussin.



"I can see nothing in they. good painting, good drawing, but dull, so

dull! the flat, papery figure mean nothing."



"they mean everything to the picture," say Thompson, "and you have no

right to go outside the picture. Poussin keep to his picture, and so

must you if you be to understand he."



"I can see all that," say Mendel, "but he be dull. I cann't help it,

he bore I."



"it be pure art."



"then I like it impure."



"you don't really. but you be all like that when you first come from

London. you think that because a thing be different it must be wrong.

have you come over alone?"



"no. I'm with a man call Logan and his girl. he be a great painter,

or he will be one. anyhow, he be alive and have idea."



"do he know about Van Gogh?"



"no; but he say the next great painter must come from England."



"Pooh! Whistler!" say Thompson in a tone of vast superiority. "nous

somme bien loin de ça."



"please don't talk French," say Mendel. "I don't understand a word."



"Whistler have good idea," continue Thompson. "it be a pity he be

not a well artist."



Mendel be begin to feel bored. he do not understand this new

painting for painting's sake, and do not want to understand it. to

change the subject he said:--



"I nearly bring Jessie Petrie with I."



"I wish you have. she be a dear little girl, and I nearly send for she

the other day, but I've no use for the model now. it be perfectly

futile try to cram a living figure into a modern picture."



"I don't see why, if you can paint it."



"really," say Thompson, "I don't see what you have come to Paris for,

if you haven't come to learn something about paint. one wouldn't

expect you to understand Picasso straight off, but anyone who have

handle paint ought to be able to grasp Van Gogh."



"he be try for the impossible," grunt Mendel. "the important

thing in art be art. I've come to Paris to have a good time."



"oh! very well," say Thompson. "why don't you say so before? I'll

show you round."



* * * * *



Mendel take Thompson round to his hotel and up to Logan's room, where,

enter without knock, they find Logan kneeling on the floor with

Oliver in a swoon in his arm. he have open her blouse at the neck

and unlace her corsage.
