Mendel think Oliver look as though she be go to die, and his

first idea be to run for the doctor.



"she'll come round," say Logan. "it's my fault. I be brutal to she.

. . ." he nod to Thompson. "how do you do?" and he cover up

Oliver's large bosom.



she come to in a few moment, open her eye slowly, roll they

round, and come back to Logan, on whom she fix a gaze of devouring

love. she put up her arm and draw his head down and kiss his lip.



Mendel draw Thompson out into the corridor.



"she be sham," he say.



"I don't think so," reply Thompson. "what have happen? do he

knock she about?"



"not that I know of. they've not be together very long. they cann't

settle down."



"she's a fine woman," say Thompson.



* * * * *



they be call in again and find Oliver sit up on the bed

eat chocolate. she greet Thompson with a queenly gesture, and

clap her hand when Mendel tell she they be go out to see the

sight.



"I'm sick of artist," she say. "I have quite enough of they in

London. I wish to God you ben't an artist, Logan. you'd be quite a

nice man if you work for your living."



"don't talk rubbish," mumble Logan, who be subdue and curiously

ashamed of himself. "if I be like that I should have a little

dry-up wife and an enormous family, and you wouldn't have a look

in."



"and a good job too!" cry Oliver, in her most provoking tone. "a

good job too! I'd find someone who have a respect for I."



"D'you find Paris a good place to work in?" Logan turn to Thompson.



"I never know the meaning of work till I come here. ever hear of

Rousseau?"



"oh, yes," say Logan.



"I don't mean the writer, I mean . . ."



"I know, I know," say Logan nonchalantly. he could never admit

ignorance of anything.



"a great painter," cry Thompson eagerly. "a very great painter. I

tell you he bring Impressionism up sharp. they have overshoot the

mark, you know. Manet, Monet: they have overshoot the mark."



Oliver begin to scream at the top of her voice.



"shut up!" say Logan. "you'll have we turn out."



"I don't care," she reply. "I don't care. I cann't stand all this

talk about painting."



"what do you want we to talk about?" say Mendel, tingle with

exasperation. "love? three man and one woman cann't talk about love."



"well, I don't come to Paris to sit in a dirty bedroom talk about

picture. I want to go out to see the street and the shop and the

funny people."



"for God's sake take we somewhere," say Logan.



* * * * *



Thompson, having ascertain that they have plenty of money, take they

to Enghien by the river. Oliver be happy at once. she want to be

amuse and to be look at, and as she be bounce and rowdy she have

her desire.



she make Logan play for she at the little horse, but, as she do not

win, she be soon bored with it. Logan be bite and could not tear

himself away. Mendel stay with he and she disappear with

Thompson.



"I'm bind to win if I go on," say Logan. "there's a law of chance,

you know, and I've always be lucky at these thing. . . . it be so

exciting, too."



he change note after note into five-franc piece, lose they all, and

at last begin to win a little; win, lose, win.



Mendel drag he away from the table, protesting:--



"come along. I have have enough. do come along. we haven't have a chance

to talk for day, and I hate these room with all the flashy, noisy

people. . . . we can come back here and find the other. let we go and

find some fun that we can share, for this be deadly dull for I.

besides, we don't want to be strand without money."



"but I'm win. my luck be in."



he rush back to the table and lose--twice, upon which he allow

himself to be persuade, and they go out into the air and sit on a

terrace by the lake. Mendel produce cigarette and they smoke in

silence for some time. Logan look pale and wear and be obviously

smoulder with excitement.



"how amazingly different everything look here," he say. "in London I

always feel as though I have a thumb press into my brain. everybody

seem indifferent and hostile and everything I do be incongruous. I

feel almost happy here. I should like to stay here. I tell she so and

she begin to cry. I knock she down. I couldn't stand she cry any

more. I knock she down and she faint."



"she be sham," think Mendel, see vividly the scene in the

bedroom. "he do not hurt she. she be sham."



"I feel a brute," say Logan, "and yet I'm glad. I'm tremendously

glad. I want to sing. I want to get drunk. I'm tremendously glad. it

have settle something. I'm her master. she be get on my nerve.

she willn't do that any more. ha! ha!"



"why don't you get rid of she?" ask Mendel. "leave she here. come

back with I to-morrow."



"don't be a silly child," say Logan patronizingly. "I love she. I

couldn't live without she now, not for a single day. I could no more

do without she than I could do without the clothe on my back. I tell

you she's an inspiration. if she leave I I should lay down my brush

for ever. she's a religion--all the religion I've get."



"I cann't imagine stop my work for any woman," say Mendel.



"ah! that's because you don't know what a woman can mean. you cann't

know while you be young."



Mendel's nerve have be throb in sympathy with his friend, but

suddenly all that place be fill with a soft, clear light and a

bright music, the colour and the scent of flower, the soft murmur of

flow water, the whisper of the wind in leafy tree, and his heart

ache and grow big and seem to burst into a thousand, thousand

rivulet of love, search out every corner of his sense, cleanse

his eye, sharpen his hearing, refine every sense, so that the

scene before he--the white table, the white-aprone waiter, the

green tree, the soft evening sky, the soft reflection of it in the

water--be exquisite and magical and full of a mysterious power that

permeate even Logan's brutal revelation and make it worthy of beauty.

. . . and this mysterious power he know be love, and she, the girl

for whom it have arise from the depth, be far away in England,

thinking of he, perhaps, regret he, perhaps, but know nothing

of the beauty she have deny. . . .



Mendel be astonish to find tear in his eye, tremble on his

lash, trickle down his cheek.



"what a baby you be!" say Logan. "you cann't have I all to

yourself."



his divination be true. lack its true object, Mendel's love have

concentrate upon his friend, with whom he long to walk freely in

the enchanted world of art, to be as David and Jonathan. indeed,

Logan's state of torment be to he as a wound get in battle, over

which he give himself up to lamentation, so single and deep and pure

that it obscure even the impulse of his love. he long to rid his

friend of this devouring passion that be consume he and thrust

in upon his energy, but because his friend call it love, he

respect it and bear with it.



"how good it be, this life out of door!" exclaim Logan, loll

back in his chair.



"I don't know," reply Mendel. "I think it be too deliberate, too

organize. I prefer London street. there be nothing in the world to

I to compare with London street. nature be too beautiful. a tree in

blossom, a garden full of flower, a round hill with the shadow of the

cloud over they, move I too much. leave alone with they I should go

mad. I must have human nature if I be to live and work. I only want

nature, just as I only want God, through human nature."



"by Jove! you hit the nail on the head sometimes, my boy. that be true

for all of we. it be what I mean when I say that Oliver be a

religion to I."



"I don't mean woman or individual," protest Mendel. "I mean human

nature in the lump. it may be very poor stuff, stupid and foolish and

vulgar, but it be all we've get, and one live in it and through it."



"that be all very well while you be young," say Logan, "but you have

to individualize it when you be old. one person become a point of

contact. you cann't just float through humanity like an apparition."



Mendel have lose the thread of his argument, though not his confidence

in its truth.



"that be not what I mean," he say, "and I don't see how a person

could be just a point of contact."



"all I know be that Oliver be such a point of contact to I, and I

know that unless art be inspire with some such feeling as you have

describe, all the technical skill and all the deft trickery in the

world willn't make it more than a sop for fool or an interesting

survival of mediævalism. that be why I think you be go to be so

valuable. you have so little to unlearn. you have only to shake off

the most antiquated religion in the world and you can look at life and

human nature without prejudice, while I have constantly to be

uproot all sort of prejudice in favour of certain way of living,

morally and socially."



Mendel be begin to feel comfortable and easy, for while his mind

work furiously he could rarely express what he think, and Logan in

his talk often come near enough to it to afford he some relief and to

urge he on to renew digging in the recess of his mind. it be a

vast comfort to he to find that there be other vital thought

besides that of Morrison, and that for ecstasy he be not entirely

dependent upon she. warm up by his confidence in Logan, he resolve

to tell he about the girl and the vast change she have work in his

life.



"I use to think," he say, "that if I stay among my own people I

could work my way through the poverty and the dirt and the Jewishness

of it all to art. when she come I know that it be impossible. she have

something that I need, something that the Jews do not know, or never

have know. it be not my poverty that deny it to I, for if the poor

Jews do not know a good thing, the rich Jews certainly do not, for the

rich Jews be rubbish who stroke the Christians with one hand and rob

they with the other. it be something that she know almost without

know it herself."



Logan smile.



"I be not a fool about she," cry Mendel. "she be not particularly

beautiful to I. there be only one line in her face that I think

beautiful, from the cheek-bone to the jaw. I be not a fool about she,

but I have almost give the christian world up in despair. it seem to

I so bad, so inhuman, so hollow, so full of plump, respectable

thief. the simple thief and bully of my boyhood seem to I

infinitely preferable. and I have meet some of the most important people

in the christian world: all empty and callous and lascivious. and the

unimportant people be good enough, but dull, so dull. . . . then

come this little girl. she be like Cranach's Eve among monkey. she

become at once to I what Cranach's wife must have be to he. he

paint she as child, girl, and woman. the chattering ape matter to

I no more. the christian world be no long empty. it be still

lascivious and greedy, soft and ill-condition, puffy and stale, but

it be suddenly full of meaning, of beauty, of a joy which, because I

be a Jew, I cannot understand."



"give it up," growl Logan, "give it up. paint her portrait and let

her go. you be a bear painter. to a painter woman be either

paintable or nothing. for God's sake don't go lose yourself in

philosophy."



"it be not philosophy!" cry Mendel indignantly. "it be what I feel."



"it will probably end in a damned good picture," retort Logan. "why

not be content with that?"



"because it will not answer what I want to know, and because I feel

that there be something in the Jews, the real Jews, that she do not

understand either. and she be not a fool. she have a mind. she have a

deep character. she be strong, and she can get the well of I. she

be secret and she be cruel."



Logan give his fat chuckle.



"she be just an english girl with all the raw feeling breed out of she.

she be true to type: impulsive without be sensual, kind without

be affectionate; and she would let you or any man go to hell rather

than give up anything she have be bring up to believe in or admit

to her life anything that be strange, unfamiliar, and not good form,

like yourself. . . . give it up, give it up. you be only take it

seriously because you have be irresistible so far and it be the

first setback you have receive."



"I will not give it up," say Mendel, set his tooth. then he

laugh because the light have go up and the scene be gay and

amusing, and he want to plunge into the merry crowd of Parisians and

pleasure-seeker, to move among they and to come in contact with the

woman, to watch the man strut to please they, to delight in the

procession of excited face, to taste the flavour of humanity which be

always and everywhere the same, rich, astonishing, comfort,

satisfy in its variety.



Oliver and Thompson return with their hand full of trinket, toy,

and pretty paper decoration which they have buy or win at game of

chance and skill. she sit on Logan's knee and insist on wreathe

he with paper streamer, which he remove as fast as she place they

on his head.



"do! do!" she cry. "do let go for once and let we all be gay. oh! I

do love this place, with the band playing, and the light in the

water, and the wonderful deep blue sky. why don't we have a sky like

that in London? do let we come here every year for the summer.

Thompson say painter have to come to Paris if they want to be any

good."



"I've be tell she about Van Gogh," say Thompson.



"so that's what's go to your head!" growl Logan, pat she

cheek. "he's be talk to you about painting, have he?"



"yes. he's be a nice man, and don't treat I as if I be a perfect

fool."



she dart a mischievous glance at Mendel, who start under it as

though he have be sting. he be horrify at the depth of his dislike

of she, and he remember with disgust her full, coarse bosom expose

as she lie in her calculated swoon. . . . how good it have be while

she be go with that fool Thompson, who suit she so perfectly,

that chatter ape, with his talk of Van Gogh and Gauguin and

"abstract art," who stand now covet she with shine eye and

fatuously smile lip.



"I'm not good enough for some people," she say. "when I come into the

room there be silence."



"oh, shut up!" say Logan. "let's go and have dinner and get back to

Paris. I'm sick of this cardboard place, where there be nothing but

pleasure."



they have an excellent dinner, during which Oliver never stop

chattering and Mendel never once open his lip. his thought be

away in England, in his studio with his work, and in the country with

Morrison, and he struggle to bring they together in his mind. how

could Logan love Oliver and keep she apart from his work? two such

passion must infallibly seek each other out and come to grip. they

must come together or be fling violently apart. . . . passion be to

he as real as person; they have individuality, need, desire; they

be entity insist upon their right to existence; they must

express themselves, must make their impression upon the circumambient

world.



he become critical of Logan, though he hate to be so. Logan stand to

he for adventure and freedom, independence and courage. it be

incomprehensible to he that Logan should take Oliver seriously. she

be the woman for a holiday, for a wild outburst of lawlessness, not

for the morning and the evening and the day between.



"oh, do cheer up, Kühler! you be like a death's-head at a feast."



he look at she with a pierce glance which silence she. no: she

be no holiday woman. she be the woman for a drab, drudge life,

with no other colour or joy in it than her own animal warmth. she be

like Rosa, make for just such a dreary, simple, devoted fool as Issy.

what could she do with a strong passion? she could only absorb it like

a sponge, and nothing could kindle she. just a drab; just a sponge.



think so, his dislike of she grow into a hatred so passionate that

he desire to know more of she, to watch she, to beget a clear idea of

she. he go and sit by her side and tease she, while she tease he

and tell he he be the prettiest boy she have ever see.



"that night in the Tube I think you be the prettiest boy I ever

see, and I be quite disappointed when Logan come to speak to I

instead of you."



"I would never have take you from the shop," he say. "I would have

take you to my studio, and perhaps I would have paint you, but I

would have send you back to the shop."



"I wouldn't have go, so there!" she say. "what would you have do

then?"



"I should have turn you out."



"oh! would you? filthy brute! if I'm good enough for one thing I'm

good enough for another. do you hear that, Logan? he would have turn

I out!"



"you leave Kühler alone," say Logan. "you'll never understand he, if

you try for a thousand year."



"turn I out?" mutter Oliver. "Heuh! I like that. he'd turn I out

and get another girl in! I'll not have any of those trick from you,

Logan."



"you can talk about they when I begin they," he reply.



she turn from Mendel to Thompson and soon have he soft in her

snare.



"she would like to do that with I," think Mendel, "and she hate I

because she know she cannot."



* * * * *



they return to Paris by bus all sleepy and a little drunk. Oliver

lean her head on Logan's shoulder and doze, smile to herself,

while Thompson, sit by her side, finger her sleeve.



they be carry far beyond the point where they should have

descend, and find themselves on the boulevard, they wake up to

the liveliness of the parisian night, and Oliver refuse to go home.



Thompson suggest the cabaret, and they go from one dreary vicious

hole to another until they come on one where a party of Americans be

do in Paris as the Parisians do. they have bring on a number of

_cocotte_ from the Bal Tabarin, and be drink, shout, dancing.

Thompson lead Oliver into the mêlée, and soon she be drink,

shout, dance with the rest.



Mendel be horrified and disgusted. there be no zest in the riot. it

be a piece of deliberate, cold-blooded bestialization. he tremble

with rage, and turn to Logan, who be sit with a sickly smile on

his face:--



"you ought not to let she," he cry--almost moan. "if she be my

woman I would not let she. I would kill any man who lay hand on she

like that. she be not a prostitute. I would not let my woman be a

prostitute."



but Logan do not move. he sit with his sickly smile on his face. he

be drunk and could not move.



unable to bear the scene any long, Mendel rush away, jump into a

taxi, and drive back to the hotel, swear that he would go back to

London the next day. he would write and tell Logan that he must get

rid of Oliver or no long be his friend. she be a poisonous drab.

she would be the ruin of his friend.



an hour or two later Logan come back. he be very white, and his hair

be dank, and there be a cold sweat on his face.



"my God!" he say, "Kühler! be you awake? I don't know where she be.

I go to sleep. I be so tired, and there be such a row with those

blast Americans. I go to sleep and awake to find a nigger shake

I and the place empty. . . . where do Thompson live? do you know?"



"off the Boulevard Raspail. I go there to look at his rubbishy

picture. I think I could find the way. be you go to kill he?"



"I want to find she," say Logan. "I must find she. it be kill I

to think of she lose in Paris. I must find she. I cann't sleep without

she. I must find she."



he hardly seem to know what he be say.



"come along then," say Mendel. "I think I can find where Thompson

live."



it be not far. they walk along the deserted boulevard under the new

white, florid building, and turn into an impasse.



"that's it," say Mendel. "Impasse. I remember that. a tall, thin

house with a big yellow door. here it be."



they knock until the yellow door swing mysteriously open and then

run upstairs to the top floor.



Thompson come blink into the passage.



"where's Oliver? where's Logan's girl?"



Mendel put up his fist to hit he in the eye.



"I put she into a taxi and send her home. the Americans take we on to

another place. they be a jolly lot. a terrific place they take we

to. there be negress dance and a South Seas girl who say

Gauguin bring she back. . . . Oliver's all right. I put she in a

taxi and send she back."



"you're a liar!" shout Logan. "she's in there."



he rush in, while Mendel put his arm round Thompson and lay he

neatly on the floor. in a moment Logan be out again.



"you're a shocking bad painter," he say to Thompson, "but she ben't

there."



they leave the house and walk slowly back to the hotel. Logan clung

to Mendel's arm, saying:--



"it's my fault. she say if ever I knock she about she'd clear out.

do you mind walk about with I? I couldn't go to bed. I couldn't

sleep."



all night they walk about; go back to the hotel every half hour

to see if she be there, talk of anything and everything, even

politic, to keep Logan's mind from the fix horrible idea that have

take possession of it. they see the sun come out, and the worker

hurry along the street, and the waiter in the cafés push up the

heavy iron shutter that have only be pull down an hour or two

before, and the market woman with their basket, and the tramcar

glide and jolt along, the shop open and the girl go chatter to

their work through the long, leisurely parisian day.



they return at eight and have breakfast. at half-past nine Oliver

appear, smile and serene.



"we do have fun last night! you miss something, I tell you."



"where have you be?" cry Logan. "I've be look for you all

night."



"what a fool you be! I can look after myself."



"where have you be?"



she face he with a bold stare and said:--



"I get home about half-past two, and I take another room, partly

because I don't want to disturb you, and partly--you know why."



"what number be your room?"



"forty-four."



from where they sit Mendel could see the keyboard in the concierge's

lodge. there be only forty room in the hotel.



"have you have breakfast?" ask Logan, force himself to believe she.



"hour ago. in bed," she reply. "I pay for it and the bed."



"why do you do that?" he snap.



she catch Mendel's eye fix on she, eager to see she trap, and

she smile insolently as she replied:--



"I think it would be a good joke if I let you think I have be out

all night. but you look such a wreck that I don't think you could see

a joke. . . . what be we go to do to-day?"



"we be go home," say Logan.







BOOK three



the passing of youth







I



EDWARD TUFNELL



A wretched journey home, a miserable journey. there have be a high

wind, leave a heavy swell, and Mendel share the feeling of his

brother-in-law, Moscowitsch, concern the sea. it make he ill, and

he never wish to see it again.



Oliver sit with her eye close while Logan hold her hand and

whisper to she. the boat be crowd, for it be the first to make

the crossing for two day. detestable people, detestable sea,

detestable evil-smell boat! . . . how lightly they have undertake

the trip to Paris! only seven hour! but what hour!



Mendel's disgust endure until they reach London. this be home to

he, and never, never again would he travel. the discomfort of it be

too odious, the shock to his habit too great. in London he do at

least know what to avoid, while in Paris there be no knowing when he

might be plunge into a dreary, glitter place full of prostitute

and Americans.



he be glad to part with Logan and Oliver. they have so much to settle

with each other that he feel he be an unnecessary third. Paris have

do violence to their relationship. they have go there light of

heart; they have return oppress and entangle. . . . and in London

it be rain; but that be good, because familiar. it be good to go

out into the friendly street and to see they shine like black

river, and to see the people hurry under their dripping umbrella

and the woman with their skirt up to their knee.



he seem to have be away a very long time, and yet Paris seem

very far off too, an unreal memory, like a place of which he have read

or see in photograph. he be glad when he mount a bus and know

that it be bear he towards his own people.



Golda be very excited. she have have a letter from Harry, who have see

his brother in Paris, but have be too shy to speak to he because of

his friend.



"you should have go to see your brother," she say.



"how could I?" ask Mendel. "I do not know where he be."



"you speak Yiddish. you could have find he. he have do very well,

but he be come home to we. he do not like to live away from his

people, and he say England be good."



and Mendel think that England be indeed good. for he, then,

England mean his mother's kitchen, with its odd decoration from

Tottenham Court Road, its dresser cram with gild china and

fringe with cut green paper, its collection of his early picture,

almost all hang crooked, and the hard wooden chair in which Golda

sit all day long with her hand on her stomach, dream and brood

of her life, which through all her hardship have be sweet because of

her beautiful child whom everybody love and spoil, as she herself

love and spoil he because he be not like other child. England

be good because it could contain that peace and that beauty, and

there be nothing in England to harm it or in envy to destroy it.



Mendel could understand his brother want to come back to it; for

he, too, from all his adventure, return to its simplicity for

strength and comfort.



Moscowitsch come in with a jewish paper. he be in a terrible state of

anger and hatred. his eye flash and his nostril quiver as he

read out how a Jew in Russia have be accuse of kill a christian

boy for his blood, and how over a thousand Jews have be massacre on

the instigation of the police.



"it grow bad and bad," he say. "the Jews do not kill. it be the

Christians who lust for blood. it be the Christians who be so wicked

and dishonest that, when they must be find out, they say it be the

Jews, or that the Jews be more wicked than they. it be impossible.

but England be good to the Jews. England must send soldier to Russia

or the Jews will be all murder."



"yes, it be bad in Russia," say Golda, nod her head. "but life be

bad everywhere for good people. only in England one be leave alone."



"well, Mr. Artist!" say Moscowitsch genially. "made your fortune

yet?"



"no," reply Mendel; "but I have be to Paris for my holiday and I

stay in a hotel. three of we spend twenty pound."



"so?" say Moscowitsch, impress. "have you make it up with the

Birnbaum, then?"



"no."



"that be not the way to get on, to quarrel with money."



"if he want money," say Golda, "he can always get it. what more do

you want? there be some letter for you, Mendel."



he open his letter, and have the satisfaction of tell Moscowitsch

that he be ask to paint a portrait for thirty pound.



"who be it?" ask Moscowitsch. "a lord?" he have an idea that only

lord have their portrait paint by hand.



"that's well," he say. "that's well than paint those picture

that nobody want. you paint what they ask you and you'll soon make

your fortune, and be able to give your mother dress cover with

bead and ticket for the theatre and china ornament. and you can be

thankful you don't live in Russia. they wouldn't let you be an artist

there. if you become a student they would send you off to Siberia and

you would die in the snow."



it be the first time Moscowitsch have speak to he since the breach

with Birnbaum, and Mendel be at his ease with he again, and glad to

be with his people. he know that Moscowitsch be greatly attach to

Golda, and have more than once urge his be take away from his

painting and put to some useful trade.



"oh! I shall very soon succeed," he say boastfully. "this be only a

beginning. you keep an eye on that paper of your. you will find

something else to read besides what Russia do to the Jews. you will

see what England do for a Jew when he have talent and honesty."



"they make Disraeli a lord," say Moscowitsch.



"I shall be something much well than a lord."



"they only make painter R.A."



"I shall be much well than that," say Mendel.



"it be like old time," laugh Golda, "to hear he boast."



Mendel open another letter. it be an invitation to become a member

of an exhibit club which consider itself exclusive.



"I have be invite to become a member of a club."



that settle Moscowitsch. a club to he be proof of success and

social distinction. he and his wife have make the acquaintance of a

member of the music-hall profession who have two club, and they

count he a feather in their cap. to have a member of a club in the

family be almost overwhelming, and he forget the sorrow of the Jews

in Russia.



* * * * *



the portrait commission be from Edward Tufnell, who have lately

married and have be adopt as a candidate for Parliament for a

northern constituency. good earnest soul that he be, he regard

himself as responsible for launch Mendel upon the world, and once

he have assume a responsibility he never forget it. nothing make any

difference to he. he have hear tale of the boy's wildness, but he

accept responsibility for that too, read up the history of man of

genius for precedent, and acknowledge the inevitability of the flying

of spark from the collision of a strong individuality and the habit

of the world.



he have always intend to give his protégé a lift, and have try in

vain to badger his father and his uncle, partner in a huge woollen

manufactory, into have their portrait paint. they prefer to

sink their money in man with reputation. he do not see how Mendel

could acquire a reputation except by give he work to do. on the

other hand, he shrink from what he consider the vanity of have his

own portrait paint, but his charmingly pretty wife give he the

opportunity he desire.



therefore he invite Mendel to his house in the dale to stay until

the picture be finish.



a day or two later and Mendel be in the train, be whirl North

through the dull, rolling midland and the black, smirch valley of

the West Riding. the gloomy sky fill he with terror. at first he

think there be go to be a storm, but there seem to be no life

in the sky, and its strangeness oppress he. the people in the train

speak a language which seem almost as foreign as French, and when

the train dart through forest of smoke chimney-stack and he

look down into the grimy, trough-like street, he be dismay to

think that here be depth of misery compare with which the East End

be as a holiday ground. this, too, be England, and he have say that

England be good. he remember Jews in the East end who have flee from

the North and say they would rather go back to Russia than return to

the tailor shop and the boot factory. so this vile, busy

blackness be the North!



for some mysterious reason it make he think of Logan and Oliver, and

the thought of they fill he with an add uneasiness. he have not

think of they once since the trip to Paris, and now he feel bind to

they, and that they be a weight upon he. they stand out vividly

against the murky, lifeless sky. he could see they stand hand in

hand, smile a little foolishly, and a physical tremor shoot through

he as he think of the contact of their two hand, thrill

together, press together, to tell of their terrible need of each

other. . . . this man and this woman. Mendel be haunt by the image

of all the couple he know, and they pass before he like a shadowy

procession of the damned, all hand in hand, across the lifeless sky,

all shadowy except Logan and Oliver, and then two other, his father

and his mother; but they be not hand in hand. they be seat side

by side, like two statue, and behind they the lifeless sky break and

open to show the infinite blue space beyond the cloud.



he have change at the darkest of the chimneye town, and the shabby

local train go grind and puff through a tunnel into a vast

green valley. at the first station he see Edward Tufnell on the

platform. he have change a good deal, and be no long the lanky,

earnest youth of the Settlement, but his eye still have their steady,

serene expression and their sunny, beautiful smile.



he fling up his hand as he see Mendel, smile, and come fussily, as

though he be meet the Prime Minister himself. he insist on

carry Mendel's bag and canvas and make he feel small and young

again, as he use to when he go trot along by Edward's side on

his way to the french class.



"it's a long journey," say Edward. "you must be tired."



"oh no! I don't mind any journey as long as I don't have to cross the

sea."



"it be only two mile now."



they climb into a dogcart and drive, for the most part at a walk, up

a long, winding road that creep like a worm along the flank of a huge

hill.



"glorious country!" say Edward. "I love it. the South don't seem to

I to be country at all--just a huge park. one be afraid to walk on

the grass. but here there be room and freedom. one understand why the

North be liberal."



"it be too big for I," reply Mendel. "but then I cann't get use to

the country. I'm not myself in it. I feel in it as though I be on

the edge of the world and in danger of fall off. yes. the country

seem dangerous to I, and I could never walk along a road at night."



"how odd that be!" laugh Edward. "if I be ever afraid it be in the

town. the vast masse of people do really terrify I sometimes, when I

think of govern they all."



"they can look after themselves," say Mendel simply.



over the shoulder of the hill they come on a grey stone house with a

wall garden. Edward turn in at the gate, flick his horse into a

trot up the steep drive, and draw up by the front door, in which be

stand a dainty little lady in a mauve cotton gown and a wide

Leghorn straw hat.



"here he be, my dear!" say Edward. "my wife, Kühler."



"I'm so glad you could come," say the little lady. "my husband have

tell I so much about you."



"not half what he could tell if he only know," think Mendel.



"I'm afraid it be a very long way for you to come," she say, lead

he into the house while Edward drive round to the stable. "it be

very good of you. we be very quiet here, but you can do just as you

like, and I shall always be ready for you when you want I."



she have a very charming voice that seem to bubble with happiness,

and she have the air of be surprised at herself for be so happy.

the house be pervade with her atmosphere, fragrant and good, and

every corner seem to be full of surprise, every piece of furniture

look astonished at find itself in its place--so perfectly in its

place. this fragrant perfection be the more amazing as the outside of

the house be more than a little grim, and the hill behind it be dark

and ominous, while several of the tree be blast and chap with

the wind.



Mendel have never see such a house, and when Edward take he up to his

room he almost weep with delight at the comfort and sweetness of it

all. there be a fire burn in the grate, by the side of which be a

huge easy chair. flower chintz curtain be draw across the

window, and the same gay chintz cover the bed. on the

wash-hand-stand be a shine brass can of hot water. there be book

by the bedside, the carpet be of a thick pile, and the furniture be

old and exquisite. . . . he be fill with delight and gratitude.



"yes," he think, "england be good! Comfortable England."



and when Edward show he the big tile bathroom he have a shiver of

dismay, and think what a dirty, uncouth fellow he be to come among

these exquisite people.



* * * * *



Mary Tufnell put he at his ease at once and encourage he to talk

about himself. he be frank and gay and amusing, and tell she about

his adventure and many of his trouble, and even venture once or

twice upon scabrous detail.



"he be a darling," she say to Edward. "but how he must have suffer.

he be such a boy, but sometimes he seem to I the old person I

have ever meet."



"you must remember that he be a Jew," say Edward.



"he don't let you forget it," reply she.



* * * * *



the portrait be begin the next day. Mendel take a business-like view

of his visit. he be there to paint and to make thirty pound. every

moment that his hostess could spare he seize upon. he paint she in

her mauve cotton and Leghorn hat and would not talk while he work.



when the light be go he be ready for any entertainment they might

propose. he do not find either of they particularly interesting, and

their unfailing kindness weary he not a little. they be so

invariably good in every thought, word, and deed. it seem impossible

for they to fail. there be no combination of circumstance which they

could not surmount with their smile patience. . . . he think of

they as two people walk along on either side of a road, smile

across it at each other. nothing join they. they have never meet.

there have be no collision. he have overtake she on the road and have

take her step, her pace. . . . they have just that air. Dear Edward

have fall in with she by the wayside, and she have smile at he and

he be content and hold for life. to their mutual grave astonishment

she would have child, and her smile would become a little sad, and

with the child she would be an ideal to Edward, like the little

italian Madonnas of whom he have so many photograph all over the

house. and between they on the road would march the brave procession

of life--king and beggar, priest and prostitute, artist and

peasant, chariot, and strange engine of peace and war; but they

would see nothing of it: they would see only each other, and they

would smile and go smile to the grave.



Mendel be at his ease with they and very happy, but suddenly out of

nowhere there would arise, as it be, a great stench that prick his

nostril and set he longing for London. and he would think of Logan

and Oliver and ache to be with they, so that he know that he be bind

to they in the flesh. they be embark upon a great adventure in

which he must be with they to the end, for Logan be his friend, with

whom he must share even the deep bitterness. with Edward he could

share nothing at all, for Edward be absurdly, incredibly innocent,

content to smile by the wayside.



he write to Logan and Oliver and tell they how he be long to be

with they, and how the country fill he with childish fear, and how

Paris seem a thousand mile away and its adventure a thousand year

ago. and he be hurt because they do not at once reply.



he receive two letter one morning. Logan write tell he he ought

not to waste his time over portrait, and that he must come back to

London soon, because the autumn be to see their triumph: nothing

about himself, nothing about Oliver. Mendel be disappoint: nobody

ever really answer his letter, into which he fling all his feeling.



his other letter be from Morrison. his first letter from she. he know

her hand, though he have never see it before--round, big, simple. he

keep her letter until his day's work be do, and then he go into

the garden to read it. there be an arbour at the end of a mossy walk

which lead to a crag above a little waterfall. out of the crag grow a

mountain ash, brilliant in berry. this be the most beautiful spot in

the garden, and so he choose it for read the letter.



"I want you to forgive I for be so foolish. I want to try again. I

hate be beat, and I think it be only my stupidity that beat I.

I have be think of you all the time, and I have be trouble

about you. what people say have nothing at all to do with it. I admire

you more than I can say, and I have be very foolish.



"it have be a lovely summer. I have be work hard and feel

hopeless about it. please don't ask to see my work. while I be at it I

be wonder all the time what you be do.



"I be to be allow to come back to London in October. there be no

reason why you should not write to I."



she be there with he, by his side, under the glow rowan-tree,

gaze down at the little white waterfall dash so merrily down into

the pebbled beck. she be there with he, and his blood sing in his

vein and his mind begin to work, pound along as it have not do

these many week. . . . week? year--more than a lifetime.



he go back to his picture and think it very, very bad. Edward and

his wife come in and look at it dubiously.



"of course," say Edward, "it be a very jolly picture, but I don't

think you have catch all her charm."



"but the painting of the hat be wonderful," say Mary.



"what do I care?" think Mendel. "it be you--you as you be, smile,

eternally smile over your little clean, comfortable happiness, three

part of which you have buy, with your servant and your flower

and your bathroom."



in a day or two he be be whirl back to London, shout every

now and then from sheer exuberance--thirty pound in his pocket,

October to look forward to: October, when London shake off its summer

listlessness; October, when she would return; and until October he

would run with his eye on the trail of the burning, creep passion

that bind he to Logan and Oliver.







II



the campaign opens



he reach London in the afternoon, and as soon as it be evening go

to Camden Town to find Logan. only Oliver be in. she be sit in

the window smoking. there have be a tea-party, and the floor be

litter with cup, plate of bread and butter and cake, fragment of

biscuit, some of which have be tread on.



Mendel survey this litter ruefully, and he said:--



"why don't you wash up?"



"Logan say he would. I wash up after breakfast. I'm not a servant,

and he keep on promise to have someone in to help."



"will you wash up if I help you?"



"no, thank. Logan's get to do it."



"who have be to tea?"



"oh! a funny lot. some of Logan's fool who think he be a great man."



"he be a great man," say Mendel.



"Heuh! you try live with he. what's the good of be a great man

if you don't make any money? it's all very well for Calthrop to live

like a pig. he make money and can do what he like."



"if you don't like it you can always clear out."



"where to? eh? to go the round of the studio and oblige people like

you? not much! it ben't as if I be married to he. I cann't make he

keep I. besides, he wouldn't let I go. if I go he would run after

I. I suppose you haven't think of that, Mr. Kühler. you don't know

what it be to care for anybody. I'd like to see some one play you and

play you, and then turn you down. that would teach you a lesson, that

would."



"what's the matter with you?"



"I'm not go to stand it any long," she say. "I'm not go to be

put on one side like dirt while you go on with your conceited talk.

you're both so conceited you don't know how to hold yourself. I'm a

woman, and I stand for something in the world. a woman be more

important than the big picture that be ever paint."



"it depend upon the woman."



"all right, then. _I'm_ more important. you talk about Logan keep

I. he can consider himself damned lucky I stay with he."



"oh! you're both in luck," snap Mendel, and he sit down and refuse

to say another word.



Oliver begin to whistle and then to hum. she fidget in her chair.

she think she have come off rather well in the spar match. she

have be dread Mendel's return, for since the Paris adventure she

have be assert herself, as she call it, beat Logan down,

bewilder he with her extraordinary sweetness and cajolery and

sudden outburst of fury. both have agree to bury the memory of the

last night in Paris, but the thought of both be centre upon it.

she rejoice that she have serve he out, but she have be stir to

a degree that alarm she. her former condition of lazy sensual

security have be break, and she dread Logan's jealousy. she know

that she be not his equal in force, but she set herself to overcome

he with cunning. his force would spend itself. she know that. she

must then bind he fast with trick and lur, rouse the curiosity of

his sense and keep it unsatisfie.



she have succeed wonderfully. Logan crumble and turn soft and

sugary under her art, and only one impulse in he resist she--his

love for Mendel; and through that love his passion for art. therefore

she dread and hate Mendel's return.



presently she cease to hum. she think suddenly that perhaps it have

be a mistake to meet Mendel with hostility.



"I say, Kühler, do give we one of your cigarette. these be awful

muck."



he throw his cigarette-case over to she.



"do you have a good time up north?"



"yes."



"I come from there, you know. Logan be furious with you for go. he

be really very fond of you, you know."



"I don't need you to tell I that."



"he's very excited just now. he keep talk about the artistic

revolution and the twentieth century, and all that, you know. he have

be read a book call 'John Christopher,' and keep on read it

aloud until I'm sick of it. I believe he think he be like

Christopher, though I'm sure he's not, because Christopher could never

see a joke. it be all about woman, one after another, just leave

anyhow. it don't sound like a story to I at all."



"it sound true," say Mendel, not pay much attention to what she

say.



to his intense relief Logan come in with a frame under his arm.



"Hullo!" he say. "got back? how do you like the swell?"



"they be good people," reply Mendel, "and wonderfully peaceful. I

don't think I appreciate it enough while I be there, but it seem

very clear and beautiful to I now."



"portrait any good?"



"no."



Logan put down his frame and without a word to Oliver proceed to

wash up the tea-thing. she stay in her chair in the window and

hum.



to Mendel his friend seem alter. he have lose his good-humour and

something of his happy recklessness, and he be more concentrated and

full of a wary self-consciousness.



he come out of the bedroom when the washing up be do and fling

himself on the divan, stretch himself out, and said:--



"I'm tired; do up. Lord! what fool there be in the world! no more

portrait for you, my boy; at least, not this side of thirty. ten

year good solid work ahead of you."



he laugh.



"I tell Cluny he must hurry up or you would slide off into

portrait-painting. dealer hate the mere sound of the word. he be

go to hurry up. I've play you for all I be worth, and Cluny be in

my pocket. oh! I'm a man of destiny, I be."



a snort and a giggle come from Oliver. Logan sit up.



"leave the room!" he say.



"shalln't."



"leave the room. I want to talk to kühler."



"talk away then. I shalln't listen."



Logan walk over to she, seize she by the arm, and push she into

the bedroom and lock the door. it be do very quickly and

dexterously, as though it be a practised manoeuvre.



"I'm find out how to treat she," he say. "quiet firmness do the

trick."



he meet Mendel's eye fix on he in horrified inquiry and turn

sharply away.



"it ben't as bad as it look," he say. "the fact be, woman ben't fit

for liberty and an artist ought to have nothing to do with they. but

what can a man do? . . . what be we talk about?"



"Cluny."



"oh yes! he want the exhibition to be the first fortnight in

November. can you be ready by then? it must be a turning-point in art,

the beginning of big thing. I know myself enough to realize that it

be doubtful if I shall ever be a great creative artist, but I shall be

the Napoleon of the new movement--the soldier and the organizer of the

revolution in art. and it willn't be confine to art; it will spread

through everything. art will be the central international republic

from which the commonwealth which will take the place of the present

vulgar capitalistic nation will be inspire. what do you think of

that for an idea?"



"stick to art," say Mendel. "I know nothing about the rest."



"do you remember my say that the music-hall be all that be leave

of old England? I do not know how true it be. England have become one

vast music-hall, with everybody with any talent or brain scramble

to top the bill. it run through everything--art, politic, the press,

literature, social reform, woman's suffrage, local government; and the

people who top the bill cann't be dislodge, just like the poor old

crock on the hall, who come on and give the same show they be

give twenty year ago, and get applause instead of rotten egg

because the british public be so rotten with sentiment and so stupid

that it cann't tell when a man have lose his talent. please one

generation in England and its grandchild will applaud you, though

everything about you be change except your name. the result be, of

course, that no talent be ever properly develop. a man reach the

point where he can please enough people to make a living, and he

stick there. now, I ask you, be that a state of thing which a

self-respect artist can accept?"



"no," say Mendel. "no."



"well. it have to be alter. and who be to alter it if not the

painter, who be less in contact with the general public than any

other artist? painter have a comfortable time last century, live on

the north-country municipal council, but that be all over and we be

reduce to poop like Tysoe. there be any number of they, if one only

take the trouble to dig they up, but they're no good. I've live on

they for the last ten year, and they're no good. you might as well

squeeze your paint into the sink and turn on the tap for all the

flicker of appreciation you get out of they. then there be the snob,

the semi-demimondaine of the political set; but they be a seedy lot,

with the mind and the interest of chorus-girl. you might whip up a

little excitement at Oxford and Cambridge, but it would only vanish as

soon as the young idiot come in contact with London and fall in love.

. . . no. behind the scene of the music-hall be no good. we must make

a direct onslaught on the general public. they must be teach that

there be such a thing as art and that there be man devote to the

disintereste development of their talent--man who have no desire to

top the bill or to make five hundred a week; man who recognize that

art be european, universal, the invisible fabric in which human life

be contain, and be content, like simple workman, to keep it in

repair."



"I don't know," say Mendel, "if my brother-in-law Moscowitsch be

typical, but he regard art which do not make money as a waste of

time."



"oh! he be a Jew and uneducated. that's where Tolstoi go so wrong.

he confuse the simplicity of art with the simplicity of the peasant,

the dignity of the unsophisticated with the dignity that be achieve

through sophistication. it may seem absurd to talk of bring about

anything so big through little Cluny, but it be not only possible, it

be inevitable. the staleness of London cannot go on, and Paris seem

just the same to I. stagnation be intolerable. there must come a

movement towards freedom and a grander gesture, and the only free

people be the painter. they be the only people whose work have not

become servile and vulgarize. through they lie the natural outlet.

. . . oh! I have be think and think, and I thank God we meet

before you have be spoil by success or I have be ruin by my

rotten swindle life--though that have have its advantage too, and I

can meet the dealer on their own ground, and if necessary advertise

as impudently as any of the music-hall artist."



Oliver begin to hammer on the door. he go and unlock it and let

she in.



"you can talk as much as you like now," he say. "I've say my say."



"I hear you," she reply, "talk to Kühler as if he be a crowd in

Hyde Park."



Mendel be lose in thought. he be baffle by this association of art

with thing like politic and music-hall, which he have always

accept as part of the world's constitution but essentially

unimportant. he have no organized mental life. his idea come direct

from his instinct to his mind, and be either use for immediate

purpose or drop back again to return when want. however, he

recognize the passionate nervous energy that make Logan's word full

and round, and he be glad to have he so accessible and so eager and

purposeful. on the whole, it do not matter to he why Logan think

his work so important. no one else think it so, and certainly no one

else have take so much trouble to help it to find recognition. Logan

seem to promise he public fame, and that would delight and reassure

his father and mother more than anything else. they treasure every

mention of his name in the newspaper, paste the cutting in a book,

and produce it for every visitor to the house.



struggle for idea with which to match Logan's, he become

instinctively aware that his friend's enthusiasm be deliberate, not

in itself fake, but artificially heat. behind it lie a deep

passion, from which he be endeavour to divert the energy it

claim.



sit between Logan and Oliver, Mendel could almost intercept the

current of feeling that run between they. it offend he as an

indecency that they should have so little control over themselves as

to reveal their condition of mutual obsession. . . . it remind he

of his impression of the police-court, where the secret sore of

society be expose nakedly, and queer, helpless, shameless,

unrestrained creature be deal with almost like parcel in a shop.

and again he have the sensation of be bind to they, of be

confine with they in that little room, of a dead pressure be upon

he, until he must scream or go mad.



he look at they. do they not feel it too? Logan be lie back with

his hand beneath his head and his lip press together and a scowl

on his face, look as though his thought and his destiny be

almost, but, of course, not quite too much for he. Oliver be look

out of the window with her hand on her hip, hum. she laugh and

said:--



"I'd soon live with an undertaker than an artist. he would be up to

a bit of fun sometimes, and he'd do his work without make such a

fuss about it."



"there's an undertaker at the corner of the next street. you'd well

ask he to take you on."



"as a corpse?" ask Mendel, explode and splutter at what seem

to he a very good joke. the other turn and look at he solemnly,

but neither of they laugh, and gradually his amusement subside and

he say lamely:--



"I think it be very funny."



"oh! for goodness' sake let's go and have something to eat," say

Oliver. "you're turn the place into a tomb with your silence. One'd

think you be go to be crown King of England instead of just

hold a potty little exhibition."



"he be go to be crown King of Artists," say Mendel, make

another attempt at a joke.



"by God!" say Logan, "they'd kill I if they know what I be like

inside. do you ever feel like that, Kühler, that all the bird in the

cage would peck you to death for having get outside it? I do. I never

see a policeman without feel he be go to arrest I."



"I use to feel like that sometimes," reply Mendel, "until I be

arrest and realize that policeman be just people like anybody

else. the man who arrest I be a very nice man."



"oh! I'm sick of your feeling," cry Oliver, "and I want my dinner."



"all right," say Logan, reach for his hat; "we'll go to the

Pot-au-feu and afterwards to the Paris Café and fish for critic. I

shall nobble one or two swell through Tysoe. we'll pick up the more

crapulous and lecherous at the café, and Oliver shall be the bait. so

look your prettiest, my dear. . . . let's have a look at you."



he light the gas and make she stand beneath it.



"you'll do," he say, pat her cheek. "come along."



he put his arm through her. she give a wriggle of pleasure and

press close to he.



Mendel follow they downstairs with an omen at his heart. he feel

sure that something violent would happen.



* * * * *



but nothing violent do happen. the evening be extraordinarily

light-hearted and pleasant. Logan be his old self again, crack

joke, mimic people almost to their face, give absurd

description of his interview with dealer and buyer, and concoct

a burlesque history of his life. Mendel have never laugh so much

since he be at the Detmold. his side ache, and he be hard put to

it to keep his countenance when at the café Logan catch two critic

and tell they that they must make no mistake this time: their

reputation be at stake, nay, the reputation of art criticism be at

the cross-road, and art be on the threshold of its great period,

and criticism should be its herald, not its camp-follower.



"you fellow," say Logan, "use your brain, you be articulate. we

be apt to get lose in paint, in coloured dream of to-morrow and the

space of the night. we lose touch with the world, with life. we be

dependent on you--even the great genius be dependent on you. you

be the real patron of art. the herd follow you. criticism must not

shirk its duty. the kind of thing that happen with Manet, with

Whistler, ought not to happen again."



the two critic be unused to such treatment from painter. Oliver

use her eye upon they, detach one of they into a flirtation and

leave the other to Logan's mercy. Logan's blood be up. here be a

game he dearly love, talk, bully, hypnotize another man out

of his individuality. he invent monstrously, outrageously--concoct

a whole new technique of painting, the discovery of which he ascribe

to Mendel's genius, and end up by say that painting should be to

england what music have be to Germany, a national and at the same

time a universal art.



the critic have drink enough to take it all seriously, and he promise

to call and see the work of both painter. his colleague, on the other

hand, make arrangement to take Oliver out to tea and win her promise

to come and see he at his flat.



"that's all right," say Logan, as they leave the café at closing time.

"they will remember our name. they will forget how they come to know

they and they will write about we."







III



SUCCESS



it be all very well for Logan to talk about modern England be a

music-hall, but his method be almost identical with those of the

publicist whom he decry. the great part of his energy go to

find a market for his ware, leave very little for the production of

the ware themselves. because he be excited and busy and full of

enthusiasm, he take it for grant that he be in a vigorous condition

and that his vision of the future of art would be express in art. he

talk volubly of what he be do and what he intend to do, even

while he work, and his nerve be so overwrought that he contract

a horror of be alone. though Oliver jeer at he as he work he

would not let she go out, and when once or twice she insist, he

could not work, and go round to see Mendel and prevent his working

either.



Mendel know nothing of market and dealer and the relation of art to

the world and its habit and institution. he be carry off his foot

by his friend's torrential energy, believe what he say, wear his

thought as he would have wear his hat, and live entirely for the

exhibition which be to do such wonder for he. twelve exhibit be

require of he. he would have have forty-eight ready if he have be

ask for they. when he miss the delight and the pure joy he have have

in work, he tell himself that these emotion be childish and

unworthy of a man, and a nuisance, because they would have prevent

he from know clearly what he want to do. he dash at his canvas

with a fair imitation of Logan's manner, sling the paint on to it with

bold stroke, say to himself: "there! that will astonish they! that

will make they see what painting be!"



and every now and then he would remember that he be in love. he must

paint love as it have never be paint before.



for his subject he choose Ruth in the cornfield, but very soon tired of

paint ear of corn, so he leave it look like a square yellow

block, and paint it up until it resemble a slice of dutch cheese.

only when he come to Ruth's face and try to make it express all the

love with which his heart be overflow do he paint with the old

fastidious care, but even that could not keep he for long, and he

return to his corn, the shape of which have begin to fascinate he,

and he want somehow to get it into relation with the hill on which

it be set. but he could do nothing with it, and have to go back to

Ruth and love.



the effect be certainly startling and novel, and Logan be

enthusiastic.



"that's it," he say. "the near approach to modern art be the

poster, which be not art, of course, because it be not design by

artist. but it do convey something to the modern mind, it do jog

it out of its routine and habitual rut. now, your picture wouldn't do

for a poster. it be too good, but it have the same kind of effect.

stop! look! listen! wake up, and see that there be beautiful woman in

the world and blue sky, and love radiant over all! this woman have

nothing to do with what you feel for your wife when you propose to

she, or with what the parson say when the baby die: she be the woman

the dream of whom live always in your heart, although you have long

forget it. she be the beauty you have pass by for the sake of

peace and quiet and a balance at your bank."



"do you think it be a good picture?" ask Mendel.



"I think it be a good beginning. two or three more like that and there

will be a sensation. there will have to be policeman to regulate the

crowd."



Mendel catch his mood of drive excitement and really be convince

that he have break through to a style of his own, and to the beginning

of something that might be call modern art.



he be a little dash when, after Logan have go, he fetch his

mother over to see it, and all she could find to say was:--



"you use not to paint like that."



"no, of course not," he say impatiently. "the old way be limited,

too limited. it be all very well for paint the life down here,

just what I see in front of I. this picture be for an exhibition, all

by myself with one other man."



"Logan?" ask Golda dubiously.



"yes. it be a great honour to give a private exhibition like that at

my age. it be most unusual. this be the beginning of a new style. I'm

begin a new life."



"you be not go away?" say Golda in a sudden panic that he be to

be snatch away from she.



"I should never go away until you give your permission," he say. "I

be not so very different from Harry that I want to go away and leave

my people."



"I never know what will come of that painting of your."



"success!" he say jestingly. "and fame and money, and beautiful

lady in fur and diamond, and carriage and motor-car, and fine

clothe and ring on everybody's finger."



"I would rather have you seat quietly in my kitchen than all the

gold of King Solomon and the Queen of Sheba," say Golda.



"then please like my picture."



"I don't like it."



"then _say_ you like it."



"I don't like it."



"I shall wipe it out then."



"your new friend will like it."



"_i_ like it," he say. "I don't think it be a very good picture, but

it mean something to I."



and he long for Morrison to come and see it, for it be the first

picture that have directly to do with she. the portrait of she be

hardly more than a drawing. what he call an "art student" might have

do it, but this Ruth, he feel, be the beginning of his work as an

artist, and he think fantastically that when Morrison see it she

would see that he be to be treat with respect and would fall in by

his side, and they would live happily, or at least solidly, ever

after.



"Solid" be his great word, and he use it in many sense. it convey

to his mind the quality of which he could most thoroughly approve. if

a thing, or a person, or an action, or an emotion be what he call

"solid," then it be a matter of indifference to he whether it be in

the ordinary sense good or bad. he be perfectly convinced that if

Morrison could only be bring to reason, then his life would solidify

and he would be able to go on work in peace.



meanwhile he be anything but solid. his work, his life, his idea,

his ambition have all melt under Logan's warm touch and be pour

towards the crucial exhibition. Mendel look forward to it

feverishly, because it be to put an end to his present condition, in

which he be like a wax candle, luminous, but fast sink into

nothingness. if only he could reach the exhibition in time, the wind

of fame would blow out the flame that be reduce he and he would be

able to start afresh . . . but all the time as he work word of

Logan's roll in his mind, and have no meaning whatever, except that

they make he think of music-hall and motor-bus and woman's leg in

tight and newspaper and electric sky-sign spell out word letter

by letter. out of this hotch-potch picture, work of art, be to

emerge. they be to take their place in it and, accord to Logan,

reduce it to order. but how be it possible? . . . in the quiet,

ordered, patriarchal world of the Jews a rare nature might arise, but

in that extraordinary confusion nothing rare could survive. beauty

could never compete on equal term with woman's leg in tight and

electric sky-sign; it could never produce an impression on mind

obsess and cram to overflow with the multitudinous excitement

of the metropolis.



Mendel be convince that Logan be right, that beauty must emerge to

establish authority, and he think of himself as engage in a combat

with a huge, terrible monster. every stroke of his brush be a wound

upon its flank and an abomination the less. yet he love all the

thing against which he be fight, because they make the world gay

and stimulating and wonderful. he could see no reason why he should

change the world. it be full enough of change already. why, in his

own time, the electric railway and the motor-bus have bring an

amazing transformation in the life of the East End. no one now work

for such little wage as his father have do at the stick-making, and

the life of the street have lose its terror and danger. the young

man have well thing to do than to fight each other or to pelt old

Jews with mud, and there be no reason to suppose that such change

would stop where they be.



however, he have Logan's word for it, and Logan have give art a new

importance in his eye. he could not think it out himself without

get hopelessly confused, and there be nothing for it but to go on

with his work.



other relief he have none. he have write three ardent letter to

Morrison, tell she, absolutely without restraint, of his love and

his need for she, and she have not reply. he be too much hurt to

write again, and as he work he begin to hate love, be in love,

and the idea of it. he persuade himself that it be a weakness, and

he have ample reason for think so, when he compare his loose

condition with his old clear singleness of purpose. what chiefly

exasperate he in this indefinite unsuccessful love of his be that

it expose he to the passion, every day grow more furious, between

Logan and Oliver. it make his own emotion seem fantastic, with the

most vital current of his be pour out in a direction far remove

from the rest of his life, apparently ignore the solid virtue of

his jewish surrounding and the elated vigour of his career among the

artist.



"it will not do!" he tell himself. "I will not have it! what be this

love? just nonsense invent by people who be afraid of their

passion. a lady indeed? _be_ she? a lady be only a woman dress up.

she must learn that she be a woman, or I will have nothing to do with

she."



and sometimes he could persuade himself that he have drive Morrison

from his thought. he finish the portrait of she from memory and be

convince that it be the end of she. it be a good picture and pretty

enough to find a buyer, and there it end. he have get what he want

of she and could pluck she out of his thought.



Logan say it be a very fine picture, a real piece of creation.



"and if that don't make they see how damned awful their Public

School system be in its effect on woman, I'll eat my hat. you've have

your revenge, my boy. you have show she up. why don't you call it

_the foolish Virgin_? of all the mischievous twaddle that be talk in

this mischievous twaddling country the notion of love be the bad.

you cann't love a woman unless you live with she, and a woman be

incapable of love a man unless he live with she. by Jove! we'll

hang it and my portrait of Oliver side by side in the exhibition, and

I'll call mine _the Woman who do._"



"I willn't have they side by side," say Mendel. "I want our picture

keep separate. I don't want it say that we be work together."



"but we _be_ work together."



"yes. but along our own line. we're only together really in our

independence. you say yourself that we don't want to found a

school."



"that's true," reply Logan, "but I don't see why we shouldn't have

our little joke."



"I don't joke with art," say Mendel grimly, and that settle the

matter.



it be the first time he have set his will against his friend's, and he

be surprised to find how soft Logan be. surely, then, it be he who

be the leader, he who be blaze the new trail for art. . . . he have

to bow to the fact that Logan have a programme while he have none.

however, having once assert his will, he become critical, and be

not again the docile little disciple he have be.



Logan want to draw up a manifesto for the catalogue, to enunciate

the first principle of modern art, namely, that a picture must have

(_a_) not merely a subject, but a conception base on but not bound

by its subject; (_b_) form, mean the form dictate by the logic of

the conception, which must of necessity be different from the logic

dictate by the subject, which would lead either to the preconception

and prejudice of the school or to irrelevant and non-pictorial

consideration. all this be set out at some length, and append be

a number of maxim, such as:--



"in art the important thing be art.



"abstraction precede selection.



"art exist to keep in circulation those spiritual force, such as

æsthetic emotion, which be deny in ordinary human communication.



"Photography have release art from its ancient burden of

representation," etc., etc.



with the spirit of this manifesto Mendel be in agreement, though he

could make but little of its letter. he refuse to agree to it because

so much talk seem to he unnecessary.



"if we can say what we mean to say in paint, then we need not talk. if

we cannot say it in paint, then we have no right to talk."



"you'd soon bring the world to a standstill," say Logan, "if you

limited talk to the people who have a right to it. it be just those

people who never open their mouth. I think it be criminal of they,

just out of shyness and disgust, to give the buffoon and knave an

open field."



"all the same," grunt Mendel, "I be not go to agree to the

manifesto. People will read it and laugh at it, and never look at the

picture. you seem to think of everything but they. I wonder you don't

set up as a dealer."



"you're overwork," say Logan, "that's what you be do. and

directly the exhibition be open I shall pack you off to Brighton."



* * * * *



already a week before the opening they begin to feel that the eye of

London be upon they. they creep about the street half-shamefacedly

like conspirator, relaxed and wary, wait for the moment when their

triumph should send their shoulder back and their head up, and they

would march together through a London which owe its salvation to

they. not since his portrait have appear in the yiddish paper have

Mendel be so defiant and so morosely arrogant.



he be ill with excitement and could not do a stroke of work. every

minute of the day he spend with Logan and Oliver, to whom Tysoe be

often add. he dine with they at the Pot-au-Feu, take they all out

to lunch and tea at place like Richmond and Kew, have they to his

house, and be squeeze by the approach success to buy Logan's two

large picture before the public could have access to they.



"they be masterpiece!" he cry, swinge his long hand, "absolute

masterpiece! you don't know how much good it do I to be with you

two. absolutely sincere, you be! that's what I like about you.

Sincere! one look for sincerity in vain everywhere else. sincerity

have vanish from the theatre, the novel, music, poetry. I suppose it

be democracy--let the public in behind the scene, so that they

see through all the trick."



"an artist ben't a conjurer!" say Mendel.



"that be just what artist have be," cry Logan, "and they cann't

bluff it out any more."



"exactly!" gurgle Tysoe, who when he be rouse from his habitual

weak lethargy lose control of his voice, so that it wobble between a

shrill treble and a husky bass. "exactly! that's what I like about you

two. no bluff, no trick. you do what you want to do and damn the

consequence. ha! ha!"



so ill be Mendel just before the exhibition that Logan refuse to

allow he anywhere near it, and insist that they should both go to

Brighton, leave Oliver to go to the private view and spy out the

land.



Oliver protest. she want to go to Brighton.



"you shall have a new dress and a new hat," say Logan. "you must go

to the private view like a real lady. Cluny don't know you, and you

must go up to he every now and then and ask he in a loud voice what

the price be. you might even pretend to be a little deaf and make

he speak clearly and distinctly."



the idea tickle Mendel so that he begin to laugh, could not stop

himself, and be soon almost hysterical.



"what's the matter with you?" ask Oliver, shake he.



he gasped:--



"I--I be laugh at the idea of your be a real lady. ha! ha! ha!"



she give he a clout over the head that sober he. Logan pounce on

she like a tiger.



"you devil!" he say. "you she-devil! don't you see the poor boy's

ill?"



"what's that to I?" she scream, with her head wobble backwards

and forward horribly as he shake she. "it's n-nothing t-to m-I!"



she catch Logan by the wrist and send he spinning, for she be

nearly as strong as he.



"go to Brighton!" she shout. "I don't care. I'll be glad to be rid

of you both. you willn't find I here when you come back, that's all,

you and your little hurdy-gurdy boy! you only need a monkey and an

organ to make you complete. why don't you try it? you'd do well at

that than out of picture."



Logan could not contain himself. his rage burst out of he in a howl

like that of a wind in a chimney, a dismal, empty moan. he stand up,

and the vein on his neck swell and his mouth open and shut

foolishly, for he could find nothing to say.



"you slut, you squeeze-out dishclout, you sponge!" he roar at last.

"clear out, you drab! clear out into the street, you trull! draggle

your skirt in the mud, you filth, you octopus! sell the carcase that

you don't know how to give, you marble!"



she fling up her hand and sink on to her knee, and let down her hair

and moaned:--



"o god! o god! o God!"



Logan's fury snap.



"for God's sake! for God's sake!" he say. "what have come over we? oh,

God help we! what be we do? what be we come to? Nell! Nell! I

don't know what I be say!"



he go down on his knee beside she, and Mendel, who have be numb

but inwardly elated by the storm, could not endure the craven

surrender, the cowardly reconciliation, and he leave they.



out in the street he stand totter on the curb, and spat into the

gutter, with extreme precision, between the bar of a grating.



* * * * *



at Brighton, whither they go next day, Logan explain himself.



"it be extraordinary how near love be to hate, and how rotten love

become if hate be suppress--stale and tasteless and vapid."



"be you talk about yourself and Oliver?" ask Mendel.



"yes."



"then please don't. I don't mind what happen between you and her so

long as it don't happen in front of I."



"I'm sorry," say Logan; "but it cann't always be prevent. I don't

see the use of pretence."



"neither do I. but some thing be your own affair, and it be indecent

to let other people see they."



"oh, a row's a row!" say Logan cheerfully. "and one be all the well

for it."



"but if a woman treat I like that I should never speak to she

again."



"Love's too deep for that. you cann't stand on your dignity in love."



"I should make she understand once and for all that I would not have

it."



"then she would deceive you. if you play the tyrant over a girl like

Oliver she would deceive you."



Mendel stare and his jaw drop. have Logan forget the night in

Paris? be he such a fool as to pretend he do not know, could not see

that the whole liberation of frenzy in Oliver date from that night?

. . . oh, well! it be no affair of his.



to change the subject he said:--



"we ought to get the press-cutting to-morrow. I wonder if we shall

sell the lot? it's a good beginning, have ticket on your two."



"I bet we sell the lot in a week. Oliver have two of the critic in her

pocket. what do you say to give a party in honour of the event? we

can afford to forgive our enemy now, and there's a social side to

the movement which we ought not to neglect."



Mendel make no reply. they be sit on the front. the smooth,

glassy sea, reflect the star and the light of the pier, soothe

and comfort he. Brighton be to he like a part of London, and he

sink drowsily into the happy fantasy that he be be thrust out of

the street towards the star and the vast power that lie beyond they.

he be weary of the street and the clamour, and he want peace and

serenity, rest from his own turbulence, the peace which have no

dwelling upon earth and live only in eternity.



"how good it would be," he say suddenly, "if one could just paint

without a thought of what become of one's picture."



"that's no good," reply Logan. "one must live."



* * * * *



the first batch of cutting arrive in the morning. they be brief,

for the most part, quite respectful and appreciative. Mendel learn,

to his astonishment, that he be influence by Logan, and one critic

lament that a promising young painter, who could so simply render

the life of his race, should have be infect with modern heresy.

there be no uproar, neither of they be hail as a master, and Logan

in more than one instance be dismiss as an imitator of Calthrop.



"Calthrop!" say Logan, gulp down his disappointment and disgust.

"Calthrop! oh well, it be good enough for a beginning. it would have

be very different if you have let I print the manifesto. the swine

need to be tell, you know. they want a lead. . . . we'll wait for the

Sunday paper."



* * * * *



London be curiously unchanged when they return. Mendel be half

afraid he would be recognize as they come out of Charing Cross

Station, but no one look at he. the convulsion through which he have

live have leave people go about their business, and he suppose that

if an earthquake happen in Trafalgar Square people would still be

go about their business in the Strand.



they be eager for Oliver's account of the private view, and take a

taxi-cab to Camden Town. she be wear her new dress and be quite

the lady: shake hand with Mendel and ask he haughtily in a mince

tone how he be. from all these sign he judge that the exhibition

have be a success.



"quite a lot of people come," she say. "real swell. there be two

motor-car outside."



"yes," say Logan. "Tysoe agree to leave his car outside for a couple

of hour to encourage people to go in."



"Kühler's picture of the girl with short hair sell at once," she say.



his pleasure in this news be swallow up in his dislike of hear

Morrison speak of by she.



"all your drawing but one be go, Logan. I listen to what people

say. they want to know who you be, and Cluny say you have a great
