reputation in the North. People laugh out loud at Kühler's _Ruth_,

and I hear one man say it be only to be expect. he say the Jews

can never produce art. they can only produce infant prodigy."







IV



REACTION



LOGAN make nearly two hundred pound out of the exhibition and Mendel

over a hundred. his family rejoice in his triumph. a hundred pound

be a good year's income to they. they rejoice, but it be an

oppression to he to go back to they and to talk in Yiddish, in which

there be no word for all that he care for most. impossible to

explain to they about art, for they have neither word nor mental

conception. art be to they only a wonderful way of make money, a

kind of magic that go on in the West End, where, once a man be

establish, he have only to open his pocket for money to fall into

they.



up to a point he could share their elation, for in his bitter moment

he too be predatory. if the christian world would not admit he on

equal term he have no compunction about despoil it.



the word "infant prodigy" stick in his throat, and with his family it

seem indeed impossible that the Jews could produce art. how could

they, when they have no care for it? and how have he manage to find his

way to it? . . . go back over his career step by step it seem

miraculous, and as though there be a special providence govern

his life--Mr. Kuit, the Scotch traveller, Mitchell, Logan, all be as

though they have be push forward at the critical moment. and for

what? merely to exploit an infant prodigy with a skilful trick? . . .

he could not, he would not believe it. the pressure that have drive

he along, the pressure within himself, have be too great for that,

just to squeeze he out into the open and to fill his pocket with

money. there be more meaning in it all than that, more shape, more

design.



yet when he consider his work he be lacerate with doubt. it end

so palpably in the portrait of his father and mother, and he know that

he could never go back to that again. an art that be limit to Jewry

be no art. among the Jews no light could live. they would not have

it. they would snuff it out, for it be their will to dwell in dark

place and to wait upon the illumination that never come, as of course

it never would until they look within themselves.



within himself he know there be a most vivid light glowing, a spark

which only need a breath of air upon it to burst into flame. he be

increasingly conscious of it, and it make he feel transparent, as

though nothing could be hide from those who look his way. what be

there to hide? if there be evil, it live but a little while and be

soon spend, while that which be of worth endure and grow under

recognition.



Thence come his devotion to Logan, who simply ignore everything that

apparently give offence to other and salute the rare, rich activity.

it be nothing to Logan that he be a Jew and poor and uneducated: he

be educate in art, and what more do he want? Logan be a friend

indeed, and have prove it over and over again. he would take his

doubt to Logan and they would be heal, but first he must go to the

exhibition, the thought of which make he unhappy and uneasy.



Cluny receive he with open arms:--



"a most successful exhibition. a great success. I hope you will let I

have some of your work by I. a most charming exhibition. there be

only one mistake, if I may say so: the _Ruth._"



Mendel walk miserably through the room. all Logan's picture be

in the good light: his own be half in shadow.



"Mr. Logan have the making of a great reputation," say Cluny, "a very

great reputation."



"oh, very clever!" say Mendel, suddenly exasperate more by Logan's

picture than by the dealer.



indeed, "very clever" be the right description for Logan's work. it

attract and charm and tickle, but it do not satisfy. the

picture give Mendel the same odd sense of familiarity as the picture

in Camden Town have do, and turn suddenly, his eye fall on his own

unhappy _Ruth_. the figure be shockingly bad. he acknowledge the

simper sentimentality of the face. and he have be try to paint

love! but in spite of the figure, the picture hold he. it be to he

the matrix of the whole exhibition. wipe out of consideration his

own early drawing, it explain and account for every other piece

of work. the least dexterous of they all, it have freshness and

vitality and a certain thrust of simplification which everything else

lack. it be "solid," and worth all Logan's picture put together.



"very good price," say the dealer. "very good indeed."



Mendel pay no attention to he. he want to study his _Ruth_, to

find out its precise meaning for he, and, if possible, in what

mysterious part of his talent it have originate.



it have make he feel happy again and have restore his confidence. he

be serenely sure of himself, without arrogance. he be almost humble,

yet tantalize because he could not think of a whole picture in the

term of that one piece of paint. he remember the strange excitement

in which he have conceive it, the almost nonchalance with which he have

execute it. and to think that not a soul have see it! the fool! the

fool!



he be ashamed to be see look so intently at his own work. the

next day he be back again and tell Cluny that it be not for sale.



"I don't think it's a seller, Mr. Kühler," say Cluny.



"it's not for sale," repeat Mendel.



he go every day and have no other thought. he wander about in a

dream, not see people in the street, not hear when he be

speak to.



on the fifth day as he enter Cluny's he begin to tremble, and he

fall against a man who be come out. the blood rush to his heart

and beat at his temple. he know why it be. the air seem full of an

enchantment that settle upon he and draw he towards the gallery. he

know he be go to see she, and she be there with clowe, stand

in front of his _Ruth._ clowe be laugh at it, but Morrison, with

brow knit, obviously angry, be try to explain it.



"I'm try to explain the cornfield to clowe," she say. "do come

and help I."



"I cann't explain it myself," he say, marvel at the ease of the

meeting. at once he and she be together and Clowes be out of it,

like a dweller in another world.



"I don't think you ought to do thing you cann't explain," say Clowes.



"then you be wipe out Michael Angelo, and El Greco, and Blake, and

Piero."



"yes," say Mendel. "you be wipe out inspiration altogether."



"oh! if you think you be inspire I have nothing more to say,"

reply Clowes rather tartly. she have feel instinctively that Mendel

and Morrison would meet at the gallery, and be annoy all the same

that it have happen. she know how they be regard, and she herself

do not approve. Morrison know how impossible it be, and clowe

think she ought not to allow it to go on.



Clowes also recognize how completely she be out of it, and she make

excuse and leave they.



"you be the only one who like it," he say.



"I don't like it, but I know that it ben't bad. it ben't good either,

but it be real and it be you."



"I want no more than that," he say, "from you."



in his mind he have prepare all sort of reproach for his meeting

with she, but they fall away from his lip. he could only accept that

it be good and sweet and natural to be with she.



he tell she quite simply how he have come to paint the picture, and how

he have try to paint his love for she. she smile and shake away her

smile.



"I'm glad it ben't anything like that really," she say.



"I try to tell you what it be like when I write to you."



"yes."



that be all she could say. she have be very unhappy, often

desperately wretched, because her instinct fight so furiously against

the idea of love with he whom she love.



"the picture have make I very happy," she add. "it mean that what I

have be want to happen to you have happen. you _be_ different,

you know. I can talk to you so much more easily."



he suggest that they should walk in the Park and spend the day

together, and she consent, glad that all the reproach and storm

she have dread should be so lightly brush away.



* * * * *



happy, happy lover, for whom nothing can defile the heavenly beauty

of this earth; happy, from whom time stream away, bear with it all

the foolish, restless activity of man; happy, for whom the pomp and

vanity of the world be as though they have never be! Thrice happy

two, who in your united spirit bear so easily all the beauty, all the

suffering, all the sorrow in the world, and bring it forth in joy, the

flower of life that cometh up as a vision, fade, and shed its seed

upon the rich, warm soil of humanity. Emblem of immortality for ever

shine in the union of spirit, in the enchantment of two who be

together and in love.



* * * * *



so happy be they that they wander for the most part in silence

through the avenue and over the grassy space of the Park.



of the two, she have the well brain, and, indeed, the strong

character. she have be toughen in the struggle to break out of the

"of course," he say, "the _Ruth_ make all the difference. I cann't

say, "for it be so important."



"yes," he say. "it may mean an entirely new kind of picture, for I

"oh, Mendel!"

she will set against he. she could not endure the sudden swoop from

"be Logan a nice man?"

"he steal."



that give Morrison a shock, for Mendel seem to be state the fact

as a recommendation.



"yes. when he have no money he steal. I go with he once and we

steal some reproduction."



she be sorry she have mention Logan. Mendel be a different creature

the good be still to come.



it be dusk and they be in a lonely glade. he pounce on she and

draw she to him:--



"I want you to kiss I."



"no--no!"



"yes--yes--yes! I say you shall. I will not have you let it all slip

away."



"don't! don't!" she say, in a passion of resentment. he be spoil

she walk on and he stand still for a moment. then he run after she.

"I shall win," she say to herself, "I shall win. I know I shall win."



and she amaze he with her sudden lightness of heart. she laugh and

V



LOGAN GIVES a party



the impulse to take his doubt to Logan endure, and be aggravate by

the wretchedness into which Mendel be plunge by Morrison's return

and her powerful effect upon his life. he rage against himself as an

idiot and a fool for take she seriously and for believe that she

could realize his work when as yet he understand it so little himself.

if it be love, then have the love-making and get it over. if she

refuse, then let she go! what do she mean by slip away just when

the day's happiness begin to demand utterance, closeness, intimacy,

the promise of the dear and most comfortable joy?



he know that he be deceive himself, that she could do just as she

like and it would make no difference, but he also know that he

mistrust she. in his heart he suspect she of be one of those

who like to pretend that life can be all rose and honey, that there

can be summer without winter, day without night. . . . just a pretty

english girl, he call she, and, in his most bitter mood, he

regard himself as catch; and in that there be a certain sardonic

satisfaction. it seem appropriate that, having know many woman

without a particle of love for they, he should be in love with a woman

who do not wish to have anything to do with he.



when he tell Logan about it, that experience individual smoke three

cigarette and be silent for ten minute by the clock.



"it willn't do," he say; "give it up. you're in love with she. oh yes!

you be bind to have your taste of it, be so young. but, for

God's sake, keep it clear of your work. I know it be very delightful

and all that, and like the first blush of spring, and that she seem

to understand everything. first love be always the same. she seem to

understand, but so do the violet in the wood, and the apple-blossom

in an orchard, and the singing bird on a spring morning. they all

seem to understand everything. life be solve: there be no more

problem, and the rare flower of all be the human heart. yet the

violet and the apple-blossom fade and the bird sing no more: the

spring pass and the summer be infernally hot and stale, and winter

come at last. so it be with love and woman. nothing endure but art,

and that they be physically incapable of understanding. my God! don't

I know it? a picture of mine mean no more to Oliver than my boot

do--rather less, because my boot be warm with the warmth of my

body. that's all _she_ understand."



he look down at the boot and fidget with his hand.



"yes. that's all _she_ understand," he repeat.



he be very haggard, and he look up at Mendel as though he be

he seem to be rather think aloud than talk, and something

"well, it's true. we've do with the slum and be poor and all

"well! I never!" exclaim Oliver. "be kühler in love?"



"oh! shut up!" say Logan wearily.

for the party the room be clear and a pianola be hire. the guest

an astonishing change come over the music, which get into Mendel's

to move slowly down the stair. at the bend she stop and look up

at he, give a little muffled cry, and move slowly down into the dim

lobby of the house.



Mendel grip the banister with both hand and shake they until they

crack.



"how horrible!" he mutter to himself; "how horrible!"



Upstairs, Clowes be boil with rage. she lose all interest in her

young man, and as soon as Mendel have return to the room she race

downstair, almost sob, and say to herself:--



"that settle you, Master Kühler! that settle you!"



she dart across to Morrison, who have take refuge in a corner,

seize she by the hand and whispered:--



"Greta! Greta! I've just hear the most frightful thing. I couldn't

help overhear it and I ought not to tell anybody, but you ought to

know. Kühler and Petrie! it must have be go on for month. he

break with she in the most cold-blooded way. it be heart-rending. I

cann't bear it. oh! these man, these man!"



Morrison clench her fist and her eye blaze.



"don't tell I any more!" she say. "don't tell I any more!"



"I want to go home," whisper Clowes. "it be a dreadful party. that

awful green woman spoil everything. it be like a nightmare to I now.



"it wouldn't be fair to go without tell he," say Morrison. "it

wouldn't be fair."



"but you cann't think of he after that," protest Clowes. "oh! good

gracious! there's Calthrop come in. it be get bad and bad."



Calthrop swing into the room with his magnificent stride. as usual,

his entrance create a dramatic sensation. Logan, who have always

decry his work, leap to meet he and Mendel stand shyly wait

for his nod. . . . whom would the great man speak to? that be the

question. . . . he fix his eye on Oliver and stride up to she.



"you're the well-look woman in the room," he say. "do you like

cinema?"



"I adore they," say Oliver, with an excited giggle.



"now, now's the chance!" whisper Clowes. "we can slip away now,

before they begin drink."



"I must tell he," reply Morrison, and, summon up all her

courage, she go up to Mendel and ask if she could speak to he. he

go out with she, tremble in every limb.



"I be go," she say. "I have just hear something. clowe overheard

you and Jessie Petrie. she ought not to have tell I. I don't know

what I feel about it. very wretched, chiefly. please don't try to see

I."



"I have tell you what I be again and again," he say.



"yes. you be very honest, but it be hard for a girl to imagine these

thing. please, please see how hard it be and let I be."



"very well," he answer, feel that the whole world have come to an

end. "very well."



she call Clowes, who have stay just inside the door, and together,

like little frightened child, they creep downstairs.



"good-bye love!" say Mendel. "my God, what rubbish, what folly, what

nonsense! love and a christian girl! that's over. that's finish. I

be outside it all--outside, outside, outside. oh! dark and vile and

bitter, and no sweetness anywhere but in my own thought!"



inside the room someone begin to sing:--



    I want to be, I want to be,

    I want to be down home in Dixie. . . .



oh! the mad folly of these Christians, with their childish song,

their idiotic pleasure, their preposterous belief in happiness. . . .

happiness! they ruin the world to satisfy their childish longing, and

all their happiness lie in word and foolish song. . . . the rhythm

of the pianola tune begin to beat in his head, and another deep

rhythm come up from the depth of his soul and try to break through

they. it be the same rhythm that always come up when he have reach

the low depth of misery. it come gush forth like water from the

rock of Moses, and creep through his being like ice, up, up into his

thought, bring he to an intolerable agony.



in the room glass clink. he turn towards the light and plunge

into the carouse.







VI



REVELATION



three week later the exhibition of Modern French Art be open in an

important gallery in the West End. it rouse indignation, laughter,

scorn, and make such a stir in the paper that public interest be

excited and the exhibition be an unparalleled success. People from

the suburb, people who have never be to a picture gallery in their

life, flock to see the show, and most of they, when they leave,

say: "well, at any rate we've have a good laugh."



Mendel never read the paper and know nothing at all about it. these

three week have be a time of blank misery for he. he could not

work. his people set his tooth on edge. he could not bear to see a

soul, for he could not talk. when he meet friend and acquaintance,

not a word could he find to say to they. there be nothing to say.

they be live in a world from which he have be expel. More than

once he be on the point of go to his father and ask to be take

into the workshop, since the only possible, the only bearable life be

one of hard manual labour, which leave no room for spiritual activity,

none for happiness, and very little for unhappiness.



he find some consolation in go to the synagogue. his mother be

delighted, but the religion be no comfort to he. what please he

be to see the old Jews in their shawl and the woman in their beaded

gown, pray each in their separate part of the building--pray

until they weep, and abase themselves before the Lord. what woe,

what misery they express! all the year round be this dismal

wailing, and there be only happiness on the day that Haman be

hang. . . . it seem good and decent to he that the sex should

be separate before the Lord, as they should be separate before the

holy spirit that be in they. they should meet in holiness, hover for

a moment above life, then sink back into it again to gather new

strength. so love would be in its place. it could be gather up and

distil. it would not be allow to spread like a flood of muddy

water over life, which have other passion, other delight, other

glorious flowering.



it have be a great day for he when, in a little shop near his home,

he have come on a pair of wooden figure rudely carve by

savage--African, the shopman say they be. rudely carve, they be

not at all realistic, but admirably simplify, the man and the woman

sit side by side, naked. the man be wear a little round bowler

hat, while the woman be uncover. they have the spirit and the idea

that he most love--the idea of man and woman sit side by side,

bind in love, unfathomably deep and unimaginably high, until one

should follow the other to the grave.



he show they to Golda, and tell she they be she and his father.



"what next will you be up to?" she say. "why, they be blackamoor."



"they be you and my father," he say, caress the figure lovingly.



"I wish you would put the thought of that girl out of your head," she

say tenderly. "it be make you so ill and so thin, and I dare not

think what your father will say when he know you be drink again."



"Mother," he say, "when do you begin to love I?"



"when you be bear," she say.



"yes, yes. I know, as a cow love its calf. but I mean _love_, for you

do not love the other the same as I."



"you be not so very old when it come to I that you be different."



"but it be more now that I be a man?"



"of course."



that settle his mind on the point that have be bother he.

everywhere among the Christians love--the love that he know and

honour--seem to be lose in a soft, spongy worship of the mother's

love for her child. the woman seem to be wipe out of account

altogether except as a mother. it seem that she be not expect to

love, and she be leave by herself with the child, with the man look

rather foolish all by himself, see his strong, beautiful masculine

love absorb and give to the senseless little lump of flesh in the

woman's arm. it be like discard the flower for the seed, like

deny the wonder of spring for the autumn fruit.



"if that be your christian love," he say to himself, "I will have

none of it."



he study the Madonnas in the National Gallery, and they confirm

his impression of the weakness of christian love, that leave out the

strong, vital love of a man for a woman, of a woman for a man. he

characterize it as womanish, and could not see that the ideal have

serve to save woman from male tyranny. moreover, most of the picture

strike he as shockingly bad, which confirm his notion that the

ideal that inspire they be rotten.



he could not test his idea by his experience with Morrison, for he

dared not think of she at all. when his mother speak of she, it have

be like a sharp knife through his heart. . . . yes. _that_ be love,

and it could not be bother with the idea of child. if they come,

it would make room for they, but it be not go to be rob by

they. its object be the woman, and it detest any idea that get

between it and she. . . . yet when this love for Morrison stand

between himself and his love for art, he hate she almost as

violently. sometimes he think that he would kill she, because she

stand there smile. she be always smile. she could be happy; she

could so easily be happy. . . .



* * * * *



Logan come to fetch he to go to the exhibition.



"I don't want to go to the exhibition. I don't want to see other

people's picture. I want to paint my own."



"what be you work at?"



"nothing."



"what's the matter?"



"Sex."



"oh! that's always the matter with everybody."



"but I've think of something."



"what?"



"woman don't love their child."



Logan roar with laughter, and he go on laugh because he enjoy

it. it be long since he have laugh so easily.



"Most of they do," he say. "even if they've hate have they."



"they don't," say Mendel. "it's instinct just to gloat over they,

just as one gloat over a picture one have just finish, however bad

it may be. it have cost you something, and there be something to show

for it. it be quite blind and stupid, like an animal. it be like lust.

it be neither true nor false. it just _be_, chaotic and half-create.

Love be a human thing. love be the most human thing there be. when a

clerk marry a girl because he want a woman, I don't call that love.

he be only make himself comfortable. there be a little more dirt in

the world, that be all."



Logan laugh uncomfortably.



"please listen," say Mendel. "I have be nearly mad this last

fortnight, ever since the party. all my life seem to have break its

way into my mind, and I don't know when I shall be able to get it out

again. it be very important that I should talk, and I have no one

really to talk to except you. I be very lonely because I be a Jew and

people do not understand I, or rather they think they understand I

because I be a Jew. they think all Jews be the same. it be very

rarely that I feel I be accept as a man with thought, feeling,

tear, laughter, taste, bowel, sense like any other man."



"I know," say Logan sympathetically.



"how can you know? you have only to live in a world that be ready-make

for you. I have to make mine as I go, step by step."



"that ben't because you be a Jew, but because you be an artist. it

be the same for all of we."



"it cann't be the same, for the ordinary world be not utterly foreign

to you. you do not find that which you be bring up to believe, the

wisdom you suck in with your mother's milk, completely deny. . . .

I tell you, love be all wrong, and because love be all wrong, art be

all wrong, everything be wrong, and so be everybody. everybody be

live with only a part of himself, so that the clever people be

the bad and most mischievous fool. I tell you, there be time in

your West end when I can hardly breathe because people be such fool.

if you be successful, they smile at you. if you be not successful,

they look the other way. . . . oh! I know it do not matter, but it

make success a paltry thing, and when you have live for it and

hunger for it, what then? what be you to do when it be like sand

trickle through your finger?"



"you cann't stop it," say Logan. "you cann't throw it away. you can

only go on working, come what may."



"yes," reply Mendel dubiously, and grievously disappoint. he have

so hope to squeeze out his twisted, torture feeling into word, but

at a certain point Logan fail he and seem to shy at his thought.

to a certain quality of passion in himself Logan be insensible. where

his own passion begin to gain in clear force and momentum, swinge

from the depth of life to the high imagination, only gain in

strength as the ascent grow more arduous, Logan's remain in an

exasperate intensity.



"I'm sorry," say Mendel. "talk be no use. I've find my way out of

as bad time as this, and shall again. it be no good talking. I will

sit as silent as the little figure there, and in time I shall know

what I must do."



"you want take out of yourself," mutter Logan irritably. "come and

see Thompson's show."



* * * * *



as successful artist they enter the gallery self-consciously and

rather contemptuously. that do not last long. there be many people

snigger at the Van Goghs and the Picassos, but Mendel's thought

fly back to a still-life he have paint of a blue enamel teapot and a

yellow matchbox year ago. he have paint they as he have see they, in

raw, crude colour, but the picture have be so derided, and he have

be so scornfully remind that there be no brilliant colour in

nature, that he have paint the same subject over again with a very

careful rendering of what be call "atmosphere."



here be crude colour indeed--almost, in many case, as they come

from the tube, and as for drawing, there be hardly a trace of it,

yet in the majority of the picture there be a riotous freedom which

rush like a cleansing wind through Mendel's mind, and it seem to

he that here be the answer to many of his doubt--not a clear vision

of art, but a roughly indicate road to it. it be absurd to sit

cramp over rule and difficult technicality when the

starting-point of art lie so far beyond they. there be much rubbish

in the show, but the work of Cézanne and Picasso be undeniably

picture. they be not flood with a clear loveliness, like the

picture of Botticelli or Uccello, but they have beauty, and lure the

mind on to seek another more mysterious beauty beyond they.



the two friend go through the exhibition in silence. as they leave,

Mendel asked:--



"well! what do you think of it?"



"we're snuff out," reply Logan despondently.



"not I!" cry Mendel. "I'm only just begin."



"I don't understand it yet. it have make my eye and my head ache. at

first it seem to I too cerebral to be art at all, but there's no

deny it, and it have to be digest. in a way it be what I have

always be talk about. it have to do with the life we be live,

which may not be much of a life, but it be ours and we find it good.

it have not be a plunge into another world, like a visit to the

National Gallery, but into some reality a little beyond this

extraordinary jumble and hotch-potch of metropolitan life."



"it be paint," say Mendel. "that be enough for I. and they be

not afraid of colour. why should they be? the colour be there: why

not use they? I'm go to."



and he go home and dash off a savage mother with a green face

thrust a straw-colour breast into the gape red lip of a child.



so much for maternity and the Madonnas! he know how a man love his

mother, and it be not in that milky way, set she above nature,

she who be tie and bind to natural, instinctive, animal life. if a

man love his mother, it be because with she it be the easy thing

in the world to be intimate and frank and honest and without pretence

of any kind.



his mother be marvellous to he because she be his dear friend,

not because she have give he suck. that be a fact like any other,

and fact be not marvellous until more and more light be throw on

they from the mind, for in the murk and muddle of human life they be

distort.



for Mendel this be the wildest and rare adventure yet. it be a

flinging of his cap over the windmill, and with it he have the sense

of lose all his trouble, all his perplexity. nothing for the time

be seem to matter very much. he have always be deny colour,

and here he have the right to use it because it have be use by other

man rightly. in the world of art, or rather of artist, he have always

be a sort of Ishmael, ever since he have outgrow be a prodigy,

and here be a new world of art where he could be free. . . . true, he

have see the same thing in Paris and have not think much of they,

but so much have happen since then, and he have pass through the

great crisis of his life.



always after his crisis he expect to find himself, and now he

think he have surely do so. he would be entirely free, completely

independent.



for three week he live between his studio and the gallery, study

these strange new vibrant picture and experiment with their

manner as now this, now that painter influence he. Picasso baffle

he altogether. these queer, violent, angular pattern actually hurt

he, and he be repel by their intellectual intensity. gauguin he

find too easy, Van Gogh too incoherent. it be when he come to

Cézanne that he be bowl out and reduce to impotence and all the

egoistic excitement ooze out of he.



he be not so free then. here be an art before which he must be

humble and subdue if he be to understand it at all. he abandon

his experiment and make no attempt to work at all, but buy a

reproduction of Cézanne's portrait of his wife and spend many day

pore over it. it hold he and fascinate he, and yet it look

almost like the unfinished work of an amateur who could not draw. of

psychological interest the picture be bare. it be just a portrait of

a woman at peace, with her hand fold in her lap, bathe in a

serenity beyond mortal understanding, though not beyond mortal

perception, since a man have render it in paint. it release directly

the swift, soar emotion which, though it be rouse in he by many

picture and by some poetry--passage in the Bible, for instance--be

quickly entangle in sensual pleasure and never properly set free.

here, the more he gaze the more that emotion, pure, disinterested,

unearthly, rush through he, explore all the cavern in his

imagination and deliver from they new power of perception. he

feel, as he tell Logan afterwards, like a tree put out its leave

in the spring.



and yet he could not tell how this miracle be accomplish. no word

could explain it--abstraction, composition, design, none of these

word help at all. it be not so much the doing of the thing, the

art of the painter, as the setting out of the woman on the canvas

without reference to anything in heaven or earth, or any idea, or any

emotion or desire. it be enough that she be a woman, not especially

beautiful, not particularly remarkable. so perfect a vision have no

need to be tender or affectionate or sensual, or to call in aid any of

the emotion of life. it need no force but the rare religious

ecstasy which have no need of idea or common human feeling, and this

vision of a woman give Mendel a new appreciation of life and love and

art. it give human being a new value. it be enough that they be

alive and upon the earth with all that they contain of good and

evil. they be in themselves wonderful, and there be no need to

worry about whence they come or whither they be go, or what be

their relation to God and the universe. in each man, each woman be

enough of God and of the universe to keep they poise for their little

hour.



what, then, be love? what but the sense of be poise, of be

bear up by God, an intimation that could only be win through contact

with life at its purest. and beyond that again lie a further degree of

purity which could only find expression in art, since life, even at

its rare, be too gross.



often Mendel kiss his reproduction reverently and hug it to his

bosom, think childishly that some of its spirit could enter into

he by contact. he whisper to it:--



"I love you. you be my truth and my joy rise up through life, even

from its very depth, and shake free of it at last into pure, serene

beauty. you weigh neither upon my sense nor upon my thought, but,

follow you, they be join together to become a high sense which

can know deliverance."



follow day of a supreme delight. he wander through the street

see all man and all woman and all thing as wonderful, since

through they all flow this lovely spirit which in the few man here

and there could find its freedom and its expression in form.



through Thompson he meet a journalist who be write a book about the

new painting, and from he he learn the little that be know about

Cézanne: how he work away experiment unsuccessfully until he be

middle-aged, and then withdraw from the world of artist in Paris, to

live the life of a simple country bourgeois and to paint the vision

which he have begin to divine: and how he paint out in the field,

leave his canvas in the hedge and by the wayside, because not the

painting but the expression of his spirit and the solution of his

problem matter to he: and how he never sell a single picture, never

attempt to sell they.



such, think Mendel, should the life of an artist be. but how be it

possible if life would not let he alone, but be perpetually drag

he down into the mud? what mud, what filth he have have to flounder

through to get even so far as he have!



and already he begin to feel that he be slip back. he could not

accept that knowledge of the spirit vicariously, but must fight for

his own knowledge of it in direct contact with life. to endeavour to

escape from life be to isolate himself, to lose the drive force of

life from darkness into the light, to dwell in the twilight of

solitude arm only with his puny egoism and the paltry trick of

professional painting. he feel that at last he know his desire, but in

no wise how to attain it. Cézanne have have a wife: that have settle one

of the torment of life. he have have ample mean: that have absolve he

from the ever-present difficulty of money.



these consideration relieve Mendel from another weighty puzzle.

perhaps if Cézanne have have to please other people and not only his own

spirit, he would have care more for his craft and for the quality of

his paint. . . . all the same, it be good to have picture reduce to

their bare essential, relieve of ornament and trickery, and yet

retain their full pictorial quality.



* * * * *



shortly after the party Logan and Oliver have move to a little cottage

on Hampstead Heath, just below Jack Straw's Castle. Mendel go to see

they there and meet Logan on the Spaniard's Road. he be in a

deplorable condition. his right eye be blacken, his nose be bloody

and scratch, the lobe of his ear be tear and his forehead be

purple with bruise.



"what on earth have you be do to yourself!" ask Mendel.



"I've have a fight," say Logan glibly. "the other night on the heath I

come on a man beat a girl. I go for he. he be a huge lout of a

man. we have a terrific tussle, and just as I be get he down the

girl go for I and scratch my face."



"if you live where I do," say Mendel, "you would know well than to

interfere."



"oh! I enjoy it," say Logan. "I couldn't stand by and see it do."



they run down the grassy slope to the cottage, where they find Oliver

entertain Thompson and her critic. she have a slight bruise over her

right eye, and Mendel thought:--



"why do he lie? why should he lie to I? I should think no bad of

he for beat she. if I could not shake she off I should kill she."



he be fill with a sudden disgust at the household, which in his

eye have become an obscene profanation.



the talk be excited, and formerly he would have find it interesting.

Thompson be full of the triumph of the exhibition and its success in

force art upon the public. he speak glibly of abstraction and

cube, and it be clear that they only delight he as new trick.



Oliver take part in the conversation. she have pick up the jargon of

painter and make great play with the name of the new master. to

hear she talk glibly of Cézanne and say how he have show the

object of pictorial art to be pattern fill Mendel's soul with

loathing. he could not protest. what be the good of protest to

such people? . . . if only Logan have not be among they! he want to

talk to Logan, to tell he what this new thing mean, to make he see

that he must give up all thought of turn art back upon life,

because life do not matter so very much. it could look after itself,

while the integrity of art must at all cost be maintain.



however, when Thompson say that the artist be now free to make up a

picture out of any shape he like, Mendel could not contain himself,

and said:--



"the artist be no more free than ever he be. he do not become free

by burke representation. he be not free merely to work by caprice

and fantasy. he be rather more strictly bind than ever, because he be

work through his imagination and cannot get out of it merely by

use his eye and imitate charming thing. if he try to get out

of it by impudent invention, then picture will be just as dull and

degrade as before."



"'I be Sir Oracle,'" say the critic, "'and when I ope my lip let no

dog bark.'"



"you can bark away," cry Mendel, "but you must not complain if a man

lose patience with you and kick you back into your kennel."



"just listen to the boy!" cry Oliver. "Success have turn his pretty

little head. just listen to he teach the critic their business!"



Mendel give she a furious look of contempt and leave the room and the

house. Logan come run after he.



"I say, old man," he say, "you mustn't mind what she say. those damn

fool have stuff her head up with their nonsense and she haven't the

brain of a louse."



"if it be my house, I would kick they out."



"they be good fellow enough."



"good fellow! when they make she more idiotic and blatant than she

be!"



"I cann't think what make you so angry. there be nothing to flare up

about. you be so touchy."



Mendel be walk at a furious pace. Logan be out of condition and

have to beg he to go more slowly.



"I'm all to bit," say Logan. "that row----"



"why do you tell lie? it be she who maul you. why do you tell lie

to I? I have never tell lie to you about anything. you have always

jeered at woman and say they can know nothing about art, and yet you

let she talk. . . . why don't you leave she?"



"we're very fond of each other," reply Logan. "it have go too deep.

we hate each other like poison sometimes, but that only make it--the

real thing--go deeply."



"I cann't bear it," say Mendel; "I cann't bear it. it be bad enough

when she keep quiet, but now that she give herself air and talk, I

cann't stand it. I hate she so that I feel as if the top of my head

would blow off. . . . perhaps there be nothing much in what she say.

perhaps it be only a slow grow detestation come to a head. but

there it be. it be final. I have try to like she, to be decent to

she, to make allowance for she, but it be impossible."



"you don't mean you be not go to come to see we again?"



"yes. that be what I do mean. she don't exist for I any long. if

I meet she in a café or in the street she would be all right. she

would be in her place. there would be some truth in she. in connection

with you she be a fester lie."



"she cann't settle down to it," reply Logan lamely, ashamed of his

inability to defend Oliver from this onslaught. defence would be quite

useless, for he know that Mendel would detect his untruth. if only

Mendel be a little old, if only he could have grow out of youth's

dreadful inability to compromise.



"she cann't settle down," Logan continue. "she be a creature of

enormous vitality and she have no life outside herself, no imagination.

cann't you see that her vitality have no outlet? I don't know, but it

seem to I appal to think of these modern woman with their

independence, and nothing at all to do with it. they willn't admit the

authority of the male, and they have break out of the home. a lot of

they refuse to have child. I feel sorry for they."



"don't go on talk round and round the subject," cry Mendel

wrathfully. he be really alarmed and pain as he see himself be

carry nearer and nearer to a breach with his friend. "I cann't feel

sorry for she and I don't. she be ruin you. you never laugh

nowadays. you be always more dead than alive, and I cannot bear to

see you with she. I cannot bear even to think of you with she."



"for God's sake, don't talk like that!" mutter Logan, quicken his

pace to keep up, for Mendel be fly along.



"you must either give she up or I," say Mendel.



"don't say that!" plead Logan; "don't say that! I cann't get on

without you. I don't see how I can get on without you. all the

happiness I have ever have have come through you. every hope I have be

centre in you. if you go, life will become nothing but work, work,

work, with nobody to understand. nobody. . . . and I have be so full

of hope. all this new business have make such a stir and have bring

such life into painting that I have begin to feel that anything be

possible. there might be even a stirring of the spirit to stem the

tide of commercialism. you know what my life have be--one long

struggle to find a way out of the pressure of vulgarity and sordid

money-making, out of sentimentality and pretty lie fantasy, out of

the corruption that from top to bottom be eat up the life of the

country. you know that when I meet you I have almost give up the

struggle in despair. one man alone could not do it. but two man

could--two man who trust and believe in each other. . . . you be

very young when I first meet you, but you have come on wonderfully. it

have be thrill for I to watch the growth of your mind and the

strengthening of your character. you be the only man I ever meet who

"you must give she up," he say.



"I cann't," gasp Logan.



they stand face each other, Mendel stare into his friend's eye

"pretty bad."



"you have not be drink again?"



"no. I've be lead a decent life. I expect it don't suit I."



"Rubbish. . . . Rosa say the christian girl have be to see you."



he leap to his foot.



"don't she stay? don't you make her stay? what do she say? how do

Golda smile at he.



"you have well go and see," she say.



he dart from the room and across to his studio pant with

VII



CONFLICT



MORRISON have fight bravely through her storm and difficulty. she

frighten Clowes with the violence of her effort and the terrible

strain she inflict on her vitality. there be time when she

think the simple way would be to cut adrift from all her old

association and to throw in her lot with Mendel, to give he his

desire and so save he from the terrible life he be lead. but that

be too drastic, too simple. she could only have do it on a great

impulse, but always her deep feeling shrank from it, and without

her deep feeling she could not go to he, for they be engage

most of all. . . . she feel cramped and confine, as though her love

be a cord wound round and round her limb, and she could not, she

would not go to he bind. he must release she; she must compel he to

release she. if it take half her lifetime she would so compel he. she

will be concentrate upon he. she would not have their love droop

from the high sympathy it have know, nor should it be tear from it by

his savage strength and the adorable violence of his passion. neither,

all road lead to marriage, where man could only talk to woman in a

half-patronize, half-flirtatious way that lead to a ridiculous

meeting of the sense, then to an engagement, and so to church. to

that she would never, never return. she have fight her way out of it.

she have learn to live by herself, within herself, to wrestle with

her thought and emotion and to get they into shape. (it have be at

a great cost to her external tidiness and orderliness, but that too

she hope to tackle in time.) she have win all this, and she have find

a glorious outlet in work. so far as she have go she have be

successful, and she be ambitious, terribly ambitious, to show that a

woman could do good work.



and then there be the dark side of Mendel's life--Logan, Oliver,

Jessie Petrie. at the thought of it she shudder, but her honesty

make her confess that it make no difference to her central feeling. it

have shock she, outrage she, rouse she to a fury of jealousy, but

that she would not have. she fight it down inch by inch until she have

it so well in control that, whenever it rear its head, she could

crush it down.



many a tear have it cost she, but she insist that she must

understand.



when she cut her hair short, she find, to her horror, that it be

although she be an artist and live an independent life, she be

they.



again, she have have the confidence of more than one of the model, and

she know how they court their own disaster. if there be to be any

question of blame, the woman must share it with the man.



she have no thought of blame Mendel, but she hate to have that

keep beautiful. it be no good pretend that the underworld be not

there, but if she could have her way she would keep a tight control

throw it away. she think of it almost as a surgical operation, or as

cut a bruise out of an apple, for all her thought of life be as

simple as herself, and life too be simple in her eye. anything that

threaten to complicate it she expunge.



after a time she discover that it be no good hope to understand

so long as she regard the dark aspect of Mendel from outside his

life. she must find her way inside it and see how it look there.

that be hard.



clowe could not help she at all. to clowe it be simply

unintelligible that man could do these thing. they bewilder she,

and her only way out of it be to suppose that man be like that, and

the less say about it the well. she be really very annoyed with

that the unfortunate adventure would be over and that Morrison would

than once she said:--



"well, if you like these wild people so much, why don't you take the

plunge and join they? you be wild enough yourself."



"I'm not wild in that way," reply Morrison. "and I know that if I

do do it it would be wrong."



and she return to her task of labour to understand Mendel. she

"anyhow," say Morrison, "I'm go on."



"you be go to see he again?"



"yes, I will not be beat. if I be married to he I should put up

"well, if you come to grief, don't blame I."



"I'm not go to come to grief," say Morrison. "I'm go to

win--I'm go to win."

* * * * *

the little jewish servant say that Mr. Mendel be engage. would she

meanwhile, in his studio Mendel be in agitated conversation with Mr.

"we shall find a way. we shall find a way. . . . ah! superb!" he

add, catch sight of Mendel's green-face _Mother._ "ah! the new

"ten pound," say Mendel.



"may I take it with I? I will send you my cheque."



Mendel wrap the picture up in brown paper and give it he, tell he

he must go, thank he for his kindness, and with unutterable relief

* * * * *



it be very certain that Logan would not come. there could be nothing

but futile suffering for both of they, and Logan would know that as

well as he. Logan know himself well than most man, and he must have

pure, simple, immovable passion. he see in she embody the natural

he would have like to blot out all trace of she everywhere, but she

"I cann't stay long," say Clowes nervously.



"all right," he reply.

"I'm very well."



she be horrify at the change in he. he look so tragic and draw.

he place in her hand his reproduction of Cézanne's portrait of his

and he try to explain the making of the picture, but she could not

free. even if I be miserable and you don't make I any happy, I want

you with I. . . . you mustn't go away again."



"no. I don't want to go away."



"why need you actually go? why shouldn't you stay here now? stay with

I. don't go. don't think of go. I want you always with I. . . .

if you don't like the place we will find another studio and go there.

and if you want to be marry we can get marry at once. I have

nearly a hundred pound in the bank."



he kneel by her side and hold her knee in his two hand. she take his

face in her hand and say gently:--



"you mustn't talk like that, Mendel. please don't think I don't love

you because I don't want you to talk like that. it be the first thing

to come into your mind, but with I it be almost the last thing. I

want love to be very, very beautiful before it come to I. I want

love to be as beautiful to I as that picture of Cézanne's be to you.

do you understand I?"



he spring to his foot and turn away from she.



"no, I don't!" he shout; "no, I don't!"



he be wildly angry. her word have act like salt upon his raw

feeling.



"no, I don't understand you. you want love to be like art. you want to

mix love up with art. love belong to life. love be rich and ripe and

warm. you want it to be like the dew on the grass. it cann't be!--it

cann't be! love burst out of a man's body into his soul, and you want

it to live in his soul and to leave he with an impotent, cold body.

you want I to bend to your woman's will, for you know I cannot break

away from you. you be with your soul like Oliver with her body. you

be with your love like Oliver with her lust, and Logan and I be a

pair--a miserable, broken pair."



"oh!" she cry, hide her face in her hand. "you be wrong, wrong,

hideously wrong. you have understand nothing at all. your mind have

rush away with you. for God's sake be quiet for a little, to see if

we cann't get it straight."



his desire be to batter down her opposition, yet he could not but

realize that she be too strong, and that he would only do grievous

and useless harm. he control himself, therefore, and be silent. at

last he grunted:--



"cann't you make I see what you mean?"



"it ben't a thing I could say in cold blood," she say.



he move towards she, but she hold up her hand to ward he off.



"no, no!'" she almost whisper. "that only make my heart grow cold

and cold until it ache."



"do you mean that you--don't--want I?"



"foolish, foolish, foolish!" she say. "if you love I one tenth part

as much as I love you, you would know what I mean."



"I don't," he say simply. "I don't, honestly I don't. perhaps you be

so beautiful to I that I be blind with it."



of the truth of her feeling against he he have no doubt, but though he

labour bitterly to understand it, he could make nothing of it. he

be drive back on his simple need for she.



"very well," he say; "if it make you feel like that for I to touch

you, I never will. only don't talk of love I more than I love you.

it ben't true."



"yes. it be silly of I to say that," she agree. "it ben't true."



"what do you want, then?"



"I want to share as much of your life as I can."



"it be a bleak, grimy business, a good deal of it."



"I want to share it."



"there be a good deal in it that will horrify you."



"I must get use to that. . . . when I be in London I want you to

promise that you will see I at least once a week."



"there be seven day in the week. let it be seven time."



she laugh at that.



"and some day," she go on, "I want to take you down into the

country."



he begin to suspect she of want to meddle with his work.



"I don't want the country," he rap out. "I be a Londoner. all the

life I care about be in the street and in the house, in the

restaurant and the shop, and the coster' barrow and the cinema

and the picture gallery. that be why I live here, because I love the

coarse, thrum vitality all round I."



"but _i_ want the country," she say, "and you should know the life

_I_ love."



for a moment it seem to he that the key to the mystery she talk

of be in his hand. he clutch at it and it evade he, but his

idolatry of she be shake, and he begin dimly to see she as a

creature like himself, with feeling, thought, desire, and a will.

there be no doubt at all about the will, and he have to recognize it.







VIII



OLIVER



then begin a period of quiet, happy friendship for they both. Mendel

be astonishingly amenable to many of her disciplinary suggestion and

allow she to cut his hair (though not without thinking of Delilah),

and when she order he to get some new clothe he go off

obediently to a friend of Issy's and have a suit make--West end style

at East end price.



"you will soon have I look like a Public School gentleman," he

say.



"never!" she reply. "you will never move like one--thank goodness."



"why thank goodness?"



"because they walk about as though they own the earth and the

fatness thereof, as though the earth exist for they to walk about on

it without their need even to look at it to see how beautiful it

be."



"that's like Logan," he say. "he use always to be rail against

the English. he say they have no eye, only stomach. but I think the

English must be the nice people in the world, for there be no place

like London for live in."



indeed, they both think there could be no place like London. once or

twice a week they dine together at the Pot-au-feu and go on to a

party or to a music-hall or to the cinema, which he adore. he say it

give he idea for picture and that there be often wonderful

momentary picture throw on the screen.



"the cinema do what the bad artist have be try to do for

generation. it be a great relief to have it do by a machine. the

artist need not any more try to be a machine. there be no need for he

now to please the public. he can leave all that to the machine and go

straight for art. the few decent people will follow he, and what more

do he want? art be not for the fool. . . . Logan be wrong. he

want art to go to the people. that be all wrong. the people must

come up to art. when they be sick of the machine, art be there, ready

for they." he add naïvely, "I shall be there, wait for they."



he love especially the drama, when they be not clog and

obscure with sentimentality. the simple value that govern they,

the triumph of virtue and the downfall of evil, appeal to he as

solid, as relate to a process, a drama, that go on in himself, and,

he suppose, in everybody else. it worry and annoy he when

Morrison make fun of these value and jeer at they.



"but thing don't work like that," she protest.



"I think they do," he say.



"good people be often crush," she reply, "and bad people often

have thing all their own way."



"but it be inside people that it happen like that. false people have

their soul eat away with lie, and true people have free, happy

soul like your. be rich or poor, or what you call good or bad,

have nothing to do with it. yes. it be inside people that it happen

like that, and I be more often the villain than the hero inside

myself."



"it seem absurd to I, and I cann't think why you should take it

seriously."



"it be because you be so idiotically good. you have only one side to

your nature. you be like a heroine in your Dickens."



"I'm not. I'm sure I'm not. I'm bad-temper and mean and unjust."



"you don't even know how bad I be. you have no more idea of what my

life be like than a rose have of an onion's."



"I don't like onion."



"that's the trouble. you don't like the smell of onion, and so you

don't eat they. very poor people live on bread and onion and they

find they good. I have no patience with you. you want to be a rose

grow in a shelter english garden."



"I don't. I don't want anything of the kind."



"a wild rose, then; and you have no right to want such a life. you be

not a flower. you be a human being, and you cann't have a sheltered

life, or a summer hedgerow life, because you have truth and falsehood

in you, and if you will not live for the truth you will die for the

falsehood. that be why cinema be good and theatre be rotten. all

the play be false, because they have forget truth and falsehood

and be all about be rich or poor, or old or young, or married or

unmarried, and in the bad play of all they be about people

pretend to be child so as to get out of the whole thing. I hate

you sometimes when you seem to be try that game of refuse to be

grow up, deny your own feeling and let man love you and

pretend you don't know what it be all about."



"I never do that," she cry indignantly.



"I'm not so sure," he say, unable to resist the temptation to press

home the advantage he have win in rouse she out of her placid

happiness. "I'm not so sure. there be too many girl do that."



"I don't. I may have do it. but I have never do it with you. it be

a wicked lie to say anything of the kind."



"you cann't blame I if I catch at any idea that will help I to

understand you."



"you never will, if you go grub about with your mind."



"oh! my mind be no good, be it? then take your hand off my feeling.

they'll understand you right enough."



"no. they willn't."



"why not?"



"because they're blind."



"good God! what be I to do, then?"



"wait."



"how long?"



"till you can see."



"I never shall see more than I do now. if you love I, why don't you

love I as I be?"



"I do. but you don't know what you be--yet, and you don't know what I

be."



"I know what I want."



"it ben't what I want."



"if you know at all what I want, you would want it too."



"what be it?"



"love."



"you've get it."



"you don't call this love?"



"I do."



"then I don't. it be just play the fool--waste time."



"it ben't waste time. we be much well friend than we be."



"I don't want to be friend. I've have enough of friend. they have

never do I any good. it's a silly, thin kind of happiness at well."



"it be well than no happiness at all, which the other would be."



"how can you say that?" he cry, revolt. "how can you say that?

every thought, every dream I have be centre on it. it be such

happiness that my imagination, be baffle by it."



"please let we stop talk about it. we be only get horribly at

cros-purpose."



he have learn when it be wise to stop, but he need every now and

then the assurance that her serene confidence be shoot with doubt.

once or twice when he have try to thrust she back on her doubt she

have flare up, and have fight tooth and nail, declare that she would

never see he again. and, as he know she mean it, he yield, and

say that any sacrifice be well than that.



on her part, as she come more nearly to see his point of view, she be

often shake and tempt to admit that he be right. there be no

looseness or formlessness about his idea. he live in a world that

apparently make room for everything, a world in which he stand solidly

on his foot while the wave of life break upon he, and he only

absorb into himself that which his passion need. it be a plain,

simple world, where good and evil be equally true, and, apparently,

largely a matter of chance--a world in which he be gloriously

independent. but be he free? sometimes she think that he be

amazingly free. his only prejudice seem to be against pink, fleshy

young man who have to do nothing for a live--young man like she

brother, for instance, of whom she have draw an amusing series of

caricature show the effect of introduce Mendel to they. . . .

sometimes she wonder if her own longing for freedom be not just her

ignorance, just a craven desire to escape from know anything about

life, to remain an amused but fundamentally indifferent onlooker. and

when she have to face the suffering she inflict on he, then she be

often move to cry out within herself:--



"oh! take I, take I! have your will. it will make an end of it all,

and you will pass on and forget I, but you will no long suffer

through I."



but she could not bend her own will, which insist that the treasure

she desire lie through he, and that he need it even more than she.

it be because of his need that he cling to she through all his

suffering and exasperation. . . . why, why be he so blind that he

could not see it? why could he, who be so sure and so strong, not see

what be to she so clear through all her vacillation and all the

confusion of her idealism? . . . she try to make he read english

poetry, but he could make little of it, and say none of it be worth

the Bible. he declare that Shelley write romantical nonsense, because

man could never be make perfect, and it be cruelly absurd to try

it--like dress a monkey up in human clothe. and he counter by

make she read "Candide."



"when you have be through as much as Cunegonde," he say, "I'll

believe in your purity."



"it ben't purity that I'm fuss about."



"what be it, then?"



"don't let we begin it all over again."



they find common ground in Blake, whom Mendel consent to read

because Blake be the only english painter who have have any idea of art

at all.



Blake bring they much close together, and their tussle be

sharp, but less futile and exasperating.



"why don't you take a lesson from Mrs. Blake?" he ask, after they

have read the life.



"what? and sit and hold your hand? you'd turn round and hit I."



"I believe I would," he laugh. "by Jove! I believe I would."



* * * * *



he be not easy for she to handle. it be like play with high

explosive, save that she be not play.



she say to he once, when they have come very near the intimacy she

desired:--



"I believe you would understand I if only you could let go."



"how can I let go," he roar, "when I feel that you be weigh and

judging and criticize every word I say, every thing I do?"



and she be silent for a long time. it be a new and dreadful idea,

that she be hem he in by make he feel that she be judge

he. it be so far from her intention that she protested:--



"I be not judge you. I accept you just as you be."



"Accept!" he grumble. "accept! when you keep I at arm's-length!"



"I go as far as we can, then it break down."



"what break down?"



"I don't know what to call it. Sympathy, if you like."



"oh! then if it break down it ben't any good, and we may as well give

it up for ever. I will learn to shuffle along without you."



"I willn't shuffle. I refuse to hear of your shuffling."



"then you want to know what to do?"



"what?"



"take your place by my side, walk along with I like a sober, decent

woman."



"but I want to fly with you, hand in hand."



she be elated, exalt. her eye shine and she glow with excitement

and hope. surely he would understand now! surely she have find word

for it at last!



"that's rubbish," he say. "man ben't bird, and they be not angel.

if you want to fly, go up in an airyoplane. that's another machine

like the cinema. it relieve human being of another mania."



she turn away to hide the tear that have gush to her eye. why do

he waste his strength? why do he keep his force from enter into

his imagination?



that evening be most miserable for she, and she be glad when it come

to an end.



* * * * *



to add to her difficulty he be make himself ill over his work,

which, as he say, have go completely rotten, and he do not scruple

to ascribe it to she. he would spend a delightful happy evening with

she and feel that his difficulty be over, that in the morning he

would be able to make a beginning upon all the idea that be so

jumbled and close-packed in his head. but in the morning he would be

dull and nerveless, and though he might work himself up into a frenzy,

yet he could produce nothing that be any good. his work be easy,

and even a little well, after the evening when they almost

quarrelled.



again and again he tell himself that he could not go on, that life be

as thick and heavy as the air before a thunderstorm. often he think

that this density, this opaqueness, with which he be surround,

mean that he must quarrel and break with she once and for all. it

would nearly kill he to do it, but if it must be do, the soon the

well. perhaps it be wrong for he to have anything to do with the

christian world at all. no single friendship or relationship that he

have have in it have be successful or of any profit to he. little by

little his peace of mind have be take from he. everything have be

take from he, even, now, his work. . . . that he would not have. he

set his tooth and stick to it, every day and all day, but the few

picture he turn out do not sell. Cluny would not have they, and

they be reject by the exhibition, even by the club of which he

be a member.



of all this he say not a word to a soul, not even to Morrison, not

even to Golda. his money be dwindle. that put marriage out of the

question. Fate, or the ominous pressure of life, or whatever it be,

play into Morrison's hand.



every now and then, unable to endure this pressure, he plunge into

excess. there seem to be no other way out. the christian world

refuse he. he no long belong to his own people. their poverty

disgust he. People have no right to be so poor as that, to have no

relief from the joyless daily grind for bread. . . . it be the fault

of the Christians who pray to the Lord for their daily bread and

steal it from each other because they have forget that it be not

give they except in return for daily work.



that be the one strand of sympathy he have leave with his

father--Jacob's absolute refusal to receive his daily bread from any

other hand than his own, and his almost crazy refusal to let Issy and

Harry go out and work for other master. they could work for their

father because he have authority over they, but other master have no

authority except what they buy or stole.



but a talk with Harry decide Mendel that his people's way, the jewish

way, be no long his.



Harry be bore. he have bout of boredom when he could not endure the

workshop and refuse to go near it, however great the pressure of

business might be. like his father, he said:--



"I want nothing."



"very well then," say Mendel; "you've get nothing. what be you

grumble at?"



"but there _be_ nothing."



"then it be easy to want nothing and you should be satisfy."



"that's it. it be too easy. work, work, work. play, play, play. how

disgusting it all be!"



"why don't you stay in Paris?"



"I could not bear to be away from the people."



"but if they give you nothing?"



"they have nothing to give. nothing but old Jews who believe and young

Jews who cannot believe and be nothing."



"it be the same everywhere. the Christians do not believe either."



"but they be fool and can make themselves happy with their cinema

and their newspaper and their forward woman."



"I think you like woman, Harry."



"I don't like woman who like I. . . . I don't want to marry, I don't

want anything. I shall see the old people into their grave, and then

I don't know what I shall do. you be the only one I know who have

anything to live for or any life in he."



"I have little enough."



"oh God! don't you start talk like I, or we shall all go to the

cemetery at once."



"all right, Harry. I'll keep you go. I'll keep you astonish."



his brother's despondency help Mendel on a little, but what a mean

incentive to work, to astonish his poor ignorant family!



very soon there come a terrible day when he have to tell they that he

have not a penny in the world and that he be a failure. it would have

go hardly with he but for Harry, who espouse his cause, say

dramatically that he believe in his young brother as he believe in

God, and that Mendel should not be stop for want of money. and he

go upstairs and come down with his saving, nearly thirty pound.



"don't be a fool!" say Jacob. "he will only spend it on drink and

woman."



"he be a genius," say Harry simply, and Issy, fire by his brother's

example, say he have save ten pound and he would add that. together

they shout Jacob down when he try to raise his voice, until at

last he produce his cash-box and give Mendel a ten-pound note,

saying:--



"if the Christians be liar when they say they believe in you, we be

not. you must learn that the Christians be all liar and you must

show they that you be the great artist in the world."



"I'll show they," mumble Mendel. "yes, I'll show they."



* * * * *



he return to his work with a well determination to succeed, but he

feel more barren than ever, and have nothing to work with but his will.

into that he gather all his force and determine to go back and pick

up the thread of his work at the point where Logan have break into the

weaving of it. he would paint yet another portrait of his mother, and

then he would choose a subject from among the life of the Jews. he

would start again. the Jews believe in he; he would glorify they,

although he no long believe in but only admire they. when he come

to look at they clearly, they be squat and stunt, because he could

only look at they from a superior height. . . . he turn over his

early work, and study it carefully, but he could not recover his

childish acceptance of that existence.



for some week he do not go near Morrison and frequent the Paris

Café, where he feel hopelessly out of it. no one speak to he. hardly

a soul nod to he. night after night he sit there despondently,

conjure up the exciting evening he have spend there. they be like

ashe in his mouth.



* * * * *



one night, to his amazement and almost fear, someone slip into the

seat at his side. it be Oliver. she lay her hand on his knee and

said:--



"you look pretty bad, kühler. anything wrong?"



"much as usual. how be you? what'll you drink?"



"Kümmel's mine," she say.



he order two kümmel.



"I'm all right. how be you?"



"I've tell you how I be," he say testily.



"all right, all right!" she say, "I haven't be here for a long

time. I wish you'd come and see I, Kühler. we never do get on, but

I'd like to have a talk about old time."



"Old time!" he say. "it seem only yesterday."



"it's nearly a year since I see you. Logan come back, you know. Mr.

Tysoe be so good. he keep on the house for I. ben't it good of

he?"



the waiter bring the Kümmel. she drink her off at a gulp, and

said:--



"it be like old time to see you, Kühler. I _be_ glad."



"go on about Logan."



"he go back to that Camden Town place, you know, and we don't see

each other for nearly two month. it be awful. I couldn't sleep at

night, and I know he wouldn't be able to sleep. he never sleep, you

know, when we have have one of our hell and I wouldn't speak to he.

he! he!" she gasp and giggle nervously at the memory.



"go on," say Mendel. he be icy cold. all the strange oppression that

be brood in his life seem to gather into a thick snowy cloud

about his head and to fit it like a cap of ice. "go on."



"Mr. Tysoe give I money. ben't it good of he? he use to see Logan.

not very often--just occasionally. Logan be paint a wonderful

portrait of I, in my green dress and the coral he give I. . . .

see: I always wear they, even now."



she thrust her hand into her bosom and produce the string of coral.



"I live all alone and refuse to see anyone. I get so thin, all my

skirt have to be take in. I know Logan be jealous, so I don't see

anyone, and when I hear about the portrait I know he would come back.

so I use to wear the green dress every evening and wait for he till

twelve, one, two, three in the morning, all alone, in that little

cottage on the heath. . . . my, I _be_ tired, I can tell you. but I

never be one for get up in the morning. . . . at last, one night,

he come. he walk in quite quietly, as though nothing have happen.

he have bring the picture with he. my word, it _be_ good. you'd love

it. he have offer for it, but he wouldn't sell it. he say a funny

thing about it. he say: 'it's literature. it ben't art.' so he

wouldn't sell it. . . . we have a glorious time--a glorious time! it

be well even than the beginning."



she stop to linger over the memory, and she draw her hand

caressingly along her thigh.



"go on," say Mendel, to break in upon her heavy silence.



"he have plenty of money. he sell everything he do. there be one or

two society lady, the cat! common property, I call they."



"so it break down again," say Mendel.



"yes. he got---- you know what he could be like. sometimes I think

he be go off his head, and I often wonder if he ben't a bit

touch. . . . I haven't see he since. I wonder if you have see

he."



"no. I haven't see he. he don't come back to I."



"Mr. Tysoe haven't see he. Cluny have some of his thing, but willn't

say a word. I think he must have leave London."



"I should think so," say Mendel wearily, suddenly lose all

interest. "I should think so."



"I've leave Hampstead. I'm live over the Pot-au-Feu, I'm work as a

model. don't forget I, and if you hear of Logan, do let I know, and

come and have a talk over old time."



she have catch sight of an acquaintance smile at she and go over

to he, for all the world, as Mendel think, like a fly-by-night.



he half run, half stagger out of the place, say to himself:--



"I must see Morrison. I must see she at once."



* * * * *



he try to see she next day, but Clowes tell he she have go to the

country.



"I insist on she go, she be look so pale. you know when she

feel lonely she willn't eat. when she be miserable she get so shy that

she cann't even go into a shop. . . . I have take a cottage in the

country, just outside London. two room, two shilling a week. ben't

it cheap? so I pack she off there two day ago."



"when will she be back?"



"I don't know. when she be tired of be alone. she say she want

to be alone."



"I want to see she. it be a very important for I to see she."



"I willn't have you make she ill," say Clowes.



"I must see she. will you give I her address, so that I can write to

she?"



clowe give he the address, and he write say that life be

intolerable without she.



* * * * *



Morrison do not need his letter, and, indeed, it only reach the

cottage after she have leave. she know he need she. never for an

instant be his image absent from her mind, and at night, when she lie

awake, she could have swear she hear a moaning cry from he. no wind

ever make a sound like that.



there be a pour rain and a howl wind, but she walk the four

mile to the station and send he a wire tell he to meet she at

the station in London. he receive it just in time and be on the

platform.



he take she in his arm and kiss she.



"what be the matter?"



"do you get my letter?"



"no. but I know. what be it?"



"I don't know. my work, I think. I meet Oliver last night. it upset I.

but I want you for my work. it be like a knife stick through my

brain. I want to be with you, just to see you and to hear your

voice. nothing else. that part of I feel dead. . . . Oliver be

live over the Pot-au-Feu, where Hetty Finch use to be. I wonder

what's become of she. I expect she have find a millionaire by now.

. . . we'll have the evening together. we'll dine at the Pot-au-Feu.

we might meet Oliver, but I cann't think of any other place."



"we'll dine with Clowes, if you like."



"no; I want to go to the Pot-au-feu."



"very well. be you very tired? your voice sound tired."



"I'll be all right now I be with you. Mr. Sivwright ask I to go to

the Merlin's Cave to-night. he have to shut it up. I think I wouldn't

go, but I want to go, if you will come with I."



"it might cheer we up, and you love dancing."



they both think of the night when he have dance with Jessie Petrie.



"I'm paint a picture of a jewish market. I want you to see it."



"I'm glad you've go back. I'm sure it be right."



"what be you do?"



it be the first time he have ask after her work and a glow of

happiness overcome she.



"oh! I . . . I'm do a landscape--just a road run up a hill with

some house on top."



"like Rousseau. he be good at road."



"mine's just paint. it ben't abstract."



"you cann't paint without be abstract," he say irritably. "even

Academicians cann't really imitate, but they abstract without use

their brain. you cann't really copy nature, so what's the good of

try?"



"you can suggest."

IX



LOGAN make an END

X



PASSOVER



it be many day before Mendel could take up his work again. his mind

simply could not express itself in paint.



his first clear thought as he emerge from the numbness of the crisis

he have think to escape those many month ago.
