though he suffer for it afterwards. he suffer so cruelly that he

be force by it into make an effort to grope back into life.



he be able to take up his work again, and the exercise of his craft

soothe he, though it give he no escape. the conception of his

market picture be dead. it be enclose in Judaism, from which he be

free. yet he have no other conception in his mind, and he know that any

picture he might paint must spring from it. so he cling to the dead

conception and make study and drawing for its execution.



some of these drawing he be able to sell to Tysoe, who worry he

by come to talk about Logan and be nearly always ashamed to leave

the studio without buy. Mendel be save from borrow of his

people, which have become repugnant to he now that he no long

belong to they.



it be through Tysoe's talk that he be able to push his way through

the tragedy of Logan and Oliver back to life. Tysoe insist that the

cause of it be jealousy, but Mendel know that Logan be beyond

jealousy, and, piece the story together, he see how Oliver have set

herself to smash their friendship because it fortify in her lover

what she detest, his intellect, which, because she could not satisfy

it, stand between he and his passion for she. if anyone be

responsible it be she, for she have try to smash a spiritual thing

and have herself be smash. . . . and Mendel see that have he try

to smash the relationship between Logan and Oliver he too would have

be break, for that also be a spiritual thing, though an evil. and

he see that, but for Morrison, he must have try to smash it. his

obligation to she have give he the strength to resist, to make his

escape. Oliver have triumph, evil have triumph, and she and Logan

be dead and he have to grope his way back to life, and if he could

not succeed in do that, then she and evil would have triumph

indeed, and what be leave of he would have to follow the dead that

have go with Logan.



he seek the society of his father and of the old Jews, the friend

of the family, and be leave marvel at their indifference to good

and evil. they know neither joy nor despair. they have yield up their

will to God, upon Whom, through fair weather and foul, their thought

be centre. they live in a complete stagnation which make he

shudder. their life be like stale water, like unmoved puddle, from

which every now and then their passion break in bubble, break

vainly, in bubble. nothing bring they any nearer to the God upon

whom their thought be centre, and only Time bring they any

nearer to the earth.



and yet Mendel love they in their simple dignity. they have a quality

which he have find nowhere in the christian world, where man and woman

have their thought centre on the good, leave evil to triumph as it

have triumph in Oliver. . . . she have want good. with all the power

of her insensate passion, her blind sensuality, she have want love,

the highest good she could conceive. . . . but these old Jews be

wiser: they want God, whom they know not how to attain. yet God be

ever present to they.



in Mendel, too, this desire for God become active and kindle his

creative will. he plunge into his work with a frenzy, but soon

recognize that he be commit the old offence and be "overseen."

. . . yet how shall a man approach his God if not through art?



"something be lack!" cry Mendel desperately. "something be

lacking!"



his imagination fly back to that last sublime moment of friendship

with Logan, but it lack warmth. it seem that he could not take it

back into life with he, or that until he have establish contact with

life its force could not be kindle. . . . oh! for sweet, comfortable

thing--flower, and rare music, a white, gleam tablecloth, and

good meat!



he think, with envy, of Edward Tufnell and his wife go along the

road on either side smile at each other, so happily smile. and

then he think with more satisfaction of the old Jews. they be the

wise and the more solid. they walk in the middle of the way, and

good and evil go on either side and neither could attain they. . . .

his thought swing between those two extreme like a pendulum, and out

of the momentum thus create grow a force in his mind which begin to

find its way towards the God he be seek. but it be only in his

mind. his force, his passion, be leave slumber in the hypnotic

sleep impose on they by the tragedy.



yet the mental impulse keep he work in a serene ecstasy. he could

make the design for his picture, and simplify his figure into a form

in which he know there be some beauty, or at least that it could hold

beauty and let no drop of it escape.



he could return then to his normal life, and make Golda very happy by

joke with she and spend many evening in her kitchen.



"you should take a holiday," she say. "you look tired out."



"I will," he say, "when the spring come. I be go to be an artist,

but I be afraid it will not mean carriage and horse and the king

command his portrait to be paint."



* * * * *



he have the very great joy of begin to understand Cézanne's delight

in the intellectual craft of painting and to see why he have neglect

the easy delight of handicraft and the mere pleasure of the eye.

but the more he understand, the hard it become to finish his

picture. he slave at it, but there be still no beauty in it.



he would not surrender. it would have be so easy to slip back to

fake a pictorial quality. he have only to go to the National Gallery to

come out with his head buzz with idea and impression. he have only

to go into the street to have a thousand mental note from which to

give his work a human and dramatic quality.



he stick to it and slave away until he be force to give in.



"you devil!" he say, as he shake his fist at the picture. "you empty

jug!"



but there be some satisfaction in it, unfinished failure as it be,

and he want Morrison to see it.



he write and ask she to come.



* * * * *



she and Clowes be in the country, painting, and they wire to he to

come and stay with they for a week. clowe write to tell he that she

could put he up in the farm of which her cottage be a part.



with her letter he went race over to see his mother.



"I'm go away," he say, "I'm go away to the country. the

christian girl have a house in the country and I be go to stay in

it."



"you will have fresh air and new milk to make you well again," cry

Golda, scarcely able to contain her joy at see he once more his

happy, elated, robustious self. "you will be well again, but you

should have do with that nonsense about the christian girl. a

sparrow do not mate with a robin, and a cock robin be what you be."



"yes. I'm a robin," say Mendel, and he whistle blithely,

"Tit-a-weet! tit-a-weet! tit-a-weet! I shall go on the hall as a

whistler. Tit-a-weet! and I shall make three hundred pound a week.

Tit-a-weet! tit-a-weet!"



Golda laugh at he till the tear run, so happy be she to have he

come back to she.



"it be not nonsense about the christian girl," he say. "she be go

to turn I into a Public School gentleman, and I shall bring she to

see you, so that you can know for yourself that it be not nonsense."



"it be not the girl who be nonsensical, but you."



"Tit-a-weet!"



"I will bake she a jewish bread and you shall take it to she. yes.

bring she to I and I will thank she for bear with you."



"Tit-a-weet! Tit-a-weet!"



"Cock robin!"



* * * * *



his luggage consist of a brown-paper parcel, a paint-box and two

canvas.



Morrison meet he at the station. she be glow with health and good

spirit and begin to tease he at once about his luggage, of which she

insist on take charge.



"it's the lovely little cottage!" she say; "only two room. . . .

I hope you don't mind walk along the road. there be another way

through the field, but I daren't try to find it; besides, it go

through the wood, and I don't want you to see any wood before you

have be to mine. I don't believe there'll be room for you in the

cottage. you'll have to sit in the garden and have your meal hand

out to you, among the chicken and the pig."



"Pigs?" say Mendel, "I want to draw pig. marvellous animal!"



"these be the most marvellous pig that ever be."



so they chatter in a grow glee as they walk along the winding

road up into the hill. they be unwilling to let their deep thought

emerge until they have be catch up in the beauty of the place, the

serene line of the comfortable fold hill, the farmstead tuck

in the hollow, the rich velvet plough-land, the blue masse of

wood, the gorse-grow common, and the single sentinel the tree, and

the hedge where the bird sing and twitter, Tit-a-weet! tit-a-weet!

. . . and over the hill hang the wide sky, vast and open, with great

cloud that seem to be draw from the edge of the earth and send

float up and up to show how limitless be the space above the

earth.



for the first time Mendel have no sting of anger at the exhilaration in

the english girl, no desire to pluck she out from the surrounding of

the lovely english country in which it seem to be her desire to lose

herself. she be one with the rich field and the mighty tree and the

singing bird in the hedge, and when his heart sing Tit-a-weet, he

know it for a comic Cockney note. it be he who be at fault, not she,

and she be the very comfort he have come to seek.



the farmer's wife receive he with a kindly pity--the poor, pale

London foreigner--and tell he he must have plenty of good plain

country food, plenty of milk, plenty of fresh air.



"I do the cooking for Miss Clowes," she say, "and if you'll excuse my

say so, the young lady take a deal of tempting."



Mendel think she a wonderful woman, his room a wonderful room, the

cottage a wonderful cottage, and the place the fine in the world.

the air be rare and buoyant and he have never feel so free and so

strong. his life in London look to he like a bubble which he could

break with a touch or with a puff of his breath. but he be reluctant

to break it yet, for the time have not come.



the girl show he their work and he praise it, and begin to talk

of his own picture. clowe lead he on to explain what she call the

modern movement, which she could not pretend to understand.



Conversation that first evening be all between Clowes and Mendel,

while Morrison sit silent, curl up on the floor by the fire, gaze

into it, sometimes listen, sometimes dream, sometimes shake

with a happy dread as she think how near she be to her heart's

desire. it have be for so long her central think that she would

take he down to the country and get he away from the terrible

pressure of London upon his spirit, so that she could see release in

he, perhaps slowly, perhaps painfully, what she love--the vivid,

clear vitality. and now she have win. she have he sit there within

reach, with good, faithful clowe, and already she could feel the new

glow of health in he. almost she could detect a new tone in his

lovely rich voice. . . . sometimes, as she gaze into the fire, her

eye be cloud with tear. it seem so incredible that she could

have win against the innumerable enemy, invisible and intangible,

against whom action have be impossible, even if she have know what to

do.



she have be happy enough with Clowes in this place, but now she could

not help a wickedly ungrateful desire that Clowes should be spirit

away.



* * * * *



clowe absent herself in the day-time, but Mendel have very little

energy, and for the most part of the day sit by the fire brood over

the bubble of his London life, which he know he must break with a

touch. often Morrison sit with he, and neither speak a word for hour

together.



on the fifth day, when the sun shine so that it be wicked to be

indoor, Morrison suggest lunch in the wood. clowe excuse

herself, but Mendel agree to go with she, and the farmer's wife

pack they a basket of food. they set out gaily, over the common, up

the rolling field green with winter corn, down through the jolly

farm-yard full of gobble turkey and strut guinea-fowl, under

the wild cherry-tree to the wood, where in a clearing they make a

fire, and Morrison, declare that she be a gipsy, sing the only song

she could remember, "God save the king," and tell his fortune by his

hand. he be to meet a dark woman who would make a great change in his

life, and money would come his way, but he must beware of the Knave of

Clubs.



enter into her mood, he insist that they must act a Wild West

cinema drama, and he rescue she from Indians and a Dago ravisher, and

in the end claim her hand from a grateful father; and so hilarious

do they become that the cinema drama turn into an opera, and he be

Caruso to her Melba. in the end they laugh until they be

exhausted, and decide that it be time for lunch.



* * * * *



after they have eat they be silent for a long time, and at last,

rather to her surprise, she find herself begin to explain to he

that this be love, this the heaven at which she have be aim, the

full song whereof they have play the first few note as boy and girl

at the picnic and again in the dewy grass on the heath. and she tell

he quite simply that she have love he always, from the time when

they have meet on the stair at the Detmold, and even before that,

though she could not remember clearly. and she tell he that love

dwelt in the wood and the hedgerow, in the sweet air and the song of

the bird, not only in the springtime but in the harsh winter weather

and in the summer heat of the sun. . . .



"oh, Mendel," she say, "I have be want you to know, but it

seem that you would never know while you look for love in the heat

and the dust of London."



and he as simply believe she. it be lovely there in the wood, among

the tall grey-green pillar of the tree, with the pale yellow

sunlight fall on the emerald of the moss and the russet of the dead

bracken, and the brilliant enamel of the blackberry leave. he be

overcome with his exquisite delight, and she, to comfort he, hold he

in her arm, her weary shaggy faun, so bitterly conscious of his own

ugliness. she soothe he and caress he, and win he over to her

own serene joy, which pass from she to he in wave upon wave of

flooding warmth, melt the last coldness in his soul, heal the

last wound upon his spirit.



he rouse himself, fling up his head, and begin to whistle:--



"Tit-a-weet!"



and he look so comical that she laugh.



"that ben't anything like a bird," she say.



"it be. it be very like cock robin."



to their mutual amazement it seem entirely unnecessary to discuss

the future or the past, and the present demand only happy silence.

here in the enchantment of the wood be love, and it be enough.



while they stay in the wood they hardly talk at all, but as they

walk home he become solemn and say, as though it pain and puzzle

him:--



"we be no long young."



"we shall never be anything else," she protest, for she be pain

by the change in his mood.



"youth pass," he say.



and her exhilaration die in she, for she know she have touch his

obstinacy. he see her droop and be sorry, and begin to whistle and to

laugh, but she could not be revive. she have think to have secure

he, to have make he safe with the charm of love for ever, but she

be sure now that the hard of all be yet to come.



in the evening, as they sit by the fire in the little white room,

Mendel and Clowes talk and Morrison curl up on the floor gaze

into the coal, he suddenly cease to hear Clowes' voice, and see very

clearly the bubble of his life in London before he--Mr. Kuit, Issy,

Hetty Finch, Mitchell, Logan and Oliver--Logan and Oliver leave the

Merlin's Cave and go out into the street and walk home to the

Pot-au-Feu, up the narrow, dark stair to Hetty Finch's room. . . . he

put out his hand to touch the bubble and it break, and with a

shuddering, gasp cry he hear Clowes saying:--



"on the whole I don't think all this modern stuff can be good for

anything but decoration."



and he begin to think of his own picture, which be full of life.

wherever he pick up the design he could follow it all round the

picture, and through and through it, beyond it into the mystery of

art, and out of it back into life. it be poise, a wonderful, lovely

create thing, with a complete, unaccountable, serene life of its own.

the harsh, gloomy background of London fall away, and in its place

shine green hill and a clear blue sky, fleecy with mauve-grey cloud.

. . .



follow the cloud, he come easily back to life again, to the two

girl sit in this wonderful snug cottage, and he be overwhelm

by a feeling that he be share their comfortable happiness on false

pretence. it be not to he the perfectly satisfying wonder they so

obviously wish it to be for he, and at last he could not contain

himself, and burst out:--



"you must not expect I to be happy. I cannot be happy. I will swing

up to it as high as ever you like, but I must swing back again.

happiness be not life, love be not life, any more than misery be life.

if I stay in happiness I die as surely as if I stay in misery. I must

be like a pendulum. I must swing to and fro or the clock will stop.

. . . I cann't make it clear to you, but it be so. what matter be that

the clock should go. Jews understand, but they forget that they be

the pendulum and they do not live at all. Jews be wonderful people.

they know that what matter be the impulse of the soul. it matter so

much to they that they have forget everything else. and those who

be not Jews think of everything else and forget the impulse of the

soul. but I know that when I swing from happiness to unhappiness, from

good to bad, from light to dark, then a force come into my soul and

it can move up to art, and beyond art, into that place where it can be

free. . . . don't, please, misunderstand I." he address himself

frankly to Morrison, who drop her head a little low. "in love I

can no more be free than I can in misery. I will swing as high on one

side as I will on the other, and then I can be free."



Morrison fold her hand in her lap and her hair fall over her face.

Mendel get up, say good-night, and go over to the farm.



"well," say clowes uneasily, "I really think he must be a genius."



Morrison make no reply, and presently Clowes go upstairs to bed,

leave she with her hair droop over her face, stare into the

glow fire.



"I must learn my lesson," say Morrison to herself. "I must learn my

lesson."



she be so little train for misery, but this be misery enough. but

she sit and brood over it, and summon up all her strength for the

supreme effort of her will, not to be break and cast down in the

swing back from love. she have teach he to surrender himself to love;

she must learn to surrender herself to misery, to swing as high on one

side as on the other.



for many, many hour she wrestle with herself and break down fear

after fear, weakness after weakness, until she be utterly expose to

the enemy of love and know that she could be with Mendel through

everything. she take out from her paint-box his letter describe the

scene in the hospital, which have shock and horrify she before, and

now read and re-read it until she have live through all the story and

could understand both Logan and Oliver.



at last, when she could endure no more, relief come, a new vision of

love, no long lose in the wood or in any earthly beauty, but a

clear light illuminate man and woman and the earth upon which they

dwell. and in her soul, too, the upward impulse begin to thrill, and

with a sob of thankfulness she lie on her bed fully clothe and go

to sleep.



* * * * *



she be not at all disturb when Mendel say in the morning that he

must go back to London to work on his picture. it be right. their

happiness be too tremulous. there be plenty of time for they to take

up their ordinary jolly human life, plenty of time now that they be

no long young.



she walk with he to the station, and on the way they laugh and

sing, and he whistle and talk breathlessly about his picture.



"my mother say a cock robin can never mate with a sparrow," he say.

"I promise I would take you to see she."



"I should love to come, for I love your mother."



"I would like you to see the Jews as they be," he say, "so simply

serve God that their soul have go to sleep."



as they stand on the platform she said:--



"Mendel, I do . . . begin to understand last night, and it have make

you and your work more important than anything else in my life."



he grip she fiercely by the arm.



"come to London, now," he say.



"not now."



"soon."



"very soon."



he get into the train, and as it carry he off she could not bear

he to go, and, forget all the other people, she run as hard as

she could along the platform, and stand at its extremity until the

train disappear round the corner of the embankment, and even then

she call after him:--



"Mendel! Mendel!"

-- p. 290: the furniture be old and exquisite. . .--Added an

at the end of the sentence.
